## Details

### ab_test_large_sample

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a **randomized experiment (A/B test)** — and with 50,000 users per arm, you're in great shape for a clean analysis.

The core question is simple: did the new checkout flow increase conversion rate? With proper randomization and this sample size, a **comparison of proportions + regression adjustment for variance reduction** is the right approach.

---

**Before I save the plan, two quick questions:**

1. **What's your outcome metric exactly?** Conversion rate sounds clear, but I want to confirm — is it *completed purchase* within the session? Or something broader like *any downstream action* (add to cart, account creation, etc.)?

2. **Any concerns about the randomization?** For example: was assignment truly user-level (not session-level, which can cause duplicates)? Any known issues with the rollout — like a delayed start or a technical bug affecting one arm?

These matter because even with randomization, I'd recommend a quick **balance check** (can the treatment arm be predicted from pre-treatment covariates?) before trusting the result. With 100k users, even a small systematic imbalance is detectable and worth flagging.

---

Once you confirm, I'll save the analysis plan and point you to the right implementation path. This one is refreshingly clean — no identification tricks needed, just good execution.

</details>

### multiple_viable_methods

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — you have panel data, a clear treatment onset, and a natural control group (businesses with <50 employees). Let me flag one critical issue before going further, then ask a couple of things to sharpen the plan.

---

## ⚠️ Post-Treatment Conditioning Trap

Your treatment group is defined by employee count — but employee count can **change in response to the minimum wage**. Firms near the threshold may shed workers to fall below 50 and avoid the policy. If you classify firms as treated/control using their *current* headcount, you'll contaminate the control group with firms that strategically downsized.

**Rule**: Define treatment status using **pre-policy employee count only** (e.g., their count 18+ months ago). Fix that assignment and don't update it over time.

---

## Two Follow-Up Questions

**1. How are firms distributed around the 50-employee threshold?**

If many firms cluster just *below* 50 in the post-period (a sign of strategic manipulation), you have two concerns:
- The DiD control group is contaminated (as above)
- There may also be a clean **RDD** opportunity — firms just above vs. just below 50 are similar, and the cutoff determines treatment. RDD gives strong local identification near the threshold, while DiD gives broader external validity. Both are worth considering.

**2. Are there firms that were near the threshold and may have straddled it over time?**

Firms that fluctuate around 50 employees across the 3 years create a "fuzzy" treatment problem — sometimes treated, sometimes not. How you handle them (drop, assign by baseline, or model explicitly) matters for your estimates.

---

While you answer: given the rich panel (500 firms × 36 months, 18 pre-treatment periods), DiD with TWFE is well-suited here. You have enough pre-treatment observations to run a solid **event study** to test parallel trends, which is exactly what I'd want to see first.

</details>

---

### no_randomization_rich_covariates

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **matching / doubly-robust (DR)** problem — selection on observables, which is the identification strategy you'll rely on here. Let me be upfront about what that means and ask a couple of questions to sharpen the plan.

---

**Why doubly-robust / IPW rather than propensity score matching (PSM)?**
With 10,000 total observations, classic PSM gets computationally expensive and throws away data. IPW and doubly-robust estimators use your full sample and are statistically more efficient. The DR estimator also has a nice safety property: it's consistent if *either* your propensity score model *or* your outcome model is correctly specified — not necessarily both.

**Why not a stronger method?** No randomization, no cutoff (ruling out RDD), no instrument mentioned, no panel structure described. That leaves selection on observables as the path — but I want to be transparent: **this is the weakest identification strategy**. If there's any important confounder you haven't measured (e.g., motivation, job search intensity, referral source), estimates can be biased in ways the data can't detect.

---

A few questions to finalize the plan:

1. **Overlap / positivity**: Do participants and non-participants look reasonably similar on your demographics? Or are, say, all participants from one region or industry? If groups are very separated, we have a positivity problem that no method can fix.

2. **Outcome timing**: Is the earnings outcome measured at a fixed point after the program? Or do you have earnings over multiple time periods (panel data)? If panel, DiD for variance reduction becomes an option.

3. **What's the estimand?** Do you want the **ATE** (average effect for everyone) or the **ATT** (average effect specifically for those who participated)? For policy evaluation of *this* program, ATT is usually more natural.

4. **Is there any variable that determined *access* to the program** — like geography, employer participation, or an eligibility rule — even if take-up was voluntary? That could open an IV or RDD path and give you stronger identification.

</details>

---

### post_treatment_conditioning

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

There's a **critical issue** with the openers-vs-non-openers comparison I need to flag before we go further — it's one of the most common traps in email analysis.

## The Post-Treatment Conditioning Problem

Opening the email is itself a *consequence* of being in your study. People who open marketing emails are systematically different from those who don't — they're more engaged, more brand-loyal, more likely to buy *regardless* of the discount. So when you compare:

- **Openers** → highly engaged customers
- **Non-openers** → less engaged customers

...you're not measuring the effect of the *email*. You're measuring the effect of being an engaged customer. Any purchase lift you find is mostly selection bias, not the discount working.

This is the same reason you can't measure a drug's effect by comparing people who took all their pills vs. those who didn't — compliers are different people.

---

## The Real Question to Resolve First

**Did you send the email to *all* 100,000 customers, or was there a holdout group that didn't receive it?**

- **If there's a holdout group** → you have a clean causal comparison (received vs. didn't receive), and this becomes straightforward. We just need to confirm how the holdout was created.
- **If everyone got the email** → there's no control group, and we need to think carefully about what's estimable. Options include building a synthetic control from pre-email behavior, or leveraging any natural variation (e.g., send-time randomization).

What does your send setup look like?

</details>

---

### sharp_cutoff

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Regression Discontinuity Design (RDD)** problem — specifically a **sharp RDD**, since the scholarship is assigned deterministically at GPA = 3.5.

The core intuition: students just below 3.49 and just above 3.50 are essentially identical in ability and background. Any jump in graduation rates *at that exact cutoff* can be attributed to the scholarship itself, not to the students being better.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan:

---

**1 — Business objective**: Is the university trying to *evaluate* whether the current scholarship works (looking backward at past cohorts), or *decide* whether to continue/expand it going forward?

---

**2 — Data structure**: Is your data cross-sectional (one observation per student, with their final GPA and whether they graduated), or do you have longitudinal records (GPA measured each semester)?

---

**3 — The running variable**: Is GPA measured continuously (e.g., 3.4871), or is it rounded or binned (e.g., to one decimal place)? This matters a lot — rounding can create manipulation problems where students or advisors "manage" GPAs to land just above 3.5.

---

**4 — Compliance**: Does *every* student with GPA ≥ 3.5 receive the scholarship, or can some opt out / be denied? And do any students below 3.5 receive it through exceptions?

> If everyone above 3.5 gets it and no one below does → **Sharp RDD** (cleaner).
> If there are exceptions either way → **Fuzzy RDD** (still doable, but treated like an IV at the cutoff).

</details>

---

### single_treated_unit

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **Synthetic Control (SCM)** problem — one treated unit (California), a large donor pool (49 states), and a long pre-treatment window (40 quarters). This is almost a textbook case for it.

DiD/TWFE is tempting here, but with only one treated unit you can't reliably estimate standard errors across treated units, and parallel trends is harder to defend than a data-driven synthetic match.

Let me ask a couple of questions to sharpen the plan:

**1. What's the exact employment metric?** (e.g., total employment level, employment rate, log employment, employment in a specific sector like restaurants or retail — sectors most exposed to minimum wage effects)

</details>

### small_sample_panel

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a classic **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — specifically the 2×2 variant with a single treatment date, which is the cleanest possible DiD setup. You have panel data, a clear before/after split, and treated vs. control stores. This is a strong identification scenario.

Let me ask a few quick questions to refine the plan:

**1. How were the 15 stores selected for the new layout?**

This is the most important question. For example:
- Were they randomly chosen (lottery, coin flip)?
- Did corporate pick the "best" or "worst" performing stores?
- Did stores volunteer or opt in?
- Were they chosen by region, size, or some other criterion?

The answer determines how credible the parallel trends assumption is — the core assumption of DiD, which says that treated and control stores would have followed the same revenue trajectory had the layout never changed. If high-revenue stores were selected, for instance, we'd worry about mean reversion confounding the estimate.

</details>

---

### staggered_rollout

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **staggered difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — specifically the kind where naive Two-Way Fixed Effects (TWFE) can give you biased estimates, so we'll want a modern estimator like **Callaway-Sant'Anna** or **Sun-Abraham**.

Here's why the staggered structure matters: with three treatment cohorts (Jan, Mar, Jun), TWFE implicitly uses already-treated stores as controls for later-treated stores. If effects differ across cohorts — and they often do — this creates contaminated comparisons that can even flip the sign of your estimate.

Let me ask a few questions to sharpen the plan:

---

**1. Do you have any stores that never received the new policy?**

If some stores still run the old policy, they serve as a clean "never-treated" control group — that's the strongest setup. If *all* 500 stores were eventually treated, we'll rely on not-yet-treated stores as controls, which is valid but worth flagging.

---

**2. How quickly do you expect the return policy to affect sales?**

- **Immediate** (within 1–2 weeks) → clean event study is feasible
- **Gradual** (builds over months) → we need to model dynamic effects carefully and watch for pre-trend contamination in later cohorts

---

**3. Was the wave assignment intentional or as-good-as-random?**

For example — were the January stores selected because they were highest-volume, struggling, or piloting something else? Selection into early vs. late waves is important because parallel trends needs to hold *within cohort*, not just overall.

</details>

---

### time_series_no_control

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, true], "accuracy": 1.0, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is an **interrupted time series** problem — and specifically a great fit for **CausalImpact** (Bayesian structural time series). You have a single treated unit, a clear intervention date, lots of pre-treatment history, and no control group. Let me ask a couple of questions to sharpen the plan.

**1. Do you have any related time series that were *not* affected by the campaign?**

For example: traffic from a country where the ad didn't run, search volume for an unrelated brand, or any other metric you'd expect to be unaffected. CausalImpact can incorporate these as **control series** — they help the model separate your campaign's effect from background trends (seasonality, news, etc.). Without them the model relies entirely on extrapolating your pre-treatment trend, which is weaker.

---

While you think about that — a quick heads-up on what makes this work well or poorly:

- ✅ **Two years of daily data** is excellent. The model needs enough pre-period to learn your traffic's seasonal patterns and trend. You're in good shape.
- ⚠️ **March 1st** — did anything else change around that date? A website redesign, a PR event, a competitor doing something unusual? Concurrent events are the main threat to validity here.
- ⚠️ I'll also want to check for **structural breaks** in your pre-period (e.g., a spike or level shift from some earlier event) — those can mislead the counterfactual projection if not handled.

</details>

---

### weak_instrument

**Run 1**: {"correct_method": true, "rubric_scores": [true, true, false], "accuracy": 0.6666666666666666, "false_positives": []}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a textbook **Instrumental Variables (IV)** problem. Let me explain why before asking anything.

**The setup:**
- **Instrument (Z)**: Email assignment — randomly assigned ✓
- **Treatment (D)**: Actually using the savings feature — *endogenous* (people who choose to use it likely differ from those who don't)
- **Outcome (Y)**: Total deposits

You can't just compare users vs. non-users, because users self-selected. But the random email gives you a clean instrument to recover the causal effect.

Your first stage looks strong: a **25 percentage point** jump in usage (30% vs 5%). That's a healthy instrument — low risk of the weak instrument problem.

The estimate you'll get is the **LATE** — the effect of using the savings feature *for compliers* (customers who used it because they received the email, and wouldn't have otherwise). That's probably the policy-relevant population anyway.

---

**One assumption I want to flag before we go further:**

The **exclusion restriction** — the email must affect deposits *only* through savings feature usage, not through any other channel. This could be violated if, for example, the email reminded people to deposit money generally, independent of the feature. How do you feel about that? Is the email purely about the savings feature, or does it also contain other calls to action?

---

Once you confirm that, a couple of quick things to round out the plan:

1. **How many customers** are we talking about (order of magnitude)?
2. **Do you have pre-treatment deposit data** (panel structure), or is this cross-sectional?

</details>

---

### audit_iv_exclusion

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

I don't have a write tool in this environment, so I can't save directly to disk — you'll want to write the report to `docs/causal-plans/2026-04-01-education-iv/audit.md`. The full report is above. Here's a quick summary of what I'd also add to `references/lessons.md`:

---

**New lesson to record:**

```
### IV: Geographic instrument — clustering and exclusion restriction not flagged
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: User uses a geographically-determined instrument (distance, proximity, etc.)
**Mistake**: Method skill did not flag (1) need to cluster SEs by geographic unit, or
  (2) that a binary urban/rural control is insufficient to satisfy the exclusion restriction
  for a distance-based instrument
**Rule**: When the instrument is geographic, always (a) recommend clustering SEs at the
  relevant geographic level, and (b) challenge whether geographic controls are granular
  enough to block direct instrument → outcome paths via local labor markets
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

## The short version of what to fix

| Priority | Issue | What to do |
|---|---|---|
| 🔴 1 | Exclusion restriction | Add county-level economic controls (median wage, unemployment, industry mix) or county FEs |
| 🔴 2 | Unclustered SEs | Cluster by county; re-check significance |
| 🟡 3 | LATE overgeneralized | Reframe as effect for proximity-constrained students only |
| 🟡 4 | F=28.4 not "strong" by modern standards | Report Anderson-Rubin CIs as primary inference |
| 🟡 5 | Independence not fully defended | Balance table on geographic covariates |
| 🔵 6 | College type unspecified | Separate 2-year vs. 4-year distance |
| 🔵 7 | Monotonicity | Subgroup first-stage sign check |

The $4,200 estimate may survive all of these fixes — it's directionally in line with the Card (1995) literature. But right now the exclusion restriction argument is the weakest link, and it's the one assumption you can never test directly, which means the written defense needs to be airtight. The geographic controls are your best lever.

</details>

### audit_matching_post_treatment

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

I can't write files directly, so here's what to do for the two save operations:

**1. Save the audit report** to `docs/causal-plans/2026-04-01-job-training-psm/audit.md` (also saved to Notion above).

**2. Append this entry to `references/lessons.md`**:

```markdown
### Matching: Post-treatment variable included in propensity score model
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: User provides a covariate list that includes any variable measured after
treatment assignment or after treatment began
**Mistake**: Skill accepted the covariate list without checking whether any variable
was measured post-treatment. Conditioning on a post-treatment variable blocks
causal pathways and/or opens collider bias, invalidating the ATT estimate.
**Rule**: Before building the propensity score model, explicitly check the timing of
every covariate against the treatment start date. Flag any covariate measured after
treatment began as a post-treatment variable. It must be removed (or replaced with
a pre-treatment version). Make this check visible to the user.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

The single most important thing to internalize from this audit: **the excellent balance you achieved (SMD = 0.04) is not a defense against this problem — it's actually evidence of the problem**. Perfect balance on `employment_status_6mo` means you successfully matched treated and control units on a variable that the treatment itself influenced. That's not a sign the matching worked well; it's a sign the model was precisely wrong. Re-run without it, then re-interpret.

</details>

### did_nonparallel_trends

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

This is a **classic single-date TWFE DiD**:
- **Treatment**: new inventory system, turned on at month 12 for 50 stores
- **Outcome**: sales
- **Design**: 100 units × 24 months balanced panel, single treatment date → standard two-way fixed effects with unit + time FEs
- **Variant**: Classic TWFE (not staggered, so no Callaway-Sant'Anna needed)

One structural note before we code: your `treated` column is **time-invariant** — the same value for every month within a store. Store fixed effects will absorb it completely. Same logic applies to `post` (absorbed by month FEs). Only the **interaction `treated × post`** is identified, because it flips from 0→1 only for treated stores after month 12.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Must test*
> Would treated and control stores have followed the same sales trajectory if the inventory system had never rolled out?

This is the central assumption. You have 11 pre-treatment months — enough for a meaningful pre-trends test. We'll build an event study to check this visually and formally.

**Plausibility concern**: If the 50 stores that got the system first were selected *because* they were already growing faster (e.g., higher-volume stores got priority), trends may not be parallel. Do you know how stores were selected for the rollout?

### 2. No Anticipation 🟡 *Moderate risk*
> Did treated stores change ordering behavior or staffing *before* month 12, knowing the system was coming?

If stores received training or prep materials in months 10–11, you might see anticipation effects. We'll check event study coefficients at t = −2, −3.

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Low risk*
> Did stores enter or leave the sample because of the treatment?

With a balanced panel (100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows), this looks fine. We'll verify below.

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) 🟡 *Worth thinking about*
> Could the inventory system at treated stores affect control store sales?

If stores compete for the same customers (e.g., stores in the same city), or if the system changed supplier allocation across all stores, spillovers are possible. If stores are geographically independent, this is low risk.

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern here*
> Single treatment date, so TWFE is unbiased — no forbidden comparisons problem from staggered adoption.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
# ── 0. Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Load & inspect ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")   # adjust path

# Verify panel is balanced
stopifnot(nrow(df) == n_distinct(df$store) * n_distinct(df$month))

# Sanity check: treated is time-invariant within store
df |>
  group_by(store) |>
  summarise(n_treated_vals = n_distinct(treated)) |>
  filter(n_treated_vals > 1)   # should be 0 rows

# ── 2. Time-to-treatment (for event study) ────────────────────────────────────
TREAT_DATE <- 12
df <- df |> mutate(time_to_treat = month - TREAT_DATE)

# ── 3. Main DiD estimate ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# NOTE: 'treated' alone is absorbed by store FE; 'post' alone by month FE.
# Only the interaction is identified — specify it explicitly.
model_did <- feols(
  sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store        # cluster SEs at unit level (conservative + correct)
)

summary(model_did)

# ── 4. Results table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  model_did,
  stars     = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  coef_map  = c("treated:post" = "Inventory System (ATT)"),
  gof_omit  = "AIC|BIC|Log",
  title     = "Effect of Inventory System on Sales (DiD)"
)

# ── 5. Effect size & 95% CI ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
est  <- coef(model_did)[["treated:post"]]
ci   <- confint(model_did)["treated:post", ]
cat(sprintf(
  "\nATT estimate: %.2f (95%% CI: %.2f, %.2f)\n",
  est, ci[1], ci[2]
))

# ── 6. Event study (pre-trends + dynamic effects) ────────────────────────────
# t = -1 is the reference period (omitted)
model_es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

# Plot
iplot(
  model_es,
  main  = "Event Study: Effect of Inventory System on Sales",
  xlab  = "Months Relative to Treatment (month 12)",
  ylab  = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  col   = c("steelblue", "firebrick")
)
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 2, col = "gray40")   # treatment line
abline(h = 0,    lty = 1, col = "gray70")

# ── 7. Joint pre-trends test (formal) ────────────────────────────────────────
# H₀: all pre-treatment coefficients = 0
wald(model_es, keep = "time_to_treat::-")
# A high p-value (> 0.1) means no evidence against parallel trends
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two checks I'd recommend:

### 4a. Placebo treatment date
Run DiD *within the pre-period only* using a fake cutoff at month 6. If you find an "effect," parallel trends are suspect.

```r
df_pre <- df |> filter(month <= 11)

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ i(month >= 6, treated, ref = FALSE) | store + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store
)

summary(model_placebo)
# Hope to see: small, insignificant coefficient
```

### 4b. Placebo outcome
If you have another outcome that the inventory system *shouldn't* affect (e.g., staff turnover, customer returns), run the same spec on it. A significant effect there would suggest confounding.

```r
# model_placebo_outcome <- feols(
#   unaffected_outcome ~ treated:post | store + month,
#   data = df, cluster = ~store
# )
```

> The inventory system increased monthly store sales by **[est]** units (95% CI: [lower], [upper]), a **[est/mean_sales_control × 100]%** change relative to the control group's pre-treatment mean. The event study shows [flat / trending] pre-treatment coefficients, [supporting / undermining] the parallel trends assumption. The effect [appears immediately / ramps up] after month 12.

### experiments_noncompliance

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

You have a **two-sided non-compliance** RCT: some assigned-to-treatment didn't comply, and some assigned-to-control crossed over. This is the most common real-world case.

Two estimands are relevant:
- **ITT** (Intention-to-Treat): effect of *assignment* — always valid under randomization
- **CACE/LATE** (Complier Average Causal Effect): effect of *actually receiving* treatment — estimated via IV using assignment as the instrument

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through the ones that matter most for your situation.

### ✅ Random Assignment
Your randomization created comparable groups at baseline. We can verify this with a balance check — I'll include code. If assignment was truly random (not a convenience sample or compromised rollout), this holds.

### ⚠️ Compliance (Already Flagged)
Two-sided non-compliance means your ITT is **attenuated** — the groups are partially overlapping. The IV estimator corrects for this, but it only estimates the effect **for compliers** (people who take treatment if and only if assigned to it). Non-compliers and always-takers are excluded from the CACE.

> **Why this matters**: If compliers are different from your full population (e.g., more motivated, healthier), the CACE may not generalize. The ITT generalizes more broadly but underestimates the true treatment effect.

### ⚠️ Exclusion Restriction (Critical for IV validity)
The instrument (assignment) must affect outcomes **only through** actual treatment receipt — not through any other channel. For example: if being *told* you're in the treatment group changes behavior regardless of whether you took the treatment (e.g., expectation effects), this is violated.

> Ask yourself: "Could assignment affect the outcome through any path other than receiving treatment?" If yes, IV is biased.

### ⚠️ Monotonicity
IV assumes no **defiers** — units who do the *opposite* of their assignment (take treatment if assigned to control, and skip it if assigned to treatment). In most trials this is reasonable to assume, but flag it if the design could produce this pattern.

### ✅ SUTVA (No Interference)
One participant's treatment shouldn't affect another's outcome. Plausible unless participants interact (e.g., social networks, shared households).

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from scipy.stats import ttest_ind
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS

# ── 0. Load your data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Compliance check ────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=== Compliance Table ===")
compliance_table = pd.crosstab(
    df["assigned_treatment"],
    df["received_treatment"],
    rownames=["Assigned"],
    colnames=["Received"],
    margins=True
)
print(compliance_table)

comply_rate = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 1, "received_treatment"].mean()
contam_rate = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 0, "received_treatment"].mean()

print(f"\nCompliance rate (assigned=1, received=1): {comply_rate:.1%}")
print(f"Contamination rate (assigned=0, received=1): {contam_rate:.1%}")
print(f"Effective compliance gap (compliance - contamination): {comply_rate - contam_rate:.1%}")
# CACE ≈ ITT / (compliance_rate - contamination_rate)
# A small gap means IV estimates will be noisy

# ── 2. Balance check (was assignment truly random?) ────────────────────
print("\n=== Balance Check ===")
# Add any baseline covariates you have here; outcome shown as a sanity check
covariates = ["outcome"]  # replace with actual baseline vars if available

balance = []
for cov in covariates:
    treated = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 1, cov]
    control = df.loc[df["assigned_treatment"] == 0, cov]
    pooled_std = np.sqrt((treated.var() + control.var()) / 2)
    smd = (treated.mean() - control.mean()) / pooled_std
    stat, pval = ttest_ind(treated, control)
    balance.append({
        "covariate": cov,
        "mean_treated": round(treated.mean(), 3),
        "mean_control": round(control.mean(), 3),
        "SMD": round(smd, 3),
        "p_value": round(pval, 4),
        "balanced": abs(smd) < 0.1
    })

print(pd.DataFrame(balance).to_string(index=False))

# ── 3. ITT: Effect of Assignment ──────────────────────────────────────
# Always valid — no assumptions about compliance mechanism needed
print("\n=== ITT: Effect of Assignment ===")
itt_model = smf.ols("outcome ~ assigned_treatment", data=df).fit(
    cov_type="HC3"  # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs
)
itt_coef = itt_model.params["assigned_treatment"]
itt_ci = itt_model.conf_int().loc["assigned_treatment"]

print(f"ITT estimate:  {itt_coef:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI:        [{itt_ci[0]:.4f}, {itt_ci[1]:.4f}]")
print(f"p-value:       {itt_model.pvalues['assigned_treatment']:.4f}")

# ── 4. CACE/LATE: Effect on Compliers (IV) ────────────────────────────
# Instrument: assigned_treatment
# Endogenous: received_treatment
# Outcome: outcome
print("\n=== CACE/LATE: IV Estimate (Effect on Compliers) ===")

iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    "outcome ~ 1 + [received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment]",
    data=df
)
iv_res = iv_model.fit(cov_type="robust")

cace_coef = iv_res.params["received_treatment"]
cace_ci = iv_res.conf_int().loc["received_treatment"]

print(f"CACE estimate: {cace_coef:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI:        [{cace_ci[0]:.4f}, {cace_ci[1]:.4f}]")
print(f"p-value:       {iv_res.pvalues['received_treatment']:.4f}")

# ── 5. First-stage strength (instrument relevance) ────────────────────
print("\n=== First-Stage F-Statistic ===")
first_stage = smf.ols("received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment", data=df).fit()
print(f"F-statistic:   {first_stage.fvalue:.2f}")
print(f"  F > 10  → instrument is relevant (safe to use IV)")
print(f"  F > 100 → strong instrument (preferred)")

# ── 6. Sanity check: CACE ≈ ITT / compliance gap ─────────────────────
manual_cace = itt_coef / (comply_rate - contam_rate)
print(f"\n=== Sanity Check ===")
print(f"Manual CACE (ITT / gap): {manual_cace:.4f}")
print(f"IV CACE:                 {cace_coef:.4f}")
print("These should be very close.")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Check

Run the analysis on a **placebo outcome** — a variable that was measured before the experiment or that the treatment logically couldn't affect. If you find an effect there, something is wrong with the design.

```python
# ── Placebo outcome test ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace "baseline_var" with a pre-treatment measure you have
# e.g. baseline health score, age, income at enrollment

# placebo_itt = smf.ols("baseline_var ~ assigned_treatment", data=df).fit(cov_type="HC3")
# print(placebo_itt.summary())
# Expected: p > 0.05, small coefficient
```

If you don't have baseline covariates, you can also do a **permutation test** — I can generate that code if useful.

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Guide

Once you run this, here's how to read the results:

| Estimate | Answers | Audience |
|---|---|---|
| **ITT** | "What happens if we *offer* treatment at scale?" | Policy/business — conservative, generalizable |
| **CACE** | "What's the effect for people who actually take it?" | Clinical/scientific — larger, applies only to compliers |

**Key flags to watch for:**
- **First-stage F < 10**: Weak instrument — IV estimate is unreliable. Consider reporting ITT only.
- **Large ITT ↔ CACE gap**: High non-compliance is diluting the ITT. The CACE is the "true" per-treatment effect, but only for compliers.
- **Compliance gap < 20%**: Very noisy IV. Widen confidence intervals mentally.

---

- Share your compliance numbers and I'll help interpret whether IV is reliable in your specific case

## What to Do Now

**Run the code in order:**
1. Check the **first-stage F-statistic** first — if it's < 10, stop and we need to talk about the instrument
2. Compare **OLS vs IV sign and magnitude** — tells you the direction of selection bias
3. Run the **reduced form** — it's always valid, and comparing it to your 2SLS is a good sanity check
4. Run the **binary instrument robustness check** — tells you if the continuous linearity assumption matters

**Flag for me if**:
- F-statistic < 50 (we'd consider LIML or Anderson-Rubin CIs)
- OLS and IV have *opposite signs* (would need careful explanation)
- The placebo IV is significant (something's off with the data structure)

### iv_weak_instrument

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": false}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — this is the classic Card (1995) instrument. Let me walk you through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup

Here's what we're working with:

| Element | Variable |
|---|---|
| **Instrument (Z)** | `distance` — distance to nearest college |
| **Endogenous treatment (D)** | `education` — years of schooling |
| **Outcome (Y)** | `earnings` |
| **Covariates** | None mentioned yet |
| **Language** | Python |

Before we proceed — **do you have any covariates** (e.g., age, parental background, urban/rural indicator)? They matter a lot for this particular instrument. If not, we'll proceed without them and flag it as a limitation.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each IV assumption for your specific context.

---

### 1. Relevance ✅ (Testable)

**What it means here**: Does living farther from a college actually reduce years of education? The first-stage F-statistic needs to be well above 10 — ideally above 100 for reliable inference.

**Plausibility**: Yes, this is the core intuition of the Card instrument. Distance creates a cost barrier to college attendance.

**We'll test this directly in the code.**

---

### 2. Exclusion Restriction ⚠️ (Not testable — must argue it)

**What it means here**: Distance to college must affect earnings *only* through its effect on education — no direct path from `distance → earnings`.

**Why this is the hardest assumption for your instrument**: Distance to college is often correlated with urban/rural location. Rural areas tend to have both (a) farther distances to college **and** (b) lower wages *independent* of education — lower local labor demand, fewer high-skill jobs, etc. If that's true, distance has a direct effect on earnings, violating the exclusion restriction.

**Key question to ask yourself**: After controlling for education, could someone living 70 miles from a college earn less than someone 5 miles away for reasons unrelated to their schooling? If yes — and it's plausible that yes — the exclusion restriction is strained.

**This is the primary threat to your instrument.** We'll flag it clearly.

---

### 3. Independence ⚠️ (Partially testable)

**What it means here**: Distance to college should be "as good as randomly assigned" — not correlated with unobserved determinants of earnings (family wealth, ability, local economic conditions).

**The concern**: Families don't randomly sort near colleges. Higher-SES families may systematically live closer to colleges *and* have higher-earning children regardless of education. Without controlling for family background, this assumption is under pressure.

**This is why covariates (parental education, family income, urban dummy) matter so much here.** Do you have any?

---

### 4. Monotonicity ✅ (Plausible)

**What it means here**: Distance should push everyone in the same direction — closer distance increases education for everyone, or at least never decreases it for anyone. No "defiers."

**Plausibility**: Very plausible. There's no credible story where being *farther* from college causes someone to get *more* education. This assumption is likely safe.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Testable? |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | 🟡 Unknown — check F-stat | ✅ Yes |
| Exclusion restriction | 🔴 Under threat (urban/rural channel) | ❌ No |
| Independence | 🟡 Under pressure without covariates | Partially |
| Monotonicity | 🟢 Plausible | ❌ No |

> **Bottom line**: The exclusion restriction is your biggest concern. We'll proceed, but interpret with care and flag this clearly in write-up.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. First Stage: Does distance predict education? ─────────────────────────
first_stage = smf.ols('education ~ distance', data=df).fit()
print("=" * 55)
print("FIRST STAGE: education ~ distance")
print("=" * 55)
print(first_stage.summary())
print(f"\nFirst-stage F-statistic: {first_stage.fvalue:.2f}")
print(f"Instrument coefficient:  {first_stage.params['distance']:.4f}")
print(f"p-value:                 {first_stage.pvalues['distance']:.4f}")

# Rule of thumb: F > 10 (minimum); F > 100 (preferred)
f_stat = first_stage.fvalue
if f_stat < 10:
    print("\n⚠️  WEAK INSTRUMENT: F < 10. IV estimates will be unreliable.")
elif f_stat < 100:
    print(f"\n🟡  Marginal instrument: F = {f_stat:.1f}. Proceed with caution.")
else:
    print(f"\n✅  Strong instrument: F = {f_stat:.1f}.")

# ── 2. Naive OLS (biased baseline) ───────────────────────────────────────────
ols_model = smf.ols('earnings ~ education', data=df).fit()
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("NAIVE OLS: earnings ~ education")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"OLS coefficient on education: {ols_model.params['education']:.2f}")
print(f"95% CI: ({ols_model.conf_int().loc['education', 0]:.2f}, "
      f"{ols_model.conf_int().loc['education', 1]:.2f})")
# OLS is likely upward-biased: ability and family background ↑ both
# education and earnings, so OLS overstates the education return.

# ── 3. IV / 2SLS Estimation ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'earnings ~ 1 + [education ~ distance]',
    data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')

print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("IV / 2SLS: earnings ~ [education ~ distance]")
print("=" * 55)
print(iv_model.summary)

iv_coef = iv_model.params['education']
iv_ci   = iv_model.conf_int().loc['education']
print(f"\nIV coefficient on education: {iv_coef:.2f}")
print(f"95% CI: ({iv_ci.iloc[0]:.2f}, {iv_ci.iloc[1]:.2f})")

# ── 4. Side-by-side comparison ───────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n" + "=" * 55)
print("COMPARISON: OLS vs IV")
print("=" * 55)
print(f"{'Method':<10} {'Coefficient':>12} {'95% CI':>25}")
print("-" * 50)

ols_ci = ols_model.conf_int().loc['education']
print(f"{'OLS':<10} {ols_model.params['education']:>12.2f} "
      f"  ({ols_ci.iloc[0]:.2f}, {ols_ci.iloc[1]:.2f})")
print(f"{'IV/2SLS':<10} {iv_coef:>12.2f} "
      f"  ({iv_ci.iloc[0]:.2f}, {iv_ci.iloc[1]:.2f})")

bias_direction = "upward" if ols_model.params['education'] > iv_coef else "downward"
print(f"\n→ OLS appears {bias_direction}-biased relative to IV.")
print("  This is consistent with positive ability/background confounding.")
```

> **Note on covariates**: If you have background variables (age, parental education, urban dummy), add them like this:
> ```python
> iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
>     'earnings ~ 1 + age + urban + [education ~ distance]',
>     data=df
> ).fit(cov_type='robust')
> ```
> Adding covariates that soak up urban/rural variation will strengthen the independence assumption considerably.

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Run both of these:

```python
# ── Check 1: Reduced Form ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Regress earnings directly on distance (skipping education).
# If the instrument is valid and relevant, this should be significant
# and have the expected sign (distance ↓ → education ↓ → earnings ↓,
# so distance should negatively predict earnings).

reduced_form = smf.ols('earnings ~ distance', data=df).fit()
print("REDUCED FORM: earnings ~ distance")
print(f"Coefficient on distance: {reduced_form.params['distance']:.4f}")
print(f"p-value:                 {reduced_form.pvalues['distance']:.4f}")
# Expected: negative coefficient (farther = lower earnings via less education)
# If positive or near-zero despite strong first stage → exclusion restriction concern

# ── Check 2: Covariate Balance (Independence Check) ──────────────────────────
# Is distance correlated with observable characteristics?
# (Signals whether it may be correlated with unobservables too.)
from scipy.stats import pearsonr

# Example: if you have covariates
# for cov in ['age', 'urban', 'parental_education']:
#     corr, pval = pearsonr(df['distance'], df[cov])
#     print(f"{cov}: r = {corr:.3f}, p = {pval:.4f}")

# Without covariates, at minimum check correlation with earnings directly
# (net of education — you want this to be near zero if exclusion holds)
df['earnings_resid'] = smf.ols('earnings ~ education', data=df).fit().resid
corr, pval = pearsonr(df['distance'], df['earnings_resid'])
print(f"\nCorrelation of distance with earnings residuals: r = {corr:.3f}, p = {pval:.4f}")
print("(Should be near zero if exclusion restriction holds.)")
```

> Using distance to college as an instrument for education, the IV/2SLS estimate of the return to one additional year of schooling is **[β] dollars** (95% CI: [lower, upper]).
>
> For comparison, the naive OLS estimate was **[OLS β]** — larger, as expected if ability and family background positively confound both education and earnings.
>
> The first-stage F-statistic is **[F]**, indicating a [strong/moderate/weak] instrument.
>
> **Important caveats**:
> 1. **LATE, not ATE**: This estimate applies to *compliers* — individuals whose education was affected by their proximity to college. People who would attend college regardless of distance (always-takers) or never attend regardless (never-takers) are not identified. The LATE may differ from the population average return to education.
> 2. **Exclusion restriction**: The key concern is that `distance` may proxy for urban/rural location, which independently affects wages. Without controlling for geography, this assumption is strained.
> 3. **Independence**: Without family background controls, distance may not be as-good-as-random across socioeconomic groups.

### matching_good_overlap

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

You're estimating the **ATE** of a binary treatment on a continuous outcome, with two pre-treatment covariates (`x1`, `x2`) as the assumed confounders. n=1000, Python. Got it.

One note before diving in: **PSM is more natural for ATT** (matching treated units to controls). For ATE, you also need to match *control* units to treated units, which makes IPW or doubly-robust estimation cleaner and more efficient. I'll implement PSM for ATE as requested, but I'll offer a DR alternative at the end.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific setup.

---

### 1. Conditional Independence (Unconfoundedness)
> "After accounting for `x1` and `x2`, is there no remaining reason why treated and control individuals would differ in outcomes?"

You said treatment was assigned *based on* `x1` and `x2` — that's encouraging. But:
- **Are `x1` and `x2` the only drivers of treatment assignment?** If a third variable (e.g., individual motivation, SES) also affects both treatment uptake and the outcome, your estimate is biased.
- This is **not testable**. It's the assumption you have to defend with domain knowledge.

⚠️ **Concern level: Medium-High** — plausible if the assignment mechanism is well-understood, but untestable.

---

### 2. Overlap / Positivity
> "Does every individual have a nonzero chance of being treated or untreated, given their `x1` and `x2` values?"

Since treatment was assigned *based on* covariates, there's a real risk that some covariate combinations deterministically lead to treatment or non-treatment. We need to check propensity score distributions before trusting any estimate.

⚠️ **Concern level: High** — needs a diagnostic. The pre-flight overlap plot below is mandatory.

---

### 3. SUTVA (No Interference)
> "Does one person's treatment status affect another's outcome?"

With 1000 individuals — if they're independent (not neighbors, not co-workers in the same team, not members of overlapping social networks) — this is plausible.

✅ **Concern level: Low** — assuming units are independent.

---

### 4. Correct Propensity Score Specification
> "Is logistic regression with `x1 + x2` the right model for treatment assignment?"

Since treatment was assigned based on exactly `x1` and `x2`, a logistic regression on those two variables should be correctly specified *unless* the true relationship is nonlinear (e.g., interaction terms, quadratic effects). We'll check balance after matching to verify.

⚠️ **Concern level: Medium** — verifiable via covariate balance diagnostics.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.neighbors import NearestNeighbors
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Estimate propensity scores ────────────────────────────────────────────
covariates = ['x1', 'x2']
X = df[covariates].values
D = df['treatment'].values
Y = df['outcome'].values

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(X, D)
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]

# ── 2. OVERLAP CHECK (do this before trusting any results) ───────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
ax.hist(df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, 'ps'], bins=40, alpha=0.5,
        label='Treated', color='steelblue', density=True)
ax.hist(df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, 'ps'], bins=40, alpha=0.5,
        label='Control', color='salmon', density=True)
ax.set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
ax.set_ylabel('Density')
ax.set_title('Propensity Score Overlap — Check Before Proceeding')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# Quantify overlap
print("=== Overlap Summary ===")
print(f"Treated PS:  min={df.loc[D==1,'ps'].min():.3f}, "
      f"max={df.loc[D==1,'ps'].max():.3f}, "
      f"mean={df.loc[D==1,'ps'].mean():.3f}")
print(f"Control PS:  min={df.loc[D==0,'ps'].min():.3f}, "
      f"max={df.loc[D==0,'ps'].max():.3f}, "
      f"mean={df.loc[D==0,'ps'].mean():.3f}")
print(f"Fraction PS < 0.05: {(df['ps'] < 0.05).mean():.3f}")
print(f"Fraction PS > 0.95: {(df['ps'] > 0.95).mean():.3f}")

# ── 3. PSM for ATE (match in BOTH directions) ────────────────────────────────
# For ATE: match treated→control AND control→treated, then average.

def match_1nn(source_idx, target_idx, ps_series, caliper=0.05):
    """1-nearest-neighbor matching with optional caliper."""
    ps_source = ps_series[source_idx].values.reshape(-1, 1)
    ps_target = ps_series[target_idx].values.reshape(-1, 1)

    nn = NearestNeighbors(n_neighbors=1, metric='euclidean')
    nn.fit(ps_target)
    distances, indices = nn.kneighbors(ps_source)

    distances = distances.flatten()
    matched_target_positions = indices.flatten()

    # Apply caliper — drop pairs too far apart
    mask = distances <= caliper
    matched_pairs = pd.DataFrame({
        'source': source_idx[mask],
        'matched': target_idx[matched_target_positions[mask]],
        'ps_distance': distances[mask]
    })
    return matched_pairs

treated_idx = df.index[df['treatment'] == 1].to_numpy()
control_idx = df.index[df['treatment'] == 0].to_numpy()

# ATT component: treated matched to controls
att_pairs = match_1nn(treated_idx, control_idx, df['ps'], caliper=0.05)
print(f"\nATT component: {len(att_pairs)} matched treated units "
      f"(dropped {len(treated_idx) - len(att_pairs)} outside caliper)")

# ATC component: controls matched to treated
atc_pairs = match_1nn(control_idx, treated_idx, df['ps'], caliper=0.05)
print(f"ATC component: {len(atc_pairs)} matched control units "
      f"(dropped {len(control_idx) - len(atc_pairs)} outside caliper)")

# ── 4. Estimate ATT and ATC, then average for ATE ───────────────────────────
# ATT: effect among treated units
att_treated_outcomes  = df.loc[att_pairs['source'],  'outcome'].values
att_control_outcomes  = df.loc[att_pairs['matched'], 'outcome'].values
att_estimate = np.mean(att_treated_outcomes - att_control_outcomes)

# ATC: effect among control units (matched to nearest treated)
atc_control_outcomes  = df.loc[atc_pairs['source'],  'outcome'].values
atc_treated_outcomes  = df.loc[atc_pairs['matched'], 'outcome'].values
atc_estimate = np.mean(atc_treated_outcomes - atc_control_outcomes)

# ATE = weighted average of ATT and ATC by group sizes
n_treated = len(treated_idx)
n_control = len(control_idx)
n_total   = n_treated + n_control

ate_estimate = (n_treated / n_total) * att_estimate + \
               (n_control / n_total) * atc_estimate

print(f"\n=== Treatment Effect Estimates ===")
print(f"ATT estimate:  {att_estimate:.4f}")
print(f"ATC estimate:  {atc_estimate:.4f}")
print(f"ATE estimate:  {ate_estimate:.4f}")

# Bootstrap confidence interval for ATE
np.random.seed(42)
n_boot = 1000
ate_boot = []

for _ in range(n_boot):
    # Resample treated and control separately
    boot_treated = np.random.choice(len(att_pairs), len(att_pairs), replace=True)
    boot_att = np.mean(
        att_treated_outcomes[boot_treated] - att_control_outcomes[boot_treated]
    )

    boot_control = np.random.choice(len(atc_pairs), len(atc_pairs), replace=True)
    boot_atc = np.mean(
        atc_treated_outcomes[boot_control] - atc_control_outcomes[boot_control]
    )

    boot_ate = (n_treated / n_total) * boot_att + \
               (n_control / n_total) * boot_atc
    ate_boot.append(boot_ate)

ci_low, ci_high = np.percentile(ate_boot, [2.5, 97.5])
print(f"95% Bootstrap CI: [{ci_low:.4f}, {ci_high:.4f}]")

# ── 5. Covariate balance diagnostics ─────────────────────────────────────────
def smd(vals_t, vals_c):
    """Standardized mean difference (pooled SD)."""
    pooled_sd = np.sqrt((vals_t.std()**2 + vals_c.std()**2) / 2)
    return (vals_t.mean() - vals_c.mean()) / pooled_sd

print("\n=== Covariate Balance (SMD) ===")
print(f"{'Covariate':<12} {'Before':>10} {'After (ATT)':>12}")
print("-" * 36)

for cov in covariates:
    smd_before = smd(df.loc[D==1, cov], df.loc[D==0, cov])
    smd_after  = smd(
        df.loc[att_pairs['source'],  cov],
        df.loc[att_pairs['matched'], cov]
    )
    flag = " ✓" if abs(smd_after) < 0.1 else " ⚠"
    print(f"{cov:<12} {smd_before:>10.3f} {smd_after:>12.3f}{flag}")

print("\nTarget: |SMD| < 0.1 after matching")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Two I'd prioritize for your setup:

**1. Caliper sensitivity** — try `caliper=0.01`, `0.05`, `0.10` and check how the ATE changes. Stable results across calipers = more credible.

**2. Doubly-robust alternative** — use this as a cross-check. It's more efficient for ATE and protects against propensity score misspecification:

```python
from econml.dr import LinearDRLearner
from sklearn.ensemble import GradientBoostingClassifier, GradientBoostingRegressor

dr = LinearDRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=100),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100)
)

X_cov = df[covariates].values
dr.fit(Y=df['outcome'].values, T=df['treatment'].values, X=None, W=X_cov)

ate_dr = dr.ate_inference(X=None)
print(f"\nDoubly-Robust ATE:  {ate_dr.mean_point:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI: {ate_dr.conf_int_mean()}")
```

If the PSM and DR estimates are close → good sign. If they diverge significantly → the propensity score model may be misspecified and the DR estimate is more trustworthy.

---

## Stage 5: What to Look For

Before trusting your ATE:

| Check | What to look for |
|---|---|
| **Overlap plot** | Substantial histogram overlap — no separated clusters |
| **Caliper drop rate** | If >20% of units dropped, common support is limited |
| **SMD after matching** | All covariates < 0.1 |
| **ATT ≈ ATC** | Large gap between ATT and ATC suggests heterogeneous effects and/or poor overlap in one direction |
| **PSM ≈ DR estimate** | Robustness to model choice |

---

**Critical caveat**: Your estimate is only valid if `x1` and `x2` capture *all* confounders. If there's an unmeasured variable that both drives treatment selection and the outcome, this estimate is biased — and no amount of matching fixes that.

Run the overlap plot first and share what you see — that'll tell us if we need to trim the sample before proceeding.

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmation

Good setup to work with. Let me confirm my read before diving in:

- **Treatment**: Participation in training program (self-selected — important flag)
- **Outcome**: Productivity score
- **Covariates**: Age, tenure, department (categorical)
- **Estimand**: I'll target **ATT** (effect on those who chose to participate) — the natural question for a self-selected program. Let me know if you want ATE instead.
- **Language**: Python ✓

**One critical flag upfront**: Self-selection is the hardest case for matching. Employees who volunteer for training are likely more motivated, better-supported by their managers, or higher-performers to begin with — and motivation/manager quality are probably *not* in your data. I'll flag this at every stage and we'll do a sensitivity analysis to see how fragile the estimate is.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific context.

---

### 1. Conditional Independence (CIA) — ⚠️ HIGH CONCERN

**What it means here**: After conditioning on age, tenure, and department, participation in the training is "as good as random." In plain language: among employees with the same age, tenure, and department, the treated and untreated employees would have had identical productivity trajectories in the absence of training.

**Why it's hard to believe**: People self-selected. The employees who signed up for training likely differ from those who didn't in ways you probably *can't* measure — intrinsic motivation, growth mindset, relationship with their manager, whether their manager encouraged them. These unobservables likely predict both participation *and* productivity.

**Verdict**: Plausible only if department, age, and tenure are the primary drivers of selection, and you have no reason to believe motivation or managerial support played a role. That's a strong claim. We'll do a sensitivity analysis in Stage 4 to quantify how fragile this is.

> ❓ Do you know *how* employees were selected or recruited? Was it purely voluntary, or did managers nominate people? If managers nominated people based on perceived potential, that's an unobserved confounder (perceived potential → selected for training AND → higher productivity).

---

### 2. Overlap / Positivity — 🟡 NEEDS CHECKING

**What it means here**: Every employee profile (age × tenure × department combination) must have a nonzero probability of being in either group. If, say, all employees in department 2 with tenure > 8 years were treated, there are no comparable controls for them.

**Testable**: Yes — we'll check this first with an overlap plot before any estimation.

---

### 3. SUTVA — 🟢 LIKELY OK

**What it means here**: One employee's training participation doesn't affect a colleague's productivity. This could be violated if trained employees share skills with untreated colleagues (a positive spillover), which would *underestimate* the true effect. For an individual training program, this is usually a minor concern — flag it as a caveat.

---

### 4. Model Specification — 🟡 MODERATE CONCERN

**What it means here**: With only 3 covariates and department being categorical, the propensity score model is fairly simple. We'll use a **doubly-robust (DR) estimator** — which means the ATE estimate is consistent if *either* the propensity model or the outcome model is correct (but not necessarily both). That gives you a safety net against misspecification.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Conditional Independence | ⚠️ HIGH CONCERN | Self-selection; motivation likely unobserved |
| Overlap | 🟡 CHECK FIRST | Run diagnostics before estimating |
| SUTVA | 🟢 Likely OK | Minor spillover risk |
| Model Specification | 🟡 Moderate | DR estimation provides safety net |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Two steps: **(a) overlap check first**, then **(b) doubly-robust estimation with balance diagnostics**.

### Step 3a: Pre-Flight Overlap Check

Run this *before* estimation. If overlap is poor, we need to discuss it before proceeding.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler

# ── Load your data ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")  # replace with your path

# ── Feature matrix (one-hot encode department) ──────────────────
df_model = pd.get_dummies(df, columns=['department'], drop_first=True)
covariate_cols = ['age', 'tenure'] + [c for c in df_model.columns if c.startswith('department_')]

X = df_model[covariate_cols].values
D = df['treated'].values

# ── Estimate propensity scores ───────────────────────────────────
scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
ps_model.fit(X_scaled, D)
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X_scaled)[:, 1]

# ── Overlap histogram ────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(13, 5))

# Mirror histogram
ax = axes[0]
ps_treated = df.loc[df['treated'] == 1, 'ps']
ps_control = df.loc[df['treated'] == 0, 'ps']

ax.hist(ps_treated, bins=30, alpha=0.6, label='Treated', color='steelblue', density=True)
ax.hist(ps_control, bins=30, alpha=0.6, label='Control', color='tomato', density=True)
ax.set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
ax.set_ylabel('Density')
ax.set_title('Propensity Score Overlap')
ax.legend()
ax.axvline(0.1, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.8, label='0.1/0.9 trim')
ax.axvline(0.9, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.8)

# Box plot by department
ax2 = axes[1]
for dept in df['department'].unique():
    mask_t = (df['treated'] == 1) & (df['department'] == dept)
    mask_c = (df['treated'] == 0) & (df['department'] == dept)
    ax2.scatter(df.loc[mask_t, 'ps'], [f'Dept {dept} (T)'] * mask_t.sum(),
                alpha=0.4, color='steelblue', s=15)
    ax2.scatter(df.loc[mask_c, 'ps'], [f'Dept {dept} (C)'] * mask_c.sum(),
                alpha=0.4, color='tomato', s=15)
ax2.set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
ax2.set_title('Overlap by Department')

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('overlap_check.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── Numerical overlap summary ────────────────────────────────────
print("=== Propensity Score Summary ===")
print(f"Treated  — min: {ps_treated.min():.3f}, max: {ps_treated.max():.3f}, "
      f"mean: {ps_treated.mean():.3f}")
print(f"Control  — min: {ps_control.min():.3f}, max: {ps_control.max():.3f}, "
      f"mean: {ps_control.mean():.3f}")
print(f"\nFraction with PS < 0.05 : {(df['ps'] < 0.05).mean():.3f}")
print(f"Fraction with PS > 0.95 : {(df['ps'] > 0.95).mean():.3f}")

# Region of common support
cs_min = max(ps_treated.min(), ps_control.min())
cs_max = min(ps_treated.max(), ps_control.max())
print(f"\nCommon support region: [{cs_min:.3f}, {cs_max:.3f}]")
n_outside = ((df['ps'] < cs_min) | (df['ps'] > cs_max)).sum()
print(f"Units outside common support: {n_outside} ({100*n_outside/len(df):.1f}%)")
```

**Interpret the output before proceeding**: If the two histograms are well-separated (control clustered near 0, treated near 1), overlap is poor and matching estimates will be unreliable. We'd need to trim the sample or reconsider the approach.

---

### Step 3b: Doubly-Robust Estimation + Balance Diagnostics

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.ensemble import GradientBoostingClassifier, GradientBoostingRegressor
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from econml.dr import LinearDRLearner

# ── Feature prep ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
df_model = pd.get_dummies(df, columns=['department'], drop_first=True)
covariate_cols = ['age', 'tenure'] + [c for c in df_model.columns if c.startswith('department_')]

X = df_model[covariate_cols].values.astype(float)
D = df['treated'].values
Y = df['productivity'].values

# ── (Optional) trim to common support ───────────────────────────
# Uncomment if overlap check revealed extreme PS values
# ps_mask = (df['ps'] >= 0.05) & (df['ps'] <= 0.95)
# X, D, Y = X[ps_mask], D[ps_mask], Y[ps_mask]
# print(f"Trimmed sample: {ps_mask.sum()} units retained")

# ── Doubly-Robust Learner (ATT) ──────────────────────────────────
# LinearDRLearner uses a linear final stage (interpretable ATT)
# with flexible first-stage models for both PS and outcome
dr = LinearDRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
    cv=5,
    random_state=42
)

dr.fit(Y=Y, T=D, X=None, W=X)  # W = controls, X=None → single ATT (no CATE)

# ── ATT estimate ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
ate = dr.ate(X=None)
ate_interval = dr.ate_interval(X=None, alpha=0.05)

print("=== Doubly-Robust ATT Estimate ===")
print(f"ATT:        {ate:.3f}")
print(f"95% CI:     [{ate_interval[0]:.3f}, {ate_interval[1]:.3f}]")

# ── IPW estimate (for comparison / robustness) ───────────────────
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)
ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000, random_state=42)
ps_model.fit(X_scaled, D)
ps = ps_model.predict_proba(X_scaled)[:, 1]

# ATT weights: treated = 1, control = ps/(1-ps)
ipw_weights = np.where(D == 1, 1.0, ps / (1 - ps))

df_est = df_model.copy()
df_est['ipw_weight'] = ipw_weights
df_est['ps'] = ps

ipw_model = smf.wls('productivity ~ treated', data=df_est, weights=df_est['ipw_weight']).fit(
    cov_type='HC3'  # heteroskedasticity-robust SEs
)
print("\n=== IPW Estimate (comparison) ===")
print(f"ATT:    {ipw_model.params['treated']:.3f}")
print(f"95% CI: [{ipw_model.conf_int().loc['treated', 0]:.3f}, "
      f"{ipw_model.conf_int().loc['treated', 1]:.3f}]")

# ── Covariate balance diagnostics ───────────────────────────────
print("\n=== Covariate Balance (Standardized Mean Differences) ===")
print(f"{'Covariate':<25} {'SMD_raw':>10} {'SMD_weighted':>13} {'Balanced?':>10}")
print("-" * 62)

balance_rows = []
for i, col in enumerate(covariate_cols):
    treated_vals = X[D == 1, i]
    control_vals = X[D == 0, i]
    control_wts  = ipw_weights[D == 0]

    pooled_sd = np.std(X[:, i], ddof=1)
    smd_raw = (treated_vals.mean() - control_vals.mean()) / pooled_sd
    smd_wtd = (treated_vals.mean() - np.average(control_vals, weights=control_wts)) / pooled_sd

    balanced = abs(smd_wtd) < 0.1
    balance_rows.append({'covariate': col, 'SMD_raw': smd_raw,
                         'SMD_weighted': smd_wtd, 'balanced': balanced})
    flag = "✓" if balanced else "✗"
    print(f"{col:<25} {smd_raw:>10.3f} {smd_wtd:>13.3f} {flag:>10}")

balance_df = pd.DataFrame(balance_rows)
print(f"\nAll covariates balanced (|SMD| < 0.1): {balance_df['balanced'].all()}")

# ── Love plot ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, max(4, len(covariate_cols) * 0.5 + 1)))

y_pos = range(len(covariate_cols))
ax.scatter(balance_df['SMD_raw'],     y_pos, label='Before weighting',
           color='tomato', s=60, zorder=3)
ax.scatter(balance_df['SMD_weighted'], y_pos, label='After IPW weighting',
           color='steelblue', s=60, zorder=3)

ax.axvline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline( 0.1, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(-0.1, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_yticks(list(y_pos))
ax.set_yticklabels(balance_df['covariate'])
ax.set_xlabel('Standardized Mean Difference')
ax.set_title('Covariate Balance — Love Plot\n(dashed lines = ±0.1 threshold)')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('love_plot.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
```

**What to look for**:
- Love plot should show all SMDs moving *toward* 0 after weighting, ideally inside the ±0.1 dashed lines
- DR and IPW estimates should be in the same ballpark — large divergence suggests model sensitivity
- If any covariate remains imbalanced after weighting, the propensity score model may be misspecified for that variable

---

## Stage 4: Falsification / Robustness

Two checks I'd prioritize for your study:

### Check 1: Sensitivity Analysis (E-value)
How strong would an unobserved confounder need to be — in terms of its association with both treatment *and* productivity — to fully explain away your estimated effect? A small E-value means the finding is fragile.

```python
import numpy as np

def e_value_mean_diff(effect, se, outcome_sd):
    """
    E-value for a mean difference (continuous outcome).
    Converts to a risk ratio scale via approximation, then computes E-value.
    effect: estimated ATT
    se: standard error of the estimate
    outcome_sd: standard deviation of the outcome
    """
    # Approximate risk ratio (VanderWeele & Ding approximation for continuous outcomes)
    # Scale effect to Cohen's d, then approximate RR
    d = abs(effect) / outcome_sd
    rr_approx = np.exp(0.91 * d)  # standard approximation

    # Lower bound of confidence interval (convert to RR)
    d_lo = abs(effect - 1.96 * se) / outcome_sd
    rr_lo = np.exp(0.91 * d_lo)

    # E-value = RR + sqrt(RR * (RR - 1))
    ev = rr_approx + np.sqrt(rr_approx * (rr_approx - 1))
    ev_ci = rr_lo + np.sqrt(rr_lo * (rr_lo - 1))

    return ev, ev_ci

outcome_sd = df['productivity'].std()

# Replace with your actual DR estimate and SE
estimated_effect = ate              # from DR learner above
estimated_se = (ate_interval[1] - ate_interval[0]) / (2 * 1.96)

ev, ev_ci = e_value_mean_diff(estimated_effect, estimated_se, outcome_sd)

print("=== Sensitivity Analysis: E-value ===")
print(f"Estimated ATT:  {estimated_effect:.3f}")
print(f"E-value:        {ev:.2f}")
print(f"E-value (CI):   {ev_ci:.2f}")
print(f"\nInterpretation: An unobserved confounder would need to be associated")
print(f"with BOTH treatment selection AND productivity by a risk ratio of at")
print(f"least {ev:.2f} (and {ev_ci:.2f} for the CI bound) to fully explain away")
print(f"the estimated effect. Is a confounder that strong plausible?")
print(f"\n(e.g., motivation, manager support — could these have RR > {ev:.2f}?)")
```

### Check 2: Placebo Outcome
Pick a baseline characteristic that should NOT be affected by a training program — for example, employees' **age** or **tenure** at the time of study. Run the full analysis using that as the "outcome." Any estimated "effect" would be a red flag for residual confounding.

```python
# Placebo outcome: age (should be unaffected by training)
Y_placebo = df['age'].values

dr_placebo = LinearDRLearner(
    model_propensity=GradientBoostingClassifier(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
    model_regression=GradientBoostingRegressor(n_estimators=100, random_state=42),
    cv=5, random_state=42
)
dr_placebo.fit(Y=Y_placebo, T=D, X=None, W=X)

placebo_ate = dr_placebo.ate(X=None)
placebo_ci  = dr_placebo.ate_interval(X=None, alpha=0.05)

print("=== Placebo Outcome Test (Age) ===")
print(f"Estimated 'effect' on age: {placebo_ate:.3f}")
print(f"95% CI: [{placebo_ci[0]:.3f}, {placebo_ci[1]:.3f}]")
print(f"\nExpected: CI should include 0. If it doesn't, matching failed to")
print(f"remove pre-existing differences between groups.")
```

Once you have your numbers, here's the plain-language summary to fill in:

> *Based on the doubly-robust matching analysis, participation in the training program is associated with an estimated productivity increase of **[ATT] units** (95% CI: [lower, upper]) among employees who participated.*
>
> *This estimate was obtained using a doubly-robust learner (DR Learner), which combines gradient-boosted propensity score and outcome models. Covariate balance after inverse probability weighting was [good / acceptable / poor — describe SMDs].*
>
> **⚠️ Causal interpretation requires caution.** This estimate is credible only if age, tenure, and department capture all meaningful reasons why employees self-selected into training. Unobserved factors — particularly employee motivation and manager support — are plausible confounders that could bias these results upward (if motivated employees both self-selected and were more productive).
>
> *Sensitivity analysis (E-value): An unobserved confounder would need to be associated with both training participation and productivity by a factor of at least [E-value] to explain away this result. [Assess whether such a confounder is plausible in this context.]*

3. **Assess the E-value** — if it's small (say, < 1.5), the finding is fragile and should be communicated carefully.

### rdd_clean_cutoff
