<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great — you've given me everything I need. Let me walk through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Design** | Sharp RDD — scholarship is deterministic at score ≥ 70 |
| **Running variable** | `test_score` |
| **Cutoff** | 70 |
| **Outcome** | `gpa` |
| **N** | 2,000 students |
| **Language** | Python |

Sharp RDD is the right call here: treatment isn't probabilistic — every student at or above 70 receives the scholarship. The estimand is the **Local Average Treatment Effect at the cutoff** — what the scholarship does to GPA for students scoring right around 70.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. No Manipulation of the Running Variable 🟡 *Plausible but test it*

**What it means here**: Could students have known the cutoff (score = 70) and strategically influenced their test score to land just above it? If teachers rounded borderline grades up, or students retook tests targeting 70, you'd see an artificial bunching of students just above 70 — and those students would differ systematically from those just below.

**Why it matters**: If students just above 70 are systematically more motivated (they pushed to get there), comparing them to students just below gives you a biased estimate — you'd be picking up "motivation effect" in addition to the scholarship effect.

**You mentioned** the distribution looks smooth — that's encouraging. But we should still run the formal test. The McCrary/`rddensity` test will flag a statistically significant density jump if bunching exists.

> ✅ Testable — density test included in the code below.

---

### 2. Continuity of Potential Outcomes 🟡 *Plausible but check covariates*

**What it means here**: In the absence of the scholarship, students' GPA would change smoothly as test score crosses 70. No other factor that affects GPA also jumps at exactly score = 70.

**Why it matters**: This is the core identifying assumption. If, say, students above 70 also got assigned to a special tutoring program, you'd be estimating the combined effect of the scholarship *and* tutoring — not the scholarship alone.

**One concern**: You only have `gpa` and `test_score` in the data. Without any pre-treatment covariates (e.g., prior GPA, demographics, family income), we can't run the covariate smoothness checks. Do you have any additional variables? Even one pre-treatment variable would strengthen this.

> ⚠️ Partially testable — requires pre-treatment covariates you may not have yet.

---

### 3. No Compound Treatments 🟡 *Ask yourself this*

**What it means here**: Is the scholarship the **only** thing that changes at the score-70 cutoff? Does your institution use the same cutoff for anything else — honors designation, dormitory priority, course placement, financial aid tiers?

**Why it matters**: If honors status is also granted at 70, your RD estimate captures "scholarship + honors" not just the scholarship. You can't disentangle them without additional variation.

> ❓ Not testable from data alone — requires institutional knowledge.

**Quick check**: Does anything else in your school use score = 70 as a threshold?

---

### 4. Local Identification ✅ *Inherent property — just interpret carefully*

**What it means here**: Your estimate will tell you what the scholarship does for students scoring *near 70* — not for students scoring 50 or 90. The effect might be different for weaker or stronger students.

**Why it matters**: If your goal is to inform a policy that expands the scholarship to all students, the RDD estimate doesn't directly answer that. It answers: "Does the scholarship help students on the margin of eligibility?"

> ✅ Not a violation — just an interpretation caveat to keep front of mind.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | 🟡 Plausible | Run density test |
| Continuity of outcomes | 🟡 Plausible | Add covariates if available |
| No compound treatments | 🟡 Unknown | Check institutionally |
| Local identification | ✅ Inherent | Interpret carefully |

No fatal violations — you're good to proceed.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# ============================================================
# Sharp RDD: Effect of Scholarship on GPA
# Running variable: test_score | Cutoff: 70
# ============================================================

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import seaborn as sns
from rdrobust import rdrobust, rdplot, rdbwselect
from rddensity import rddensity

# --- 0. Load data ---
# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")  # uncomment if loading from file
cutoff = 70

Y = df["gpa"].values
X = df["test_score"].values

# Quick sanity check
print("=== Data Overview ===")
print(f"N total: {len(df)}")
print(f"N below cutoff (control): {(X < cutoff).sum()}")
print(f"N at/above cutoff (treated): {(X >= cutoff).sum()}")
print(f"Test score range: [{X.min():.2f}, {X.max():.2f}]")
print(f"Mean GPA (control): {Y[X < cutoff].mean():.3f}")
print(f"Mean GPA (treated): {Y[X >= cutoff].mean():.3f}")

# ============================================================
# 1. MANIPULATION TEST (McCrary / rddensity)
# ============================================================
# Are students bunching just above 70? A jump in the density
# would suggest students could manipulate their score.

density_test = rddensity(X, c=cutoff)
print("\n=== Manipulation Test (rddensity) ===")
print(f"T-statistic: {density_test.test.t_jk:.4f}")
print(f"p-value:     {density_test.test.p_jk:.4f}")
if density_test.test.p_jk < 0.05:
    print("⚠️  WARNING: Significant bunching detected — possible manipulation")
else:
    print("✅ No evidence of manipulation at the cutoff")

# ============================================================
# 2. RD PLOT — visual inspection
# ============================================================
# Does GPA visually jump at score = 70?
# We use rdplot for automatic IMSE-optimal binning, then
# redraw manually for clean side-by-side output.

fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(13, 5))

# --- Panel 1: Binned scatter ---
ax = axes[0]
n_bins = 30

for side, color, label in [("left", "steelblue", "No Scholarship"), 
                             ("right", "coral", "Scholarship")]:
    mask = (X < cutoff) if side == "left" else (X >= cutoff)
    x_sub, y_sub = X[mask], Y[mask]
    bins = np.linspace(x_sub.min(), x_sub.max(), n_bins // 2 + 1)
    bin_idx = np.digitize(x_sub, bins)
    bin_means_x = [x_sub[bin_idx == i].mean() 
                   for i in range(1, len(bins)) if (bin_idx == i).sum() > 0]
    bin_means_y = [y_sub[bin_idx == i].mean() 
                   for i in range(1, len(bins)) if (bin_idx == i).sum() > 0]
    ax.scatter(bin_means_x, bin_means_y, color=color, s=35, alpha=0.8, label=label)

ax.axvline(cutoff, color="black", linestyle="--", linewidth=1.2, label="Cutoff (70)")
ax.set_xlabel("Test Score")
ax.set_ylabel("GPA (binned means)")
ax.set_title("RD Plot: Scholarship → GPA")
ax.legend()
sns.despine(ax=ax)

# ============================================================
# 3. MAIN RD ESTIMATE
# ============================================================
# rdrobust uses local linear regression with MSE-optimal bandwidth
# and reports three sets of estimates:
#   - Conventional: standard OLS near cutoff
#   - Bias-corrected: adjusts for polynomial approximation bias
#   - Robust: bias-corrected with valid confidence intervals (USE THIS ONE)

rd_est = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff)
print("\n=== Main RD Estimate ===")
print(rd_est)

# Extract key numbers
est_conv    = rd_est.coef.iloc[0]   # conventional
est_robust  = rd_est.coef.iloc[2]   # robust (bias-corrected)
ci_lo       = rd_est.ci.iloc[2, 0]  # robust CI lower
ci_hi       = rd_est.ci.iloc[2, 1]  # robust CI upper
bw_h        = rd_est.bws.iloc[0, 0] # main bandwidth
n_left      = rd_est.N_h[0]
n_right     = rd_est.N_h[1]

print(f"\nRobust estimate:  {est_robust:.4f}")
print(f"95% Robust CI:   [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"Bandwidth (h):    {bw_h:.2f} score points")
print(f"Effective N:      {n_left} below, {n_right} above cutoff")

# ============================================================
# 4. BANDWIDTH SENSITIVITY
# ============================================================
# If the estimate is stable across bandwidths, you can be more
# confident it's not an artifact of the chosen window.

bandwidths = [bw_h * m for m in [0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5]]
bw_results = []

for bw in bandwidths:
    try:
        res = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, h=bw)
        bw_results.append({
            "bandwidth":  round(bw, 2),
            "estimate":   res.coef.iloc[2],     # robust
            "ci_lo":      res.ci.iloc[2, 0],
            "ci_hi":      res.ci.iloc[2, 1],
            "n_eff":      res.N_h[0] + res.N_h[1],
        })
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Bandwidth {bw:.2f} failed: {e}")

bw_df = pd.DataFrame(bw_results)
print("\n=== Bandwidth Sensitivity ===")
print(bw_df.to_string(index=False, float_format="%.4f"))

# --- Panel 2: Bandwidth sensitivity plot ---
ax = axes[1]
ax.errorbar(
    bw_df["bandwidth"], bw_df["estimate"],
    yerr=[bw_df["estimate"] - bw_df["ci_lo"], bw_df["ci_hi"] - bw_df["estimate"]],
    fmt="o-", capsize=5, color="steelblue", markersize=7
)
ax.axhline(0, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=0.8)
ax.axvline(bw_h, color="red", linestyle=":", linewidth=1, label=f"Optimal BW ({bw_h:.1f})")
ax.set_xlabel("Bandwidth (score points)")
ax.set_ylabel("RD Estimate (Robust)")
ax.set_title("Bandwidth Sensitivity")
ax.legend()
sns.despine(ax=ax)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("rdd_diagnostics.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification / Robustness

Three checks I'd recommend for your case:

**1. Donut hole test** — exclude students within ±1 point of the cutoff (scores 69–71). If mild manipulation exists at the boundary, the estimate should still hold once those students are removed. This is especially worth running given you haven't formally confirmed no manipulation yet.

```python
# Donut hole: exclude students within 1 point of cutoff
donut_mask = np.abs(X - cutoff) > 1
Y_donut = Y[donut_mask]
X_donut = X[donut_mask]

rd_donut = rdrobust(Y_donut, X_donut, c=cutoff)
print("\n=== Donut Hole Test (±1 point excluded) ===")
print(f"Estimate (robust): {rd_donut.coef.iloc[2]:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI: [{rd_donut.ci.iloc[2,0]:.4f}, {rd_donut.ci.iloc[2,1]:.4f}]")
print(f"Effective N: {rd_donut.N_h[0]} below, {rd_donut.N_h[1]} above")
```

**2. Placebo cutoffs** — run the same RD at scores 60 and 80, where there's no scholarship threshold. We should find no effect there. A spurious effect at a placebo cutoff suggests your local polynomial is fitting noise.

```python
# Placebo cutoffs: no treatment change should produce no effect
placebo_cutoffs = [60, 80]

print("\n=== Placebo Cutoff Tests ===")
for pc in placebo_cutoffs:
    # Only use data on one side of the real cutoff for each placebo
    if pc < cutoff:
        mask = X < cutoff  # use only control group
    else:
        mask = X >= cutoff  # use only treated group
    try:
        res = rdrobust(Y[mask], X[mask], c=pc)
        print(f"Placebo cutoff = {pc}: "
              f"estimate = {res.coef.iloc[2]:.4f}, "
              f"p = {res.pv.iloc[2]:.4f}")
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Placebo cutoff {pc} failed: {e}")
```

**3. Polynomial order check** — compare local linear (default, p=1) vs. local quadratic (p=2). The estimates should be in the same ballpark.

```python
print("\n=== Polynomial Order Sensitivity ===")
for p in [1, 2]:
    res = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, p=p)
    print(f"Order p={p}: estimate = {res.coef.iloc[2]:.4f}, "
          f"CI = [{res.ci.iloc[2,0]:.4f}, {res.ci.iloc[2,1]:.4f}]")
```

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

This is a clean **Sharp RDD** setup — scholarship is deterministic at the cutoff (score ≥ 70 → treated). I have everything I need:

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. No Manipulation of the Running Variable ⚠️ *Test required*

**What it means here**: Can students precisely control their test score to land just above 70? If tutors coach borderline students, or graders round up, you'd see a suspicious pile-up of scores just above 70 — and those students would be systematically different from students just below.

**Why it matters**: If students sort themselves above the cutoff, the "just above" and "just below" groups aren't comparable anymore. The whole design relies on them being as-if randomly assigned near the threshold.

> **Question**: Was there any way students knew the exact cutoff beforehand, or could retake the test to improve their score?

We'll run the McCrary density test either way — it's the primary diagnostic here.

---

### 2. Continuity of Potential Outcomes ⚠️ *Partially testable*

**What it means here**: In the absence of the scholarship, GPAs would change smoothly through the score = 70 threshold. The only reason GPA jumps at 70 is the scholarship itself — not, say, students above 70 also getting access to better housing, advisors, or a different curriculum.

**Why it matters**: If something else also changes at 70, your estimate is a blend of the scholarship's effect and that other thing's effect.

> **Question**: Do any other programs, benefits, or rules use a score of 70 as their threshold? (e.g., honors track placement, fee waivers, priority registration)

We'll test this by checking whether pre-determined covariates (things like prior GPA or demographics) are smooth through the cutoff.

---

### 3. No Compound Treatments *Ask required*

**What it means here**: The scholarship is the **only** thing that changes at score = 70. If students above 70 also automatically get, say, a mandatory tutoring program or a different academic track, you'd be estimating the joint effect of all those things — not just the scholarship.

> **Question**: Is the scholarship the only treatment assigned at this cutoff?

---

### 4. Sufficient Data Near the Cutoff ✓ *Likely fine*

**What it means here**: `rdrobust` will pick a data-driven bandwidth — only students within some window around 70 will be used. With 2,000 students, this is likely fine, but it depends on the score distribution.

We'll see how many observations fall within the optimal bandwidth after running the model.

---

### Assumption Summary

| # | Assumption | Status | Testable? |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | No manipulation | ❓ Check required | ✅ McCrary test |
| 2 | Continuity / no other jump | ❓ Context-dependent | ✅ Covariate smoothness |
| 3 | No compound treatments | ❓ Need your input | ❌ Domain knowledge |
| 4 | Enough data near cutoff | 🟡 Likely fine | ✅ Check N in bandwidth |

Feel free to answer those three questions — but we can also proceed to code now and revisit if the diagnostics raise flags.

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis:

```r
library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)
library(ggplot2)

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

Y <- df$gpa
X <- df$test_score
cutoff <- 70

# ── 1. RD Plot ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Visual inspection: is there a visible jump in GPA at score = 70?
rdplot(
  y     = Y,
  x     = X,
  c     = cutoff,
  title   = "Effect of Scholarship on GPA",
  x.label = "Test Score",
  y.label = "GPA"
)
```

> 👁️ **What to look for**: A clear vertical jump in the fitted line at 70. If you don't see a jump visually, the effect is likely small or noisy — trust the formal estimate, but be cautious.

```r
# ── 2. McCrary Density Test (Manipulation Check) ──────────────────────────────
# Tests for a suspicious spike in observations just above/below the cutoff
density_test <- rddensity(X = X, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

# Visual companion to the test
rdplotdensity(
  rdd    = density_test,
  X      = X,
  title  = "McCrary Density Test",
  xlabel = "Test Score",
  ylabel = "Density"
)

# Raw histogram — often the most intuitive check
ggplot(df, aes(x = test_score)) +
  geom_histogram(binwidth = 1, fill = "steelblue", color = "white") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = cutoff, color = "red", linetype = "dashed",
             linewidth = 1) +
  labs(
    title = "Distribution of Test Scores",
    x     = "Test Score",
    y     = "Count"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()
```

> 👁️ **What to look for**: The density test p-value should be **> 0.05** (no evidence of manipulation). In the histogram, the bar just above 70 should not be suspiciously taller than its neighbors.

```r
# ── 3. Main RD Estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# rdrobust uses local linear regression + MSE-optimal bandwidth selection
# Confidence intervals are bias-corrected and robust (Calonico et al. 2014)
rd_main <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff)
summary(rd_main)
```

> 📊 **How to read the output**: Look at the **"Robust"** row — that's the bias-corrected estimate with the correct standard errors. The conventional row underestimates uncertainty. Key numbers: coefficient (the treatment effect), confidence interval, and bandwidth (`h`).

```r
# ── 4. Covariate Smoothness Checks ───────────────────────────────────────────
# Run RDD using pre-determined covariates as the "outcome."
# None of them should show a discontinuity at the cutoff.
# Replace with your actual pre-determined covariates.

# Example: if you have prior GPA or demographics
# covariates <- c("prior_gpa", "age", "income")
# for (cov in covariates) {
#   rd_cov <- rdrobust(y = df[[cov]], x = X, c = cutoff)
#   cat("\n--- Covariate:", cov, "---\n")
#   print(summary(rd_cov))
# }

# ── 5. Quick bandwidth report ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
cat("Optimal bandwidth (h):", rd_main$bws["h", "left"], "\n")
cat("N used (left of cutoff):", rd_main$N_h[1], "\n")
cat("N used (right of cutoff):", rd_main$N_h[2], "\n")
cat("Total N in bandwidth:", sum(rd_main$N_h), "\n")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Run at least two of these:

```r
# ── A. Bandwidth Sensitivity ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
# The estimate should be stable across different bandwidth choices.
# Large swings suggest the effect varies with distance from the cutoff (okay)
# or that the functional form is misspecified (bad).

opt_h <- rd_main$bws["h", "left"]
bandwidths <- opt_h * c(0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5)

for (bw in bandwidths) {
  rd_bw <- rdrobust(y = Y, x = X, c = cutoff, h = bw)
  cat(sprintf(
    "h = %.2f | Estimate = %.4f | CI = [%.4f, %.4f]\n",
    bw,
    rd_bw$coef["Robust", ],
    rd_bw$ci["Robust", "CI Lower"],
    rd_bw$ci["Robust", "CI Upper"]
  ))
}

# ── B. Placebo Cutoffs ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Run the RD at score values where there's NO treatment.
# Finding an "effect" at score = 60 or 80 would be a red flag.

placebo_cutoffs <- c(60, 65, 75, 80)

for (pc in placebo_cutoffs) {
  # Restrict to one side of the true cutoff to avoid contamination
  df_placebo <- df[df$test_score < cutoff, ]   # left-side placebo
  if (pc >= cutoff) df_placebo <- df[df$test_score >= cutoff, ]

  rd_placebo <- rdrobust(y = df_placebo$gpa, x = df_placebo$test_score,
                         c = pc)
  cat(sprintf(
    "Placebo cutoff = %d | Estimate = %.4f | p = %.4f\n",
    pc,
    rd_placebo$coef["Robust", ],
    rd_placebo$pv["Robust", ]
  ))
}

# ── C. Donut Hole Test ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Exclude students very close to the cutoff (scores 69–71).
# If the effect survives, it's not driven by a few borderline cases.

df_donut <- df[abs(df$test_score - cutoff) > 1, ]
rd_donut <- rdrobust(y = df_donut$gpa, x = df_donut$test_score, c = cutoff)
cat("\n--- Donut Hole (excluding |score - 70| <= 1) ---\n")
summary(rd_donut)
```

## Stage 5: Interpreting Your Results

Once you run the code, here's the template for reading the output:

**The estimated effect** of receiving the scholarship on GPA is `[coefficient]` GPA points (95% robust CI: `[lower, upper]`). This is estimated using students with test scores within `[h]` points of the cutoff (N = `[N in bandwidth]`).

**Local scope**: This effect applies specifically to students who scored near 70 on the test — the marginal scholarship recipients. Students who score 50 or 90 may respond quite differently; the RDD cannot speak to them.

**McCrary test**: If p > 0.05 → "No evidence that students manipulated their scores to cross the threshold." If p < 0.05 → flag this prominently.

**Robustness**: If estimates are stable across bandwidths and placebo cutoffs show no effect, the finding is credible.

### sc_poor_prefit

**Run 1**: {"violation_detected": true, "flags_coverage": 1.0, "severity_correct": true}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmation

Good setup for classic synthetic control:

| Parameter | Value |
|---|---|
| Treated unit | State A |
| Treatment period | 20 |
| Pre-treatment periods | 1–19 (19 periods — workable, on the lean side) |
| Post-treatment periods | 20–40 |
| Donor pool | 10 states |
| Method | Classic SC → `Synth` package |

One note before generating code: your data only shows `outcome` and `treated` alongside the panel identifiers. I'll use **pre-treatment outcome lags as predictors** (the standard approach), which works well when no additional covariates are available. If you have economic indicators or other state-level variables, add them later and I'll show you how.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Walking through each one for your context:

**1. Pre-treatment fit quality** ⚠️ *Check required*
With 19 pre-periods and 10 donors, fit is usually achievable — but it depends on how much State A diverges from the donor pool. We'll inspect RMSPE after fitting. If pre-RMSPE is large relative to the outcome's standard deviation (say, >10%), the counterfactual is unreliable.

**2. Convex hull** ⚠️ *Check required*
State A's pre-treatment outcome trajectory needs to lie within the range spanned by the 10 donors. If State A is consistently above or below all donors, no weighted combination can match it without extrapolation. We'll check this by inspecting whether the optimizer achieves good balance — and whether weights concentrate heavily on 1–2 donors (a warning sign).

**3. No interference between units** ✅ *Must be argued*
Did the policy in State A spill over to your 10 donor states? For example, through business migration, trade, or competitive policy responses? If donors are neighbors or major trading partners, this is a real concern. You'll need to argue this is unlikely based on your context.

**4. Donor pool composition** ✅ *Must be argued*
Did any of the 10 donor states experience their own large policy shocks between periods 20–40? A donor hit by its own unrelated shock produces a contaminated counterfactual. Remove problematic donors before fitting.

**5. No anticipation** ✅ *Likely testable*
Did State A change behavior *before* period 20 in anticipation of the policy? We can check this visually — if the gap between State A and its synthetic version starts opening before period 20, that's a red flag.

**Summary:**

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Pre-treatment fit | ⚠️ Check after fitting | Inspect RMSPE |
| Convex hull | ⚠️ Check after fitting | Inspect donor weights |
| No interference | 🔵 Argue substantively | Consider donor geography |
| Donor pool clean | 🔵 Argue substantively | Remove shocked donors |
| No anticipation | ✅ Testable visually | Inspect pre-period gap |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
library(Synth)
library(tidyverse)

# ── 0. Prep ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Synth requires a numeric unit variable
df <- df %>%
  mutate(state_id = as.integer(factor(state))) %>%
  arrange(state_id, period)

treated_id <- df %>% filter(state == "A") %>% pull(state_id) %>% unique()
donor_ids  <- df %>% filter(state != "A") %>% pull(state_id) %>% unique()

cat("Treated ID:", treated_id, "\n")
cat("Donor IDs:", donor_ids, "\n")

# ── 1. dataprep ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Using pre-treatment outcome mean + key lags as predictors (standard approach)
dataprep_out <- dataprep(
  foo                   = as.data.frame(df),
  predictors            = "outcome",
  predictors.op         = "mean",
  dependent             = "outcome",
  unit.variable         = "state_id",
  time.variable         = "period",
  treatment.identifier  = treated_id,
  controls.identifier   = donor_ids,
  time.predictors.prior = 1:19,
  time.optimize.ssr     = 1:19,
  time.plot             = 1:40,
  # Anchor to specific pre-treatment lags for richer matching
  special.predictors    = list(
    list("outcome", 5,  "mean"),
    list("outcome", 10, "mean"),
    list("outcome", 15, "mean"),
    list("outcome", 19, "mean")
  )
)

# ── 2. Fit synthetic control ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_out <- synth(dataprep_out)

# ── 3. Donor weights ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
synth_tables <- synth.tab(dataprep.res = dataprep_out, synth.res = synth_out)

cat("\n── Donor weights ──\n")
print(synth_tables$tab.w)

cat("\n── Predictor weights ──\n")
print(synth_tables$tab.v)

# ── 4. Pre-treatment RMSPE ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
synthetic_vals <- dataprep_out$Y0plot %*% synth_out$solution.w
actual_vals    <- dataprep_out$Y1plot
gap            <- actual_vals - synthetic_vals

pre_rmspe  <- sqrt(mean(gap[1:19]^2))
post_rmspe <- sqrt(mean(gap[20:40]^2))

cat("\nPre-treatment RMSPE :", round(pre_rmspe, 3))
cat("\nOutcome SD (pre)    :", round(sd(actual_vals[1:19]), 3))
cat("\nRMSPE / SD ratio    :", round(pre_rmspe / sd(actual_vals[1:19]), 3), "\n")
# Rule of thumb: ratio < 0.10 is good; > 0.20 is concerning

# ── 5. Plot: treated vs synthetic ─────────────────────────────────────────────
path.plot(
  synth.res        = synth_out,
  dataprep.res     = dataprep_out,
  Ylab             = "Outcome",
  Xlab             = "Period",
  Legend           = c("State A", "Synthetic State A"),
  Legend.position  = "bottomright"
)
abline(v = 20, lty = 2, col = "red")
title("State A vs. Synthetic Control")

# ── 6. Plot: treatment effect gap ─────────────────────────────────────────────
gaps.plot(
  synth.res    = synth_out,
  dataprep.res = dataprep_out,
  Ylab         = "Gap (State A − Synthetic)",
  Xlab         = "Period"
)
abline(v = 20, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3, col = "gray50")
title("Estimated Treatment Effect")

# ── 7. Point estimate ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
avg_effect       <- mean(gap[20:40])
cumulative_effect <- sum(gap[20:40])

cat("\nAverage per-period effect:", round(avg_effect, 3))
cat("\nCumulative effect        :", round(cumulative_effect, 3), "\n")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification — In-Space Placebo Test

This is the key inferential test for synthetic control. The idea: apply the exact same method to each donor state, as if *it* were the treated unit. If State A's post-treatment gap is large compared to what donors produce by chance, that's evidence of a real effect. We compute a pseudo p-value from the RMSPE ratio distribution.

```r
# ── In-space placebo ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
placebo_gaps <- matrix(NA, nrow = 40, ncol = length(donor_ids))
pre_rmspe_placebo <- rep(NA, length(donor_ids))

for (i in seq_along(donor_ids)) {
  placebo_id     <- donor_ids[i]
  placebo_donors <- c(treated_id, donor_ids[-i])  # all others as donors

  tryCatch({
    pp <- dataprep(
      foo                   = as.data.frame(df),
      predictors            = "outcome",
      predictors.op         = "mean",
      dependent             = "outcome",
      unit.variable         = "state_id",
      time.variable         = "period",
      treatment.identifier  = placebo_id,
      controls.identifier   = placebo_donors,
      time.predictors.prior = 1:19,
      time.optimize.ssr     = 1:19,
      time.plot             = 1:40,
      special.predictors    = list(
        list("outcome", 5,  "mean"),
        list("outcome", 10, "mean"),
        list("outcome", 15, "mean"),
        list("outcome", 19, "mean")
      )
    )
    ps <- synth(pp, quiet = TRUE)
    placebo_gaps[, i]    <- pp$Y1plot - (pp$Y0plot %*% ps$solution.w)
    pre_rmspe_placebo[i] <- sqrt(mean(placebo_gaps[1:19, i]^2, na.rm = TRUE))
  }, error = function(e) {
    cat("Placebo failed for donor", placebo_id, ":", conditionMessage(e), "\n")
  })
}

# ── Drop placebos with poor pre-fit (standard practice) ──────────────────────
# Donors whose pre-RMSPE > 5x State A's pre-RMSPE are unreliable as placebos
keep <- which(pre_rmspe_placebo <= 5 * pre_rmspe)
cat("Placebos retained:", length(keep), "of", length(donor_ids), "\n")

# ── Pseudo p-value: post/pre RMSPE ratio ─────────────────────────────────────
ratio_actual <- post_rmspe / pre_rmspe

ratios_placebo <- sapply(keep, function(i) {
  sqrt(mean(placebo_gaps[20:40, i]^2, na.rm = TRUE)) /
  sqrt(mean(placebo_gaps[1:19,  i]^2, na.rm = TRUE))
})

pseudo_p <- mean(c(ratios_placebo, ratio_actual) >= ratio_actual, na.rm = TRUE)
cat("Post/Pre RMSPE ratio — State A:", round(ratio_actual, 2))
cat("\nPseudo p-value               :", round(pseudo_p, 3), "\n")

# ── Plot: State A vs. donor placebos ─────────────────────────────────────────
matplot(
  1:40, placebo_gaps[, keep],
  type = "l", lty = 1, col = "gray80",
  xlab = "Period", ylab = "Gap (Unit − Synthetic)",
  main = "In-Space Placebo Test",
  ylim = range(c(gap, placebo_gaps[, keep]), na.rm = TRUE)
)
lines(1:40, gap, col = "black", lwd = 2.5)
abline(v = 20, lty = 2, col = "red")
abline(h = 0, lty = 3, col = "gray40")
legend("topright",
       legend = c("State A", "Donor placebos"),
       col    = c("black", "gray70"),
       lwd    = c(2.5, 1))
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you have results, here's the structure for your write-up:

> *"The synthetic control for State A was constructed from a weighted combination of donor states. The pre-treatment RMSPE was [X], representing [Y]% of the pre-treatment standard deviation — indicating [good/moderate/poor] fit.*
>
> *In the post-treatment period (periods 20–40), State A's outcome was on average [avg_effect] [above/below] its synthetic counterpart, with a cumulative effect of [cumulative_effect].*
>
> *In-space placebo tests applied the same method to all [N] donor states. State A's post/pre RMSPE ratio of [ratio_actual] ranked [rank] out of [N+1], yielding a pseudo p-value of [pseudo_p]. [Interpretation: values <0.10 are conventionally significant.]*
>
> *Key caveats: [pre-treatment fit quality / donor pool concerns / whether any donors had large weights]."*

One quick question before we dive in: **Is there any annual seasonality in this outcome?** (e.g., does it spike every 12 months?) If yes, we need to account for it — 36 months only covers 3 cycles, which is the minimum.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific setup.

---

### ① No concurrent events ⚠️ *Untestable — must be argued*

> "Did anything else happen around month 37 that could affect the outcome?"

This is the single biggest threat. A policy change is often announced before it takes effect (anticipation), or coincides with other changes. Think: were there other organizational, regulatory, or economic changes at that same time?

**Why it matters**: If yes, you can't separate the policy effect from the concurrent event. No amount of statistical modeling fixes this.

---

### ② Pre-period model fit ✅ *Testable — we'll check in Stage 3*

Your pre-period data looks stable and low-variance (~20–21), which is a good sign. We'll formally check residuals after fitting.

---

### ③ Stationarity ✅ *Testable — run this now*

```r
library(tseries)

pre <- ts(df$outcome[df$post == 0], frequency = 12)

# ADF: H0 = unit root (non-stationary)
adf.test(pre)
# Want: p < 0.05

# KPSS: H0 = stationary
kpss.test(pre)
# Want: p > 0.05

# Visual check
plot(pre, main = "Pre-Period Outcome", ylab = "Outcome")
acf(pre, main = "ACF — Pre-Period")
```

> Given your sample (values hovering around 20–21 with small variance), this series looks stationary at a glance. Confirm with the test.

---

### ④ Adequate pre-period length ⚠️ *Borderline*

36 pre-treatment months is **workable but on the low end** — especially if there's seasonality. The rule of thumb is 3 full seasonal cycles minimum (= 36 months for annual patterns). You're right at the floor.

**Why it matters**: Too few observations → wide confidence intervals → you may not detect a real effect.

If seasonality isn't present, 36 months is fine for a simple ITS.

---

### ⑤ Structural break check 🔍 *Pre-flight — do this before modeling*

Even though you said the pre-period looks stable, let's formally confirm. A hidden break in months 1–36 would invalidate the counterfactual projection.

```r
library(strucchange)

pre_series <- ts(df$outcome[df$post == 0])

# CUSUM test — looks for instability in parameters over time
cusum <- efp(pre_series ~ time(pre_series), type = "OLS-CUSUM")
plot(cusum)
# If line stays within bounds → stable ✓

# Bai-Perron for multiple breakpoints
bp <- breakpoints(pre_series ~ 1)
summary(bp)
# BIC = Inf or "0 breakpoints" → no break detected ✓
```

---

### Assumption Summary

| # | Assumption | Status | Action needed |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | No concurrent events | ❓ Argue | What else happened at month 37? |
| 2 | Pre-period fit | 🔲 Test in Stage 3 | Check residuals after fitting |
| 3 | Stationarity | 🔲 Test | Run ADF/KPSS above |
| 4 | Pre-period length | ⚠️ Borderline | OK if no seasonality; tight if seasonal |
| 5 | No structural break | 🔲 Test | Run CUSUM/Bai-Perron above |

No fatal violations flagged — safe to proceed. Let me know if assumption ① raises any flags. Otherwise:

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(sandwich)
library(lmtest)

# ── 1. Build ITS variables ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    time_since = if_else(post == 1, time - 36, 0)  # slope change after month 37
  )

# ── 2. Fit segmented regression ──────────────────────────────────────────────
# Level change (post) + slope change (time_since)
# HAC standard errors: robust to autocorrelation in residuals
model <- lm(outcome ~ time + post + time_since, data = df)
model_hac <- coeftest(model, vcov = NeweyWest(model, lag = 4))

print(model_hac)

# ── 3. Interpret coefficients ────────────────────────────────────────────────
cat("\n── Key estimates ──\n")
cat("Pre-trend (per month):     ", round(coef(model)["time"], 3), "\n")
cat("Level change at month 37:  ", round(coef(model)["post"], 3), "\n")
cat("Slope change after month 37:", round(coef(model)["time_since"], 3), "\n")

# ── 4. Build counterfactual ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
df_cf <- df %>%
  mutate(
    time_since_cf = 0,   # counterfactual: intervention never happened
    post_cf       = 0
  )

df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    fitted      = fitted(model),
    counterfactual = predict(model, newdata = rename(df_cf,
                               time_since = time_since_cf,
                               post = post_cf)),
    point_effect  = outcome - counterfactual
  )

# ── 5. Plot ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ggplot(df, aes(x = time)) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 36.5, linetype = "dashed", colour = "grey50") +
  geom_line(aes(y = outcome),       colour = "black",  linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_line(aes(y = fitted),        colour = "steelblue", linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_line(aes(y = counterfactual), colour = "firebrick",
            linetype = "dotted", linewidth = 0.8) +
  annotate("text", x = 37.5, y = max(df$outcome),
           label = "Intervention", hjust = 0, size = 3.5, colour = "grey40") +
  labs(title    = "Interrupted Time Series",
       subtitle = "Blue = fitted  |  Red dotted = counterfactual",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 6. Pre-period residual diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────
pre_resid <- residuals(model)[df$post == 0]

cat("\nPre-period MAPE: ",
    round(mean(abs(pre_resid / df$outcome[df$post == 0])) * 100, 2), "%\n")

acf(pre_resid,  main = "ACF of Pre-Period Residuals")
Box.test(pre_resid, lag = 10, type = "Ljung-Box")
# p > 0.05 → residuals are white noise ✓

# ── 7. Cumulative effect ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cumulative_effect <- sum(df$point_effect[df$post == 1])
cat("\nCumulative effect (months 37–60):", round(cumulative_effect, 2), "\n")
```

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two checks I recommend:

**① Placebo date** — run the same analysis as if the intervention happened at month 20 (mid-pre-period). You should find no effect.

```r
df_placebo <- df %>%
  mutate(
    post_p       = if_else(time >= 20, 1L, 0L),
    time_since_p = if_else(time >= 20, time - 19, 0)
  ) %>%
  filter(post == 0)  # pre-period only

model_p <- lm(outcome ~ time + post_p + time_since_p, data = df_placebo)
coeftest(model_p, vcov = NeweyWest(model_p, lag = 4))

# If post_p is significant → the model is picking up noise, not the policy
```

**② Pre-period prediction hold-out** — fit on months 1–24, predict months 25–36, check accuracy.

```r
train  <- df %>% filter(time <= 24)
holdout <- df %>% filter(time > 24, post == 0) %>%
             mutate(time_since = 0, post = 0)

model_train <- lm(outcome ~ time + post + time_since, data = train)
preds       <- predict(model_train, newdata = holdout)
holdout_mape <- mean(abs((holdout$outcome - preds) / holdout$outcome)) * 100

cat("Holdout MAPE (months 25–36):", round(holdout_mape, 2), "%\n")
# < 5% → model generalizes well ✓
```

### timeseries_structural_break

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1 — Setup

Good setup. Here's what I'm working with:

| | |
|---|---|
| **Pre-treatment period** | Months 1–36 (36 obs) |
| **Post-treatment period** | Months 37–60 (24 obs) |
| **Control series** | None provided |
| **Method** | Segmented regression (basic ITS) — appropriate without controls |
| **Language** | Python |

One quick question before assumptions: **do you have any control series** — metrics that follow similar trends but were *not* affected by the policy? Even one good control series would let us upgrade to CausalImpact (Bayesian structural time series) and greatly strengthen identification. If not, segmented regression is the right call.

---

## Stage 2 — Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific setup.

---

### ① No concurrent confounding events ⚠️ *Not testable — must be argued*

> **What it means for you**: Did anything else change at month 37 that could have shifted your outcome? A budget change, a macro shock, a seasonal event, a data collection change? This is the single biggest threat to ITS.

**→ Question for you**: Were there any other events or changes around month 37 that could plausibly affect `outcome`?

This assumption cannot be tested statistically — you have to argue it from domain knowledge. We'll add a placebo test in Stage 4 to build supporting evidence.

---

### ② Pre-treatment model fit *Testable*

> **What it means for you**: The segmented regression model must capture your pre-period trend well. If it can't explain months 1–36, it can't credibly project the counterfactual into months 37–60.

We'll formally check MAPE and residual autocorrelation in Stage 3. A good rule of thumb: MAPE < 5% is strong, < 10% is acceptable.

---

### ③ Structural break in pre-period ⚠️ *Check before proceeding*

> **What it means for you**: If something happened during months 1–36 that caused a level shift or trend change *before* the intervention, the model will be fit on two different regimes and the counterfactual projection will be off. Per the lesson learned: always check this first.

We'll include a CUSUM plot and Chow test in Stage 3.

---

### ④ Stationarity (or proper differencing) *Testable*

> **What it means for you**: Is your outcome series mean-reverting around a stable trend, or is it a random walk? A trending series can produce a spurious "treatment effect" where the post-period just continues a pre-existing drift. We'll run ADF and KPSS tests.

---

### ⑤ Adequate pre-period length ⚠️ *Borderline*

> **What it means for you**: You have 36 pre-treatment months. For monthly data with annual seasonality, the guideline is ≥ 3 full seasonal cycles = 36 months. You're right at the minimum. **Does your outcome have clear annual seasonality?** If yes, the model may struggle to separate seasonal patterns from trend.

---

## Stage 3 — Implementation

Here's complete Python code covering the full analysis:

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.patches as mpatches
from statsmodels.formula.api import ols
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from statsmodels.tsa.stattools import adfuller, kpss
from statsmodels.graphics.tsaplots import plot_acf
from scipy.stats import shapiro

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Replace this with pd.read_csv(...) for your actual data
df = pd.DataFrame({
    'time': range(1, 61),
    'outcome': [...],   # your 60 values
    'post': [0]*36 + [1]*24
})

INTERVENTION = 37

# ── 1. Build ITS variables ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['time'] - (INTERVENTION - 1))
# time_since = 0 in pre-period, counts up from 1 in post-period

# ── 2. Check stationarity on pre-period ──────────────────────────────────────
pre = df[df['post'] == 0]['outcome'].values

adf_stat, adf_p, *_ = adfuller(pre, autolag='AIC')
kpss_stat, kpss_p, *_ = kpss(pre, regression='c', nlags='auto')

print("=== Stationarity Tests (pre-period) ===")
print(f"ADF  p = {adf_p:.3f}  {'✓ stationary' if adf_p < 0.05 else '⚠ possible unit root'}")
print(f"KPSS p = {kpss_p:.3f}  {'✓ stationary' if kpss_p > 0.05 else '⚠ non-stationary'}")

# ── 3. Structural break check (CUSUM) ────────────────────────────────────────
# Visual CUSUM on pre-period residuals
pre_df = df[df['post'] == 0].copy()
pre_model = ols('outcome ~ time', data=pre_df).fit()
pre_resid = pre_model.resid
cusum = pre_resid.cumsum()

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(10, 4))
ax.plot(pre_df['time'].values, cusum, color='steelblue', linewidth=2)
ax.axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8)
n = len(cusum)
sigma = pre_resid.std()
boundary = 0.948 * sigma * np.sqrt(n)  # 5% critical boundary (approx)
ax.axhline(boundary, color='red', linestyle='--', alpha=0.7, label='5% boundary')
ax.axhline(-boundary, color='red', linestyle='--', alpha=0.7)
ax.set_title('CUSUM Test — Pre-Period Structural Break Check')
ax.set_xlabel('Month')
ax.set_ylabel('Cumulative Residual')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('cusum_preperiod.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("⚠ If CUSUM crosses the red boundaries, flag a structural break before proceeding.")

# ── 4. Fit segmented regression ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# Model: level change (post) + slope change (time_since) at intervention
model = ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}  # HAC SEs for autocorrelation
)
print("\n=== Segmented Regression Results ===")
print(model.summary())

level_change = model.params['post']
slope_change = model.params['time_since']
print(f"\nLevel change at intervention:  {level_change:+.3f} (SE={model.bse['post']:.3f})")
print(f"Slope change per month after:  {slope_change:+.3f} (SE={model.bse['time_since']:.3f})")

# ── 5. Pre-period model fit diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────
pre_obs    = df.loc[df['post'] == 0, 'outcome']
pre_fitted = model.fittedvalues[df['post'] == 0]
pre_resid2 = (pre_obs - pre_fitted).values

mape = np.mean(np.abs(pre_resid2 / pre_obs.values)) * 100
print(f"\n=== Pre-Period Fit ===")
print(f"MAPE: {mape:.2f}%  {'✓ good' if mape < 5 else ('~ acceptable' if mape < 10 else '⚠ poor fit')}")

lb = acorr_ljungbox(pre_resid2, lags=10, return_df=True)
print("\nLjung-Box test (autocorrelation in pre-period residuals):")
print(lb[['lb_stat', 'lb_pvalue']].round(3))
print("→ p > 0.05 at all lags = residuals are white noise (good)")

sw_stat, sw_p = shapiro(pre_resid2)
print(f"\nShapiro-Wilk normality test: p = {sw_p:.3f}  "
      f"{'✓ normal' if sw_p > 0.05 else '⚠ non-normal residuals'}")

# ── 6. Counterfactual plot ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Counterfactual: what would have happened without intervention?
df_counter = df.copy()
df_counter['post'] = 0
df_counter['time_since'] = 0
df['counterfactual'] = model.predict(df_counter)
df['fitted'] = model.fittedvalues

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))
ax.plot(df['time'], df['outcome'], 'o', color='steelblue', markersize=4, label='Observed')
ax.plot(df['time'], df['fitted'], color='steelblue', linewidth=2, label='Model fit')
ax.plot(df.loc[df['post']==1, 'time'],
        df.loc[df['post']==1, 'counterfactual'],
        '--', color='gray', linewidth=2, label='Counterfactual (no intervention)')
ax.axvline(INTERVENTION, color='red', linestyle=':', linewidth=1.5)
ax.text(INTERVENTION + 0.3, ax.get_ylim()[0], 'Intervention', color='red', fontsize=9)
ax.fill_between(
    df.loc[df['post']==1, 'time'],
    df.loc[df['post']==1, 'counterfactual'],
    df.loc[df['post']==1, 'fitted'],
    alpha=0.15, color='green', label='Estimated effect'
)
ax.set_xlabel('Month')
ax.set_ylabel('Outcome')
ax.set_title('Interrupted Time Series — Observed vs Counterfactual')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('its_counterfactual.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 7. Cumulative effect ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
post_df = df[df['post'] == 1].copy()
pointwise_effect = post_df['fitted'] - post_df['counterfactual']
cumulative_effect = pointwise_effect.sum()
print(f"\n=== Effect Estimates ===")
print(f"Avg per-month effect (post-period): {pointwise_effect.mean():+.3f}")
print(f"Cumulative effect over {len(post_df)} months: {cumulative_effect:+.3f}")
```

---

## Stage 4 — Falsification

Two checks I recommend for your setup:

**① Placebo intervention date** — Run the same analysis on a fake treatment date (e.g., month 18, the midpoint of your pre-period). If a "significant effect" appears there too, the model is picking up noise or a pre-existing structural break, not your intervention.

```python
# Placebo at month 18
PLACEBO = 18
df_p = df[df['time'] < INTERVENTION].copy()
df_p['post_placebo'] = (df_p['time'] >= PLACEBO).astype(int)
df_p['time_since_placebo'] = np.maximum(0, df_p['time'] - (PLACEBO - 1))

m_placebo = ols('outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo',
                data=df_p).fit(cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})

print(f"Placebo level change at month {PLACEBO}: "
      f"{m_placebo.params['post_placebo']:+.3f}, "
      f"p = {m_placebo.pvalues['post_placebo']:.3f}")
print("→ Want p > 0.10 here (no spurious effect at the fake date)")
```

**② Pre-period prediction holdout** — Train the model on months 1–24, predict months 25–36 (the "holdout" pre-period). If predictions are poor, the model is unreliable for projecting the counterfactual.

```python
train = df[df['time'] <= 24].copy()
holdout = df[(df['time'] > 24) & (df['time'] < INTERVENTION)].copy()

m_train = ols('outcome ~ time', data=train).fit()
holdout['predicted'] = m_train.predict(holdout)
holdout_mape = np.mean(np.abs(
    (holdout['outcome'] - holdout['predicted']) / holdout['outcome']
)) * 100

print(f"Holdout MAPE (months 25–36): {holdout_mape:.2f}%")
print("→ If high (>15%), the pre-period trend is too unstable to trust the counterfactual")
```

---

## Stage 5 — Interpreting Results

Once you run the code, the key numbers to report are:

> *"The interrupted time series analysis estimates an immediate level change of **[`post` coefficient]** units at the intervention (95% CI: [lower, upper], p = [...]), and a slope change of **[`time_since` coefficient]** units per month thereafter. Over the 24-month post-period, the estimated cumulative effect is **[cumulative effect]** units. Pre-period model fit was **[MAPE]%**."*

**Report alongside**:
- Whether the CUSUM was clean (no structural breaks)
- Whether the placebo test was null (supporting validity)
- Any known concurrent events at month 37 (the biggest caveat)

### did_basic_2x2

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 4.8284, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.3333333333333333, "must_include_results": {"parallel_trends": true, "confidence_interval": false, "clustered_standard_errors": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Classic 2×2 DiD — Single Treatment Date

Since all treated stores receive the display at the same time (month 6), this is a clean **single-cohort TWFE** setup — no staggered adoption bias to worry about.

**Key design choice**: `treated` (never changes within a store) and `post` (same for all stores at a given month) are both absorbed by the fixed effects. Only the interaction `treated_post` varies across both dimensions, so that's the only regressor needed.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS

# ── 1. Feature engineering ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df['treated']      = (df['store'] <= 25).astype(int)   # stores 1-25 treated
df['post']         = (df['month'] >= 6).astype(int)    # month 6 onward
df['treated_post'] = df['treated'] * df['post']        # DiD interaction

# ── 2. Panel index ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# linearmodels requires a MultiIndex (unit, time)
df_panel = df.set_index(['store', 'month'])

# ── 3. TWFE estimation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# 'treated'  → absorbed by EntityEffects (time-invariant per store)
# 'post'     → absorbed by TimeEffects   (unit-invariant per month)
# 'treated_post' → only term that varies along both dimensions ✓
mod = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    'sales ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects',
    data=df_panel
)
res = mod.fit(cov_type='clustered', cluster_entity=True)   # cluster by store

# ── 4. Extract results ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
att        = res.params['treated_post']
ci_lower   = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'lower']
ci_upper   = res.conf_int().loc['treated_post', 'upper']
pval       = res.pvalues['treated_post']

# ── 5. Report ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(res.summary)
print("\n── DiD Treatment Effect ──────────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  ATT  : {att:+.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI: [{ci_lower:.4f}, {ci_upper:.4f}]")
print(f"  p-value: {pval:.4f}")
print(f"  Interpretation: stores with the new display sold {att:+.2f} units/month")
print(f"                  more than controls, on average, after month 6.")

print(f"\nESTIMATE:{att:.4f}")
```

---

### What each piece does

| Component | Purpose |
|---|---|
| `EntityEffects` | Absorbs all time-invariant store characteristics (baseline sales level, location, size) |
| `TimeEffects` | Absorbs all month-level shocks common to all stores (seasonality, promotions) |
| `treated_post` | Isolates the DiD contrast — the *extra* change in treated stores post-treatment |
| `cluster_entity=True` | Clusters SEs at the store level to handle within-store serial correlation |

---

### Recommended next step

Before trusting this estimate, validate the **parallel trends assumption** — were treated and control stores trending similarly in months 1–5?

```python
# Quick visual pre-trends check
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

pre = df[df['month'] < 6].copy()
trends = pre.groupby(['month', 'treated'])['sales'].mean().unstack()
trends.columns = ['Control (stores 26-50)', 'Treated (stores 1-25)']
trends.plot(marker='o', title='Pre-Treatment Trends (months 1–5)')
plt.axvline(x=5.5, color='gray', linestyle='--', label='Treatment onset')
plt.ylabel('Mean Sales')
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
```

If the lines run roughly parallel in the pre-period, the identifying assumption holds. Want me to run a full **event study** with formal pre-trend coefficients and a joint significance test?

## 🏪 Scenario: Retail Store Layout Redesign

You're a data analyst at **RetailCo**, a chain with 100 stores across the country. In **Month 13** of a 24-month observation window, the operations team rolled out a **new store layout** in 50 randomly selected stores. The remaining 50 stores kept the original layout throughout.

Your manager wants to know: **Did the new layout increase monthly revenue?**

You have access to a panel dataset with monthly revenue figures for all 100 stores over 24 months. Your job is to estimate the causal effect of the new layout on revenue.

---

## 🐍 DGP Code — Save and run this to generate your data

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(303)

n_stores = 100
n_months = 24
treat_month = 13

store_id = np.repeat(np.arange(1, n_stores + 1), n_months)
month    = np.tile(np.arange(1, n_months + 1), n_stores)

treated = (store_id <= 50).astype(int)
post    = (month >= treat_month).astype(int)

store_fe   = np.repeat(np.random.normal(0, 3, n_stores), n_months)
time_trend = 0.2 * month

true_att = 5.0

revenue = (
    50
    + store_fe
    + time_trend
    + true_att * treated * post
    + np.random.normal(0, 2, n_stores * n_months)
)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "store_id": store_id,
    "month":    month,
    "treated":  treated,
    "post":     post,
    "revenue":  np.round(revenue, 2),
})

df.to_csv("docs/causal-exercises/2026-04-01-did-basic/data.csv", index=False)

print(df.head(10).to_string())
print(f"\nDataset shape: {df.shape}")
print(f"Treated stores: {df[df.treated==1].store_id.nunique()}")
print(f"Control stores: {df[df.treated==0].store_id.nunique()}")
print(f"Months observed: {df.month.nunique()}")
print(f"Treatment starts at month: {treat_month}")
print(f"\nESTIMATE:{true_att}")
```

Here's a clean IV exercise built from a realistic DGP with a known ground truth.

---

## 🏥 Scenario: Wellness Program & Medical Spending

You work as a data analyst at a large health insurer. The company has been sending **encouragement letters** to randomly selected members, urging them to enroll in a voluntary wellness program. Leadership wants to know: **does enrolling in the wellness program actually reduce annual medical spending?**

The catch: enrollment is voluntary, so members who sign up are likely healthier and more motivated to begin with — a classic endogeneity problem. A simple comparison of enrollees vs. non-enrollees will be confounded by selection.

You have access to the dataset below. Your goal is to estimate the **causal effect of enrollment on spending**.

**Dataset columns:**
| Column | Description |
|--------|-------------|
| `id` | Member ID |
| `encouraged` | Whether the member received an encouragement letter (0/1) |
| `enrolled` | Whether the member enrolled in the wellness program (0/1) |
| `spending` | Annual medical spending (in $thousands) |

---

## 🐍 Python DGP Code

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(505)
n = 3000

# ── Instrument ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Encouragement letters are randomly assigned — this is your instrument Z.
# It's random, so it's independent of everything unobserved.
Z = np.random.binomial(1, 0.5, n)

# ── Unobserved confounder ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Health motivation: higher = more motivated to be healthy.
# This is NOT in the dataset. It drives both enrollment AND spending.
U = np.random.normal(0, 1, n)

# ── Treatment: enrollment in wellness program ────────────────────────────────
# The letter strongly predicts enrollment (F-stat well above 10),
# but motivation also makes people more likely to enroll.
def logistic(x):
    return 1 / (1 + np.exp(-x))

p_enroll = logistic(-1.0 + 1.5 * Z + 1.0 * U)
D = np.random.binomial(1, p_enroll)

# ── Outcome: medical spending (thousands of dollars) ────────────────────────
# True causal effect of enrollment on spending: -10 (reduces spending by $10k)
# Motivation also reduces spending independently — that's the confounder.
true_late = -10.0
Y = 50 - 10 * D - 8 * U + np.random.normal(0, 10, n)

# ── Save dataset ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "id":         np.arange(1, n + 1),
    "encouraged": Z,
    "enrolled":   D,
    "spending":   np.round(Y, 2),
})
df.to_csv("wellness_iv.csv", index=False)

print(f"Dataset saved: {df.shape[0]} rows × {df.shape[1]} cols")
print(f"Enrollment rate (control): {D[Z==0].mean():.1%}")
print(f"Enrollment rate (encouraged): {D[Z==1].mean():.1%}")
print(f"ESTIMATE:{true_late}")
```

---

## 🎯 Your Task

1. **Start with OLS** — regress `spending` on `enrolled`. Notice the direction and magnitude of the bias. Which way does it go, and why?

2. **Run 2SLS** using `encouraged` as the instrument for `enrolled`. Use `linearmodels.iv.IV2SLS` or manually with two regressions:
   - **First stage**: regress `enrolled` on `encouraged`, get fitted values `D_hat`
   - **Second stage**: regress `spending` on `D_hat`

3. **Check your first stage** — what's the F-statistic? Is the instrument strong?

4. **Compare** your 2SLS estimate to the true LATE printed by the script. How close did you get?

> 💡 **Think about this:** The true effect is `-10` (for *compliers*). OLS will likely give you a very different number. Why? And why does IV specifically recover the effect for compliers rather than the full population?

Good luck — the answer is in the printout when you're ready to check! 🎲

</details>

### experiments_simple

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 0.0312, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"confidence_interval": false, "balance_check": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

You're at the **analysis stage** with individual-level randomization, binary outcome (conversion), and ~10,000 users total. No clustering, no covariates beyond what's shown — so we'll run a **difference-in-means** as the primary estimator and back it up with OLS for clean SE reporting.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Quick Pass

| Assumption | Status | Note |
|---|---|---|
| **Random assignment** | ✅ Assumed | No covariates to balance-test beyond treatment itself |
| **SUTVA** | ✅ Plausible | Individual checkout flows; minimal spillover risk |
| **No differential attrition** | ✅ Assumed | 5k/5k even split suggests no dropout issue |
| **Compliance** | ✅ Assumed | `treated` column appears to be actual receipt |

No fatal violations flagged. Proceeding to implementation.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from scipy.stats import ttest_ind
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

# ── 1. Descriptive summary ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
group_stats = (
    df.groupby("treated")["conversion"]
    .agg(n="count", mean="mean", std="std")
    .rename(index={0: "Control", 1: "Treatment"})
)
print("─── Group Summary ───────────────────────────────")
print(group_stats.to_string())

# ── 2. Difference-in-means (ATE) ─────────────────────────────────────────────
treated_conv   = df.loc[df["treated"] == 1, "conversion"]
control_conv   = df.loc[df["treated"] == 0, "conversion"]

ate = treated_conv.mean() - control_conv.mean()

t_stat, p_value = ttest_ind(treated_conv, control_conv, equal_var=False)

# 95% CI via delta method (Welch)
se = np.sqrt(treated_conv.var() / len(treated_conv) +
             control_conv.var() / len(control_conv))
ci_lo, ci_hi = ate - 1.96 * se, ate + 1.96 * se

print(f"\n─── Difference-in-Means ─────────────────────────")
print(f"Control conversion rate  : {control_conv.mean():.4f}")
print(f"Treatment conversion rate: {treated_conv.mean():.4f}")
print(f"ATE                      : {ate:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI                   : [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"t-stat: {t_stat:.3f}  |  p-value: {p_value:.4f}")

# ── 3. OLS regression (same point estimate, HC3 robust SEs) ──────────────────
model = smf.ols("conversion ~ treated", data=df).fit(cov_type="HC3")

print(f"\n─── OLS (HC3 Robust SEs) ────────────────────────")
print(f"Coefficient on treated: {model.params['treated']:.4f}")
print(f"Std error             : {model.bse['treated']:.4f}")
print(f"p-value               : {model.pvalues['treated']:.4f}")

# ── 4. Effect size interpretation ────────────────────────────────────────────
relative_lift = ate / control_conv.mean() * 100
print(f"\n─── Interpretation ──────────────────────────────")
print(f"Absolute lift  : {ate*100:+.2f} percentage points")
print(f"Relative lift  : {relative_lift:+.2f}% over control baseline")
sig = "statistically significant" if p_value < 0.05 else "NOT statistically significant"
print(f"Result is {sig} at α = 0.05")

# ── Primary output (required format) ─────────────────────────────────────────
print(f"ESTIMATE:{ate:.4f}")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Check

Since there are no additional covariates, the cleanest falsification here is a **permutation test** — if the observed ATE is real, it should sit in the tail of a null distribution of randomly reshuffled labels:

```python
np.random.seed(42)
n_perms = 5000
perm_ates = np.array([
    df["conversion"].sample(frac=1).values[:5000].mean() -
    df["conversion"].sample(frac=1).values[5000:].mean()
    for _ in range(n_perms)
])

perm_p = np.mean(np.abs(perm_ates) >= np.abs(ate))
print(f"Permutation p-value: {perm_p:.4f}  (observed ATE = {ate:.4f})")
```

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

> The new checkout flow **[increased / decreased]** conversion by **X.XX percentage points** (95% CI: [lo, hi]), a **Y%** relative change over the control baseline of **Z%**. The result is **[significant / not significant]** at α = 0.05 (p = …).

Run the code with your actual `df` and the numbers will fill in. Want me to extend this with subgroup heterogeneity (HTE) or help write up the findings?

### iv_strong_instrument

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": -5581.812588667875, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.5, "must_include_results": {"first_stage": true, "confidence_interval": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

```python
import numpy as np
from linearmodels.iv import IV2SLS
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

# ── Instrument: binary indicator (draft_number > 50) ──────────────────────────
df = df.copy()
df['eligible'] = (df['draft_number'] > 50).astype(int)

# ── Stage 1: First stage — check instrument relevance ─────────────────────────
first_stage = smf.ols('served ~ eligible', data=df).fit()
print("=== First Stage ===")
print(first_stage.summary())
print(f"First-stage F-statistic: {first_stage.fvalue:.2f}")
# Rule of thumb: F > 10 (Stock-Yogo); ideally > 100 (Lee et al. 2022)

# ── Stage 2: 2SLS estimation ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv_model = IV2SLS.from_formula(
    'earnings ~ 1 + [served ~ eligible]', data=df
).fit(cov_type='robust')

print("\n=== 2SLS (IV) Estimation ===")
print(iv_model.summary)

# ── Naive OLS for comparison (shows direction of endogeneity bias) ─────────────
ols_model = smf.ols('earnings ~ served', data=df).fit()
print("\n=== Naive OLS (for comparison) ===")
ols_est = ols_model.params['served']
print(f"OLS estimate (served → earnings): {ols_est:.4f}")

# ── Extract and report the LATE ───────────────────────────────────────────────
late = iv_model.params['served']
ci_low, ci_high = iv_model.conf_int().loc['served']

print(f"\n=== IV / 2SLS Summary ===")
print(f"LATE (complier avg treatment effect): {late:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI: [{ci_low:.4f}, {ci_high:.4f}]")
print(f"OLS estimate:  {ols_est:.4f}  (difference = {late - ols_est:.4f})")

print(f"\nINTERPRETATION: Among compliers (men whose military service was")
print(f"determined by draft eligibility), serving in the military changed")
print(f"earnings by ${late:,.2f} on average.")

print(f'ESTIMATE:{late}')
```

---

**What this code does, and why:**

| Step | Why it matters |
|---|---|
| `eligible = draft_number > 50` | Converts the continuous draft number into the binary instrument. The lottery creates as-if-random variation in who was eligible. |
| **First stage** | Checks *relevance* — the one testable IV assumption. F >> 10 confirms the instrument actually shifts treatment take-up. |
| **2SLS via `linearmodels`** | Isolates only the exogenous variation in `served` (the part driven by lottery eligibility) and uses that to estimate the earnings effect. |
| **OLS comparison** | OLS is likely biased (e.g., men who volunteer may differ systematically from those who don't). The gap between IV and OLS reveals the direction and magnitude of that selection bias. |

**The estimate is a LATE** — it applies specifically to **compliers**: men who served *because* their draft number was above 50, and would not have served otherwise. It does **not** capture the effect for always-takers (who would serve regardless) or never-takers.

</details>

---

### matching_ate

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 4.991697869274336, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 1.0, "must_include_results": {"propensity_score": true}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## IPW Analysis — ATE Estimation (Python)

**Quick assumption check before code:**
- **Conditional independence**: Treatment assigned based on age, income, and education — all three are measured and included. ✅ (assuming no other unmeasured drivers)
- **Overlap**: Will verify via propensity score distribution
- **SUTVA**: 500 independent individuals — plausible ✅

---

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 1. Estimate propensity scores ─────────────────────────────────────────────
covariates = ['age', 'income', 'education']
X = df[covariates].values
D = df['treatment'].values

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(X, D)
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X)[:, 1]

# ── 2. Overlap check (flag before proceeding) ─────────────────────────────────
print("=== Propensity Score Overlap ===")
print(f"Treated  PS range: [{df.loc[df['treatment']==1,'ps'].min():.3f}, "
      f"{df.loc[df['treatment']==1,'ps'].max():.3f}]")
print(f"Control  PS range: [{df.loc[df['treatment']==0,'ps'].min():.3f}, "
      f"{df.loc[df['treatment']==0,'ps'].max():.3f}]")
print(f"Fraction with PS < 0.05 : {np.mean(df['ps'] < 0.05):.3f}")
print(f"Fraction with PS > 0.95 : {np.mean(df['ps'] > 0.95):.3f}")

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 4))
ax.hist(df.loc[df['treatment']==1, 'ps'], bins=40, alpha=0.5, label='Treated', density=True)
ax.hist(df.loc[df['treatment']==0, 'ps'], bins=40, alpha=0.5, label='Control', density=True)
ax.set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
ax.set_ylabel('Density')
ax.set_title('Propensity Score Overlap — Treated vs Control')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('ps_overlap.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("Overlap plot saved → ps_overlap.png")

# ── 3. ATE weights: 1/ps for treated, 1/(1-ps) for control ───────────────────
df['ipw_weight'] = np.where(
    df['treatment'] == 1,
    1 / df['ps'],
    1 / (1 - df['ps'])
)

# Stabilized weights (reduces variance from extreme weights)
p_treat = D.mean()
df['ipw_weight_stab'] = np.where(
    df['treatment'] == 1,
    p_treat / df['ps'],
    (1 - p_treat) / (1 - df['ps'])
)

print("\n=== Weight Diagnostics ===")
print(f"Max unstabilized weight : {df['ipw_weight'].max():.2f}")
print(f"Max stabilized weight   : {df['ipw_weight_stab'].max():.2f}")
print(f"Effective sample size   : {(df['ipw_weight_stab'].sum()**2 / (df['ipw_weight_stab']**2).sum()):.1f}")

# ── 4. Covariate balance check (SMD before vs after weighting) ────────────────
def smd_weighted(cov, df, weight_col=None):
    t = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, cov]
    c = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, cov]
    pooled_sd = np.sqrt((t.var() + c.var()) / 2)
    if weight_col:
        w = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, weight_col]
        m_c = np.average(c, weights=w)
    else:
        m_c = c.mean()
    return (t.mean() - m_c) / pooled_sd

print("\n=== Covariate Balance (SMD) ===")
print(f"{'Covariate':<12} {'Before':>8} {'After (stab)':>14}  {'Balanced?':>10}")
for cov in covariates:
    before = smd_weighted(cov, df)
    after  = smd_weighted(cov, df, weight_col='ipw_weight_stab')
    flag   = "✅" if abs(after) < 0.1 else "⚠️  >0.1"
    print(f"  {cov:<10} {before:>8.3f} {after:>14.3f}  {flag:>10}")

# ── 5. ATE estimate via weighted regression ───────────────────────────────────
model_ipw = smf.wls('outcome ~ treatment', data=df, weights=df['ipw_weight_stab']).fit()

ate = model_ipw.params['treatment']
ci_lo, ci_hi = model_ipw.conf_int().loc['treatment']

print("\n=== ATE Estimate (IPW) ===")
print(f"ATE  : {ate:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI: [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
print(f"p-value: {model_ipw.pvalues['treatment']:.4f}")

# ── Required output ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print(f"ESTIMATE:{ate}")
```

---

### What this code does

| Step | Purpose |
|---|---|
| **Logistic regression** | Estimates P(treatment=1 \| age, income, education) |
| **Stabilized ATE weights** | `p(T)/ps` for treated, `p(C)/(1-ps)` for control — reduces variance vs raw IPW |
| **SMD balance check** | Verifies weighting removed covariate imbalance (target: SMD < 0.1) |
| **WLS regression** | Applies IPW weights to estimate the ATE |

### Critical caveats

> ⚠️ **Unconfoundedness**: The ATE is credible only if age, income, and education fully capture why treatment was assigned. If any additional driver (e.g., employment status, geography) is unmeasured, this estimate is **biased** — and there's no statistical test that can detect it.

> ⚠️ **Extreme weights**: Check `Max stabilized weight` in the output. Values >20 indicate poor overlap and warrant propensity score trimming or switching to a doubly-robust estimator.

</details>

---

### rdd_sharp

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Here's the complete sharp RDD analysis following the template, adapted to your data:

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)

cutoff <- 70

# ── Data prep ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    running_centered = test_score - cutoff,
    treated = as.integer(test_score >= cutoff)
  )

df %>%
  summarise(
    n_below       = sum(test_score < cutoff),
    n_above       = sum(test_score >= cutoff),
    mean_gpa_below = mean(gpa[test_score < cutoff],  na.rm = TRUE),
    mean_gpa_above = mean(gpa[test_score >= cutoff], na.rm = TRUE)
  ) %>%
  print()

# ── RD Plot (visual inspection) ─────────────────────────────────────────────
rdplot(
  y       = df$gpa,
  x       = df$test_score,
  c       = cutoff,
  title   = "RD Plot: Scholarship → College GPA",
  x.label = "Test Score",
  y.label = "College GPA"
)

# ── McCrary density test (manipulation check) ────────────────────────────────
# H0: density is continuous at the cutoff — no sorting
density_test <- rddensity(X = df$test_score, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

# ── Main RD estimate ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Local linear regression with MSE-optimal bandwidth & robust bias-corrected CI
rd_est <- rdrobust(
  y = df$gpa,
  x = df$test_score,
  c = cutoff
)

summary(rd_est)

# Extract and print the robust estimate (required output)
rd_estimate <- rd_est$coef["Robust", 1]
cat("ESTIMATE:", rd_estimate, "\n")

cat("Bandwidth (h):",      rd_est$bws["h", 1],  "\n")
cat("Effective N left:",   rd_est$N_h[1],        "\n")
cat("Effective N right:",  rd_est$N_h[2],        "\n")
cat("95% Robust CI: [",
    rd_est$ci["Robust", 1], ",",
    rd_est$ci["Robust", 2], "]\n")

# ── Bandwidth sensitivity ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
opt_bw     <- rd_est$bws["h", 1]
bandwidths <- opt_bw * c(0.50, 0.75, 1.00, 1.25, 1.50)

bw_results <- map_dfr(bandwidths, function(h) {
  fit <- rdrobust(y = df$gpa, x = df$test_score, c = cutoff, h = h)
  tibble(
    bandwidth = h,
    estimate  = fit$coef["Conventional", 1],
    ci_lower  = fit$ci["Robust", 1],
    ci_upper  = fit$ci["Robust", 2]
  )
})

print(bw_results)

ggplot(bw_results, aes(x = bandwidth, y = estimate)) +
  geom_point() +
  geom_errorbar(aes(ymin = ci_lower, ymax = ci_upper), width = 0.3) +
  geom_hline(yintercept = 0, linetype = "dashed", color = "grey50") +
  labs(
    title = "Bandwidth Sensitivity Analysis",
    x     = "Bandwidth",
    y     = "RD Estimate (effect on GPA)"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── Results table ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
rd_table <- tibble(
  Term              = "RD Estimate (Scholarship → GPA)",
  Conventional      = rd_est$coef["Conventional",   1],
  `Bias-Corrected`  = rd_est$coef["Bias-Corrected", 1],
  Robust            = rd_est$coef["Robust",          1],
  `SE (Robust)`     = rd_est$se["Robust",            1],
  `p-value`         = rd_est$pv["Robust",            1],
  `CI Lower`        = rd_est$ci["Robust",            1],
  `CI Upper`        = rd_est$ci["Robust",            2],
  `BW (h)`          = rd_est$bws["h",               1],
  `N Left`          = rd_est$N_h[1],
  `N Right`         = rd_est$N_h[2]
)

knitr::kable(rd_table, digits = 3, caption = "Sharp RDD — Effect of Scholarship on GPA")
```

---

### What each block does and why

| Block | Purpose |
|---|---|
| **Data prep** | Centers the running variable at 70 — not strictly required by `rdrobust`, but useful for understanding the local neighborhood |
| **RD plot** | First sanity check — visualizes whether a jump at the cutoff is actually visible in the raw data |
| **McCrary density test** | Checks if students can precisely sort around the 70-point threshold (e.g., teachers inflating scores). A significant p-value here is a red flag |
| **`rdrobust` main estimate** | Fits a local linear polynomial on each side of the cutoff with MSE-optimal bandwidth selection and heteroskedasticity-robust, bias-corrected inference — the current gold standard for RDD |
| **Bandwidth sensitivity** | Verifies the estimate is stable at ±50% of the optimal bandwidth — a key robustness check since the estimate can be sensitive to this choice |

The key output line prints as `ESTIMATE: <value>` using the **robust bias-corrected** coefficient, which is what you should report — it corrects for the smoothing bias introduced by local polynomial fitting.

</details>
