### sc_basic

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 0.8066470088691645, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"pre_treatment_fit": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Synthetic Control — Pure NumPy/Pandas/SciPy

Here's a complete implementation using `scipy.optimize.minimize` with SLSQP to solve the constrained weight problem.

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
from scipy.optimize import minimize

# ── 1. Setup ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
TREATED_UNIT   = 1
TREATMENT_TIME = 21

donors = sorted(df[df["unit"] != TREATED_UNIT]["unit"].unique())

pre  = df[df["time"] < TREATMENT_TIME].copy()
post = df[df["time"] >= TREATMENT_TIME].copy()

# Pre-treatment outcome vectors / matrices
Y_pre_treated = (
    pre[pre["unit"] == TREATED_UNIT]
    .sort_values("time")["outcome"].values
)                                                    # shape: (T0,)

Y_pre_donors = np.column_stack([
    pre[pre["unit"] == u].sort_values("time")["outcome"].values
    for u in donors
])                                                   # shape: (T0, J)

# ── 2. Constrained optimisation ────────────────────────────────────────────────
#   minimise  ||Y_treated_pre  -  Y_donors_pre @ w||²
#   s.t.      sum(w) = 1,   w_j >= 0   (simplex constraint)
J  = len(donors)
w0 = np.ones(J) / J                                  # equal-weight starting point

def objective(w):
    residuals = Y_pre_treated - Y_pre_donors @ w
    return np.mean(residuals ** 2)                   # MSPE

def grad_objective(w):
    residuals = Y_pre_treated - Y_pre_donors @ w
    return (2 / len(residuals)) * (-Y_pre_donors.T @ residuals)

result = minimize(
    objective,
    w0,
    jac=grad_objective,
    method="SLSQP",
    bounds=[(0, 1)] * J,
    constraints=[{"type": "eq", "fun": lambda w: w.sum() - 1}],
    options={"ftol": 1e-12, "maxiter": 2000},
)

if not result.success:
    print(f"[WARNING] Optimiser: {result.message}")

weights = result.x

# ── 3. Donor weight table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
weight_df = (
    pd.DataFrame({"donor_unit": donors, "weight": weights})
    .query("weight > 0.001")
    .sort_values("weight", ascending=False)
)
print("Donor weights (>0.1%):")
print(weight_df.to_string(index=False))

# ── 4. Pre-treatment fit ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Y_pre_synth = Y_pre_donors @ weights
rmspe_pre   = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y_pre_treated - Y_pre_synth) ** 2))
print(f"\nPre-treatment RMSPE : {rmspe_pre:.4f}")
print(f"Outcome std dev (pre): {Y_pre_treated.std():.4f}")
print(f"RMSPE / SD ratio    : {rmspe_pre / Y_pre_treated.std():.4f}  (want < 0.10)")

# ── 5. Post-treatment counterfactual & gap ─────────────────────────────────────
Y_post_treated = (
    post[post["unit"] == TREATED_UNIT]
    .sort_values("time")["outcome"].values
)

Y_post_donors = np.column_stack([
    post[post["unit"] == u].sort_values("time")["outcome"].values
    for u in donors
])

Y_post_synth = Y_post_donors @ weights
gap_series   = Y_post_treated - Y_post_synth       # period-by-period ATT

gap_df = pd.DataFrame({
    "time"     : range(TREATMENT_TIME, TREATMENT_TIME + len(gap_series)),
    "treated"  : Y_post_treated,
    "synthetic": Y_post_synth,
    "gap"      : gap_series,
})
print("\nPost-treatment gaps:")
print(gap_df.to_string(index=False))

# ── 6. Main estimate (average ATT over post-treatment window) ──────────────────
gap_estimate = float(np.mean(gap_series))
print(f"\nAverage ATT (post-treatment): {gap_estimate:.4f}")
print(f"ESTIMATE:{gap_estimate}")

# ── 7. Permutation / in-space placebo p-value ──────────────────────────────────
def rmspe_ratio(Y_unit_pre, Y_donors_pre, Y_unit_post, Y_donors_post):
    """Fit SC for a placebo unit; return post/pre RMSPE ratio."""
    w0_p = np.ones(Y_donors_pre.shape[1]) / Y_donors_pre.shape[1]
    res  = minimize(
        lambda w: np.mean((Y_unit_pre - Y_donors_pre @ w) ** 2),
        w0_p,
        method="SLSQP",
        bounds=[(0, 1)] * Y_donors_pre.shape[1],
        constraints=[{"type": "eq", "fun": lambda w: w.sum() - 1}],
        options={"ftol": 1e-12, "maxiter": 2000},
    )
    w_p         = res.x
    rmspe_pre_p = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y_unit_pre  - Y_donors_pre  @ w_p) ** 2))
    rmspe_pst_p = np.sqrt(np.mean((Y_unit_post - Y_donors_post @ w_p) ** 2))
    return rmspe_pst_p / (rmspe_pre_p + 1e-10)

treated_ratio = np.sqrt(np.mean(gap_series ** 2)) / (rmspe_pre + 1e-10)

ratios = [treated_ratio]
for placebo_unit in donors:
    pool = [u for u in donors if u != placebo_unit] + [TREATED_UNIT]
    pool_sorted = sorted(pool)

    Y_pl_pre  = pre[pre["unit"]  == placebo_unit].sort_values("time")["outcome"].values
    Y_pl_post = post[post["unit"] == placebo_unit].sort_values("time")["outcome"].values

    Y_pool_pre  = np.column_stack([
        pre[pre["unit"]  == u].sort_values("time")["outcome"].values for u in pool_sorted])
    Y_pool_post = np.column_stack([
        post[post["unit"] == u].sort_values("time")["outcome"].values for u in pool_sorted])

    ratios.append(rmspe_ratio(Y_pl_pre, Y_pool_pre, Y_pl_post, Y_pool_post))

ratios      = np.array(ratios)
p_value     = np.mean(ratios >= treated_ratio)      # treated unit's rank in permutation dist.
print(f"\nPermutation p-value (in-space placebo): {p_value:.3f}")
print(f"Treated RMSPE ratio: {treated_ratio:.3f}  |  "
      f"Median placebo ratio: {np.median(ratios[1:]):.3f}")
```

---

### What the code does, step by step

| Stage | What happens | Why it matters |
|---|---|---|
| **Optimisation** | SLSQP finds `w` on the simplex minimising pre-treatment MSPE | Guarantees non-negative, convex weights — the core SC constraint |
| **Pre-fit check** | RMSPE / SD ratio | Ratio < 0.10 means a tight fit; poor fit → untrustworthy counterfactual |
| **Gap series** | `treated − synthetic` each post-period | Directly shows when/how the effect materialises |
| **`ESTIMATE`** | Average gap over all post-treatment periods | The ATT (average treatment effect on the treated) |
| **Placebo test** | Rerun SC on every *donor* as if it were treated | Permutation p-value: if `p < 0.10`, the effect is unusually large |

**Interpreting the estimate**: A positive `ESTIMATE` means the treated unit's outcome rose *above* what its synthetic twin would have predicted — the treatment had a positive effect. A negative value means the treatment pulled it *below* the counterfactual.

</details>

---

### timeseries_causalimpact

**Run 1**: {"has_code": true, "runs_without_error": true, "execution_error": null, "estimate": 3.4312, "estimation_accurate": true, "diagnostic_coverage": 0.0, "must_include_results": {"pre_treatment": false, "confidence_interval": false}}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Interrupted Time Series — Segmented Regression (Python)

Here's the complete implementation. The model estimates three things: the underlying time trend, the **level shift** at the intervention (the causal effect estimate you asked for), and the **slope change** after the intervention.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

# ── 1. Build ITS variables ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
intervention_period = 41

df['time']        = df['period']                                      # time index (1–60)
df['post']        = (df['period'] >= intervention_period).astype(int) # 0 = pre, 1 = post
df['time_since']  = np.maximum(0, df['period'] - (intervention_period - 1))
# time_since = 0 throughout pre-period, then 1, 2, 3 … in the post-period
# This captures any SLOPE CHANGE after intervention, separate from the level shift

# ── 2. Fit segmented regression with HAC standard errors ─────────────────────
# HAC (Newey-West) SEs correct for autocorrelation — important for time series
model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}
)
print(model.summary())

# ── 3. Extract and report the causal effect estimate ─────────────────────────
level_shift  = model.params['post']        # immediate level change at intervention
slope_change = model.params['time_since']  # change in monthly trend after intervention

ci_low  = model.conf_int().loc['post', 0]
ci_high = model.conf_int().loc['post', 1]
p_val   = model.pvalues['post']

print("\n── Effect Estimates ──────────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Level shift  (post coef):  {level_shift:.4f}  [{ci_low:.4f}, {ci_high:.4f}]  p={p_val:.4f}")
print(f"  Slope change (time_since): {slope_change:.4f}")
print(f"  Avg monthly trend pre:     {model.params['time']:.4f}")

print(f'ESTIMATE:{level_shift:.4f}')

# ── 4. Counterfactual plot ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Project what outcomes would have been WITHOUT the intervention (post=0, time_since=0)
df_counter = df.copy()
df_counter['post']       = 0
df_counter['time_since'] = 0
df['counterfactual'] = model.predict(df_counter)
df['fitted']         = model.fittedvalues

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 5))
ax.plot(df['period'], df['outcome'],       'k.-',  label='Observed',        lw=1.5)
ax.plot(df['period'], df['fitted'],        'b-',   label='Model fit',       lw=1.5)
ax.plot(df['period'], df['counterfactual'],'b--',  label='Counterfactual',  lw=1.5)
ax.axvline(intervention_period, color='red', linestyle=':', lw=1.5, label='Intervention')

# Shade the gap between observed and counterfactual in the post-period
post_mask = df['period'] >= intervention_period
ax.fill_between(
    df.loc[post_mask, 'period'],
    df.loc[post_mask, 'counterfactual'],
    df.loc[post_mask, 'outcome'],
    alpha=0.15, color='blue', label='Estimated effect'
)

ax.set_xlabel('Period')
ax.set_ylabel('Outcome')
ax.set_title('Interrupted Time Series — Segmented Regression')
ax.legend()
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('its_segmented_regression.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 5. Residual diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from scipy.stats import shapiro

residuals = model.resid
lb = acorr_ljungbox(residuals, lags=10, return_df=True)
sw_stat, sw_p = shapiro(residuals)

print("\n── Residual Diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Ljung-Box p-values (lags 1–10):\n{lb[['lb_pvalue']].round(3).to_string()}")
print(f"  Shapiro-Wilk normality: stat={sw_stat:.4f}, p={sw_p:.4f}")
if lb['lb_pvalue'].min() < 0.05:
    print("  ⚠  Residual autocorrelation detected — HAC SEs mitigate this, "
          "but consider an ARIMA-based counterfactual for tighter inference.")
else:
    print("  ✓  No significant residual autocorrelation.")
```

---

### What each coefficient means

| Coefficient | Variable | Interpretation |
|---|---|---|
| `time` | Pre-existing trend | Monthly change in outcome *before* intervention |
| **`post`** | **Level shift** | **Immediate jump/drop at period 41 — this is your causal effect** |
| `time_since` | Slope change | Change in monthly growth rate *after* intervention |

**Why HAC standard errors?** Monthly outcomes are almost always autocorrelated — adjacent periods resemble each other. OLS standard errors understate uncertainty in that case. HAC (Newey-West with `maxlags=4`) corrects for this without needing a full ARIMA model.

**Key caveat to keep in mind:** The level-shift estimate is only causal if nothing else changed at period 41. If any concurrent event coincided with the intervention, it's absorbed into `post` and would bias the estimate — there's no statistical fix for that, only domain knowledge.

</details>

---

**Save this as:** `docs/causal-plans/2026-04-01-mentoring-program/audit.md` — copy the full audit above.

**Also append this to `references/lessons.md`:**

```markdown
### Matching: Post-treatment covariate included in propensity score model
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: User includes a covariate measured after treatment start in their propensity model, justified by its predictive power for the outcome
**Mistake**: Method skill failed to flag that including a post-treatment variable conditions on a mediator or collider, biasing the ATT estimate
**Rule**: Always verify covariate timing relative to treatment onset. Any variable measured after treatment assignment must be excluded from the propensity score model. "Strong predictor of the outcome" is not a valid reason to include a variable — only pre-treatment confounders belong in the model.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

The single most important thing: **`performance_rating_q3` has to go**. Everything else in this audit is secondary. The intuition to hold onto —

> A variable earns its place in the propensity score model by being a *pre-treatment confounder*, not by being a good predictor. A great predictor measured after treatment starts is either a mediator (blocking the effect you're trying to measure) or a collider (opening a backdoor that didn't exist before). In either case, conditioning on it breaks the analysis.

</details>

I'd also append this to `references/lessons.md`:

```
### DiD: Outcome measurement endogenous to treatment
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: Treatment is a system/tool that also generates the outcome data (POS, ERP, CRM upgrades)
**Mistake**: Method skill runs standard DiD checks without flagging that the measurement instrument changed with treatment
**Rule**: When treatment is a data collection or recording system, always ask: "Is the outcome variable measured by the thing being treated?" If yes, flag measurement contamination as a potential fatal threat before running any regressions.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

## Bottom line

🔴 **Red — do not report this result without resolving the measurement issue.**

Here's the honest picture:

| Finding | Severity | Direction of bias |
|---|---|---|
| POS system measures its own outcome | Fatal | Upward |
| Anticipation (6-month advance notice) | Fatal | Upward |
| Spillovers (50% saturation) | Serious | Upward |
| IT infrastructure selection | Serious | Unknown |
| Effect size implausibility | Serious | — |

Every major threat pushes the estimate **upward**. That's a pattern that should make you suspicious, not confident. A 12.3% lift is a remarkable claim — right now the most plausible explanation is that your new POS system is capturing transactions your old one missed, not that customers are suddenly buying more.

The single most valuable next step: pull card processor or invoice data and compare it to POS sales for treated stores. If those external signals show ~0% change while your POS shows +12%, you have your answer — and it's not the one you want.

</details>

### did_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.5555555555555555}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — this is a textbook DiD scenario. Single treatment date, clean panel. Let me walk you through all 5 stages.

---

## Stage 1: Design

Here's what you have:

| Feature | Your Study |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New checkout system, activated at month 12 |
| **Units** | 100 stores (50 treated, 50 control) |
| **Outcome** | Sales |
| **Design** | Classic single-date TWFE DiD |

Since every treated store flips at the same month, you don't need a staggered estimator — standard TWFE is the right tool here.

**One note on your data structure**: `treated` is time-invariant (same value for a store across all months). When we add store fixed effects, that column gets absorbed — we can't include it as a standalone regressor. What identifies the effect is the **interaction** `treated × post`: stores that are treated *and* in the post period. This is not a bug — it's how FE DiD works.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let's go through each one for your context before touching any code.

### ① Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Testable — most important*

**What it means for you**: Without the new checkout system, treated and control stores would have had sales trending at the same rate. They can differ in *level* (some stores are just bigger), but the *growth trajectory* should be the same.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores were already on a faster growth path before month 12 — say, they're in better locations — DiD will attribute that pre-existing advantage to the checkout system. That would be a false positive.

**How we test it**: We'll plot an event study — coefficients for each month relative to treatment. If the pre-treatment coefficients (months 1–11) are all near zero, the assumption looks plausible.

### ② No Anticipation 🟡 *Partially testable*

**What it means**: Stores didn't change behavior *before* month 12 in anticipation of getting the system. For example, if store managers knew the rollout was coming and started training staff in month 10, that would bleed into the pre-period.

**Why it matters**: It would make your pre-trends look dirty and shrink your estimated effect (some of the benefit already happened before "treatment").

**For your case**: A checkout system rollout with a hard go-live date is usually low-risk here. We'll look at t = -2, -3 in the event study to be sure.

### ③ Stable Composition 🟢 *Testable*

**What it means**: Stores didn't open or close *because of* the treatment. If weak stores in the treated group shut down post-month 12, you'd only observe the surviving (better) stores — inflating the estimate.

**For your case**: 100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows expected. We'll verify you have a balanced panel.

### ④ No Spillovers 🔴 *Not directly testable*

**What it means**: The new checkout system in treated stores shouldn't affect sales at nearby control stores (e.g., if faster checkout at a treated store pulls customers away from a neighboring control store).

**For your case**: If stores compete locally (same neighborhood), this is worth thinking about. We'll flag it as a caveat in the interpretation.

### ⑤ Correct Functional Form 🟢 *Not a concern here*

**What it means**: Because all treated stores get treatment at the same month, you have a single cohort. This sidesteps the TWFE heterogeneity bias problem entirely. Standard TWFE is valid.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis code:

```r
# ── 0. Packages ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Load & validate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")   # adjust path

# Confirm balanced panel: expect 100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows
stopifnot(nrow(df) == n_distinct(df$store) * n_distinct(df$month))
cat("Panel is balanced:", nrow(df), "rows\n")

# ── 2. Exploratory plot ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Raw trends by group — first visual check of parallel trends
df |>
  group_by(month, treated) |>
  summarise(mean_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  mutate(group = if_else(treated == 1, "Treated", "Control")) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = mean_sales, color = group)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1) +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 11.5, linetype = "dashed", color = "gray40") +
  annotate("text", x = 12.5, y = Inf, label = "Treatment →",
           hjust = 0, vjust = 1.5, size = 3.5, color = "gray40") +
  labs(title = "Average Sales by Group Over Time",
       x = "Month", y = "Mean Sales", color = NULL) +
  theme_minimal()

# ── 3. Main DiD estimate ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Note: `treated` alone is absorbed by store FE (it never changes within a store).
# The effect is identified by `treated:post` — the interaction.
model <- feols(sales ~ treated:post | store + month,
               data = df,
               cluster = ~store)

summary(model)

# Clean results table
modelsummary(
  list("DiD (TWFE)" = model),
  stars   = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  gof_map = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title   = "Effect of Checkout System on Sales"
)

# Effect size with 95% CI
est  <- coef(model)["treated:post"]
se   <- se(model)["treated:post"]
ci_lo <- est - 1.96 * se
ci_hi <- est + 1.96 * se

cat(sprintf(
  "\nDiD estimate: %.2f (95%% CI: [%.2f, %.2f])\n",
  est, ci_lo, ci_hi
))

# ── 4. Event study (pre-trends + dynamic effects) ────────────────────────────
# Build time-to-treatment variable; set reference period to month before treatment
df <- df |>
  mutate(time_to_treat = month - 12)   # 0 = treatment month; -1 = reference

event_study <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, treated, ref = -1) | store + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store
)

iplot(
  event_study,
  main  = "Event Study: Effect of Checkout System on Sales",
  xlab  = "Months Relative to Treatment",
  ylab  = "Estimated Effect on Sales",
  ref.line = TRUE
)
abline(v = -0.5, lty = 2, col = "gray60")   # treatment threshold
```

**What to look for in the event study plot**:
- **Pre-period (left of the dashed line)**: Coefficients should hover near zero with overlapping confidence intervals. This supports parallel trends.
- **Post-period (right)**: Should show when and how large the effect is, and whether it grows, stabilizes, or fades.

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Run a **placebo test** using only pre-treatment data with a fake treatment at month 6. If the checkout system is what drove the post-period difference, this fake DiD should find nothing.

```r
# ── Placebo: fake treatment at month 6, pre-period only ──────────────────────
df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month <= 11) |>          # pre-treatment period only
  mutate(post_placebo = if_else(month >= 6, 1L, 0L))

placebo <- feols(sales ~ treated:post_placebo | store + month,
                 data    = df_pre,
                 cluster = ~store)

summary(placebo)

cat("\nPlacebo estimate (should be near zero):",
    round(coef(placebo)["treated:post_placebo"], 3), "\n")
```

**Interpretation**: A significant placebo estimate means the "parallel trends" window is contaminated — the groups were already diverging before treatment. A near-zero placebo estimate is reassuring.

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation

Once you run the code, you'll fill in the blanks — but here's the template:

> The DiD estimate suggests the new checkout system changed sales by **[estimate]** units per store per month (95% CI: [lower, upper]). This is equivalent to a **[%]** change relative to the pre-treatment mean in control stores.
>
> **Supporting evidence**: The event study shows no significant pre-treatment differences between groups, which supports the parallel trends assumption.
>
> **Caveats to mention**:
> - We cannot rule out spillovers if treated and control stores compete locally.
> - The estimate reflects the **average effect** for the treated stores during months 12–24. It may not generalize to stores with different baseline sales levels.

| Balanced panel check | Built into code (stopifnot) |
| Main DiD estimate | `feols(sales ~ treated:post \| store + month)` |
| Event study / pre-trends | `iplot(event_study)` |
| Placebo falsification | Fake treatment at month 6 |

### did_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a textbook staggered DiD situation, with one flag that needs immediate attention before we touch any code. Let me walk through this properly.

---

## Stage 1: Design Confirmed

| Element | Detail |
|---|---|
| **Design** | Staggered DiD — 3 cohorts, different treatment dates |
| **Cohorts** | Jan (n=100), Mar (n=200), Jun (n=200) |
| **Outcome** | Weekly sales |
| **Estimand** | Overall ATT (average across all treated stores) |
| **Language** | Python |

**Critical upfront decision**: Standard TWFE is off the table. With 3 cohorts and potentially heterogeneous treatment effects, naive TWFE uses already-treated January stores as implicit controls for March/June stores — those comparisons are contaminated. We'll use **Callaway-Sant'Anna**, which restricts comparisons to clean never-treated (or not-yet-treated) controls.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. ⚠️ No Anticipation — *Your Biggest Threat*

You already identified this: later-wave stores "heard about the program and started informal training on their own." That's anticipation — treated units changing behavior before their official treatment date.

**Why it matters here**: If March stores started informal training in February, their pre-treatment sales trend is already contaminated. The C&S estimator computes group-time ATTs by comparing each cohort to its pre-treatment baseline. A corrupted baseline means a biased ATT — likely **understated**, because some of the effect is absorbed into the "clean" pre-period.

**What to do**: The `differences` package has an `anticipation` parameter. You set it to the number of periods you believe anticipation extends backward. For weekly data, if you think stores started informally ~4 weeks before their wave, set `anticipation=4`. This shifts the effective "clean" pre-period back by that many weeks.

You'll also want to inspect the event study closely: if March/June stores show positive pre-treatment coefficients trending upward before t=0, that's the smoking gun.

---

### 2. 🔶 Parallel Trends

**What it means for you**: Without the training program, would January, March, and June stores have followed the same sales trajectory? Stores weren't randomly assigned to waves — if the company picked higher-performing stores for the first wave, or if wave timing correlated with region/season, trends may differ.

**Test**: Pre-trends event study — plot coefficients for each period before each cohort's treatment date. With weekly data and 2 years, you have decent pre-treatment history. We'll generate this in Stage 3.

---

### 3. 🟡 Stable Composition

**What it means for you**: Did any stores open, close, or get reassigned during the 2 years? A store closing after its training wave (maybe because it struggled) would create survivorship bias. Check that your panel is balanced or that attrition isn't differential.

---

### 4. 🟡 No Spillovers (SUTVA)

**What it means for you**: Could trained stores affect untrained stores' sales? If they're in the same region competing for the same customers, a sales lift in January stores could depress control stores — biasing your estimate downward. Alternatively, if stores share staff knowledge, control stores could partially benefit. Neither is testable directly, but worth noting.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | 🔶 Verify | Pre-trends test, event study plot |
| **No anticipation** | ⚠️ Likely violated | Set `anticipation` parameter; inspect pre-period |
| Stable composition | 🟡 Check | Balance check on panel |
| No spillovers | 🟡 Flag | Note geographic proximity of stores |
| Correct functional form | ✅ Addressed | Using C&S, not TWFE |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
# ============================================================
# Staggered DiD — Callaway & Sant'Anna (2021)
# Weekly store sales data, 3 treatment cohorts
# ============================================================

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as mticker

# ── 0. Package check ────────────────────────────────────────
try:
    from differences import ATTgt
except ImportError:
    raise ImportError("pip install differences")

# ── 1. Data prep ────────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns:
#   store_id   : int, unique store identifier
#   week       : int, week number (1–104 for 2 years)
#   sales      : float, weekly sales
#   first_treat: int, week of first treatment (0 = never treated)

df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")   # ← replace

# Encode never-treated stores as 0 (required by differences package)
# If you have no never-treated stores, use not-yet-treated as control
# (C&S supports this automatically)
assert df['first_treat'].dtype in [int, np.int64], \
    "first_treat must be integer week numbers; use 0 for never-treated"

# Quick balance check — flag differential attrition
total_weeks = df['week'].nunique()
obs_per_store = (df.groupby('store_id')['week']
                   .nunique()
                   .reset_index(name='n_weeks'))
obs_per_store = obs_per_store.merge(
    df[['store_id','first_treat']].drop_duplicates(), on='store_id')
obs_per_store['attrited'] = obs_per_store['n_weeks'] < total_weeks

attrition = (obs_per_store.groupby('first_treat')['attrited']
                          .mean()
                          .rename('attrition_rate'))
print("Attrition rate by cohort:\n", attrition)

# ── 2. Main estimation ───────────────────────────────────────
# anticipation=4: assume stores may react up to 4 weeks before
# their official wave. Set to 0 if you believe no anticipation.
ANTICIPATION = 4   # ← tune based on your domain knowledge

att = ATTgt(
    data        = df,
    outcome_name  = "sales",
    time_name     = "week",
    unit_name     = "store_id",
    cohort_name   = "first_treat",
    anticipation  = ANTICIPATION,
    control_group = "notyettreated",  # uses later-wave stores as controls
                                      # change to "nevertreated" if available
)
att.fit(est_method="dr")   # doubly-robust (recommended)

# ── 3. Overall ATT ──────────────────────────────────────────
agg_simple = att.aggregate("simple")
print("\n── Overall ATT ──────────────────────────────")
print(agg_simple)

# Extract point estimate + 95% CI for reporting
overall_att   = agg_simple.ATT.iloc[0]
overall_se    = agg_simple.se.iloc[0]
ci_lower      = overall_att - 1.96 * overall_se
ci_upper      = overall_att + 1.96 * overall_se
print(f"\nATT = {overall_att:.2f}  (95% CI: {ci_lower:.2f}, {ci_upper:.2f})")

# ── 4. ATT by cohort ────────────────────────────────────────
agg_cohort = att.aggregate("cohort")
print("\n── ATT by Cohort (Wave) ─────────────────────")
print(agg_cohort)

# ── 5. Event study ──────────────────────────────────────────
agg_dynamic = att.aggregate("dynamic")

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(12, 5))

periods = agg_dynamic['e'].values
coefs   = agg_dynamic['ATT'].values
ses     = agg_dynamic['se'].values

ax.errorbar(
    periods, coefs,
    yerr = 1.96 * ses,
    fmt  = 'o-', color='steelblue',
    capsize=4, linewidth=1.5, markersize=5,
    label='ATT(e) ± 95% CI'
)
ax.axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8, linestyle='--')
ax.axvline(-0.5, color='red', linewidth=1.2, linestyle=':',
           label='Treatment onset')

# Shade anticipation window
if ANTICIPATION > 0:
    ax.axvspan(-ANTICIPATION - 0.5, -0.5,
               alpha=0.08, color='orange',
               label=f'Anticipation window ({ANTICIPATION} wks)')

ax.set_xlabel("Weeks Relative to Treatment", fontsize=12)
ax.set_ylabel("ATT (Sales)", fontsize=12)
ax.set_title("Event Study: Dynamic Treatment Effects\n"
             "Pre-period coefficients near zero → parallel trends supported",
             fontsize=12)
ax.legend()
ax.yaxis.set_major_formatter(mticker.FuncFormatter(
    lambda x, _: f"${x:,.0f}"))

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("event_study.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

print("\nEvent study saved to event_study.png")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two checks I'd run here:

**Check 1 — Placebo outcome**: Pick a metric the training program couldn't plausibly affect (e.g., number of customer returns for items purchased before any training, or a supply-side metric like inventory shrinkage from vendor errors). Run the identical C&S estimation. If you get a significant effect, something else is driving your results.

**Check 2 — Pre-treatment placebo**: Take only the pre-treatment weeks and assign a fake treatment date at the midpoint. Run C&S. Pre-treatment coefficients should be flat and insignificant.

```python
# ── Placebo: restrict to pre-treatment weeks, fake treatment date ───
pre_df = df[df['week'] < df['first_treat'].replace(0, 9999)].copy()

# Find midpoint of pre-period for each cohort
pre_df['fake_treat'] = pre_df['first_treat'] // 2   # rough midpoint

att_placebo = ATTgt(
    data          = pre_df,
    outcome_name  = "sales",
    time_name     = "week",
    unit_name     = "store_id",
    cohort_name   = "fake_treat",
    anticipation  = 0,
    control_group = "notyettreated",
)
att_placebo.fit(est_method="dr")
agg_placebo = att_placebo.aggregate("simple")
print("\n── Placebo ATT (should be near zero) ───────")
print(agg_placebo)
```

Once you have results, the plain-language read is:

> "On average, the training program increased weekly sales by **$[ATT]** per store (95% CI: $[lower]–$[upper]), pooling across all three rollout waves. This estimate accounts for differences in when stores were trained and uses only valid comparisons between stores that hadn't yet received training. The event study [showed/did not show] evidence of pre-existing trends, [supporting/complicating] a causal interpretation."

**Key caveat to include**: "If stores in later waves began informal training before their official wave date, this estimate likely understates the full effect of formal training, because some effect is absorbed into the pre-period baseline."

**One decision you need to make before running**: How many weeks of anticipation do you want to allow? `ANTICIPATION=4` (one month) is a reasonable starting point, but you know your stores better than I do. If you have any operational data on when informal training started (Slack messages, manager reports), use that to calibrate. You can also try `ANTICIPATION=0`, `2`, `4`, `8` and see how sensitive the ATT is — that sensitivity itself tells you something.

### exercise_iv_quality

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.0, "safety": 0.0, "actionable": 0.0, "overall": 0.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great learning goal — validity is the crux of IV, and it's easy to apply the method mechanically without catching when it breaks.

Before I generate the exercise, one quick question:

**What difficulty level?**

- **Basic** — A clean, valid instrument. You practice the mechanics (first stage, 2SLS, F-stat) and recover the true effect.
- **Intermediate** — A weak instrument. The method applies but you'll need to diagnose the problem and understand what it does to your estimates.
- **Advanced** — An instrument that *looks* valid but violates the exclusion restriction. The real challenge: the bias is invisible in the data unless you reason carefully about the mechanism.

Given your learning goal ("valid vs. not"), I'd lean toward **Advanced** — you'd run the analysis, get a plausible-looking result, and then have to figure out *why* it's wrong. But Basic or Intermediate are better starting points if you want to build up intuition first.

What sounds right?

</details>

### experiments_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.0, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.5}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — individual-level randomization, binary outcome (retained/not at day 7), n=10,000. That's enough to get started. Let me walk you through this stage by stage.

---

## Stage 1: Setup — Confirmed ✓

| Element | Details |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New onboarding flow |
| **Control** | Existing onboarding flow |
| **Randomization** | Individual-level |
| **Outcome** | 7-day retention (binary) |
| **N** | 10,000 users |
| **Stage** | Analysis (data in hand) |

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific context. I'll flag severity and ask where I need your input.

---

### 1. Random Assignment ⚠️ — Needs verification

**What it means here:** Each user had a 50/50 chance of seeing the new vs. old onboarding flow, and nothing about the user (device, signup time, location) influenced which flow they got.

**Why it matters:** If assignment wasn't truly random — e.g., if there was a bug that sent iOS users to treatment and Android to control — then any retention difference might just reflect device-type differences, not the onboarding flow itself.

**We can test this** with a balance table: do treatment and control look similar on baseline covariates?

> 🔹 **Question for you:** Do you have baseline covariates in your data? (e.g., device type, OS, signup channel, country, time of day they signed up)

---

### 2. SUTVA / No Interference ✅ — Likely satisfied

**What it means here:** One user being in the new onboarding flow doesn't affect another user's retention. 

**Why it matters:** If your app has viral loops or friend invites baked into onboarding, a treated user inviting a control user could contaminate the control group.

**For a typical mobile onboarding flow, this is usually fine** — users go through onboarding independently. Worth flagging only if onboarding includes "invite a friend" prompts.

> 🔹 **Question:** Does the new onboarding flow include any social/referral features that would connect treated and control users?

---

### 3. No Differential Attrition ⚠️ — Important to check

**What it means here:** Users aren't dropping *out of measurement* at different rates between groups. For retention, this typically means: are there users whose 7-day window hasn't elapsed yet, or who you can't observe?

**Why it matters:** If 5% of treated users uninstalled before day 7 could be measured, vs. 1% of control users, the "retained" comparison is unfair — you're comparing a selected subset.

> 🔹 **Question:** Do all 10,000 users have a complete 7-day observation window, or are some users too recent to have reached day 7 yet?

---

### 4. Compliance ✅ — Likely satisfied (but confirm)

**What it means here:** Users assigned to the new flow actually *saw* the new flow, and control users saw the old one. 

**Why it matters:** If your app's A/B framework had a bug — e.g., 10% of treatment users fell back to the control flow due to a crash — the true effect is diluted.

> 🔹 **Question:** Do you have any log data confirming which flow each user actually saw? Or do you only have assignment data?

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Random assignment | ⚠️ Verify | Run balance table |
| No interference | ✅ Likely OK | Confirm no social features |
| No differential attrition | ⚠️ Verify | Check observation windows |
| Compliance | ✅ Likely OK | Confirm no fallback bugs |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

While you answer those questions, here's the **complete analysis code**. I've structured it so you can run it now and layer in more checks as needed.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
from scipy import stats
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import seaborn as sns

# ── 0. Load your data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns:
#   user_id       : unique user identifier
#   treatment     : 1 = new onboarding, 0 = existing onboarding
#   retained_7d   : 1 = retained at day 7, 0 = churned
#   (optional)    : device_type, os, country, signup_channel, signup_hour …

df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

# Quick sanity checks
print("Shape:", df.shape)
print("\nGroup sizes:")
print(df['treatment'].value_counts())
print("\nMissing outcomes:", df['retained_7d'].isna().sum())
print("\nRetention by group:")
print(df.groupby('treatment')['retained_7d'].mean().rename({0: 'Control', 1: 'Treatment'}))


# ── 1. Balance Table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Only run if you have baseline covariates.
# Replace covariates list with your actual columns.

covariates = ['device_type_ios', 'signup_hour', 'country_us']  # ← adjust

if all(c in df.columns for c in covariates):
    balance = []
    for cov in covariates:
        treated = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, cov]
        control = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, cov]
        pooled_std = np.sqrt((treated.var() + control.var()) / 2)
        smd = (treated.mean() - control.mean()) / pooled_std if pooled_std > 0 else np.nan
        _, pval = stats.ttest_ind(treated, control)
        balance.append({
            'covariate': cov,
            'mean_treatment': treated.mean(),
            'mean_control': control.mean(),
            'SMD': smd,
            'p_value': pval,
            'balanced': abs(smd) < 0.1  # rule of thumb: |SMD| < 0.1 is good
        })

    balance_df = pd.DataFrame(balance)
    print("\n── Balance Table ──")
    print(balance_df.to_string(index=False))
    print("\n⚠️  Flag any |SMD| > 0.1 — those covariates are imbalanced.")
else:
    print("No baseline covariates found — skipping balance table.")


# ── 2. Primary Analysis: Difference in Retention Rates ───────────────────────

# 2a. Raw proportions
p_treat = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, 'retained_7d'].mean()
p_control = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, 'retained_7d'].mean()
n_treat = df['treatment'].sum()
n_control = len(df) - n_treat

print(f"\n── Retention Rates ──")
print(f"  Treatment : {p_treat:.3f} ({p_treat*100:.1f}%)")
print(f"  Control   : {p_control:.3f} ({p_control*100:.1f}%)")
print(f"  Difference: {p_treat - p_control:+.3f} ({(p_treat - p_control)*100:+.1f} pp)")

# 2b. Two-proportion z-test
count = np.array([df.loc[df['treatment']==1,'retained_7d'].sum(),
                  df.loc[df['treatment']==0,'retained_7d'].sum()])
nobs  = np.array([n_treat, n_control])

from statsmodels.stats.proportion import proportions_ztest, proportion_confint
stat, pval = proportions_ztest(count, nobs)

# 95% CI on the difference (using normal approximation)
se_diff = np.sqrt(p_treat*(1-p_treat)/n_treat + p_control*(1-p_control)/n_control)
ci_lower = (p_treat - p_control) - 1.96 * se_diff
ci_upper = (p_treat - p_control) + 1.96 * se_diff

print(f"\n── Inference ──")
print(f"  z-statistic : {stat:.4f}")
print(f"  p-value     : {pval:.4f}")
print(f"  95% CI on Δ : [{ci_lower:+.3f}, {ci_upper:+.3f}]")
print(f"  Significant : {'✅ Yes' if pval < 0.05 else '❌ No'} (α = 0.05)")

# 2c. Relative lift
lift = (p_treat - p_control) / p_control
print(f"  Relative lift: {lift:+.1%}")


# ── 3. Regression Adjustment (OLS — Lin estimator) ───────────────────────────
# Adding pre-experiment covariates reduces variance and improves precision.
# OLS with binary outcome is fine for inference with large n (CLT).
# Use only pre-treatment covariates — never post-treatment ones.

if all(c in df.columns for c in covariates):
    cov_str = ' + '.join(covariates)
    formula = f'retained_7d ~ treatment + {cov_str}'
    model = smf.ols(formula, data=df).fit(cov_type='HC2')  # HC2 = heteroskedasticity-robust

    print("\n── OLS Regression (covariate-adjusted) ──")
    print(model.summary().tables[1])  # just the coefficients table
    print(f"\nAdjusted treatment effect: {model.params['treatment']:.4f}")
    print(f"95% CI: [{model.conf_int().loc['treatment', 0]:.4f}, "
          f"{model.conf_int().loc['treatment', 1]:.4f}]")
else:
    print("\nSkipping regression adjustment (no covariates available).")


# ── 4. Visualization ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 5))

# Plot 1: Retention rates with CIs
groups = ['Control', 'Treatment']
rates = [p_control, p_treat]
cis = [
    1.96 * np.sqrt(p*(1-p)/n)
    for p, n in [(p_control, n_control), (p_treat, n_treat)]
]
colors = ['#6c8ebf', '#82b366']

axes[0].bar(groups, rates, color=colors, yerr=cis, capsize=6, width=0.5)
axes[0].set_ylabel('7-Day Retention Rate')
axes[0].set_title('Retention Rate by Group')
axes[0].set_ylim(0, min(1.0, max(rates) * 1.3))
for i, (r, c) in enumerate(zip(rates, cis)):
    axes[0].text(i, r + c + 0.005, f'{r:.1%}', ha='center', fontsize=11, fontweight='bold')

# Plot 2: Effect size (difference) with CI
axes[1].axvline(0, color='gray', linestyle='--', linewidth=1)
diff = p_treat - p_control
axes[1].errorbar(diff, 0.5, xerr=1.96*se_diff, fmt='o', color='#82b366',
                 markersize=10, capsize=8, linewidth=2)
axes[1].set_yticks([])
axes[1].set_xlabel('Treatment Effect (percentage points)')
axes[1].set_title('Estimated Effect on 7-Day Retention\nwith 95% Confidence Interval')
axes[1].text(diff, 0.6, f'Δ = {diff:+.3f}\np = {pval:.3f}',
             ha='center', fontsize=10)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('ab_test_results.png', dpi=150, bbox_inches='tight')
plt.show()

print("\n✅ Analysis complete. Plot saved to ab_test_results.png")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Check

Once you have results, run this **placebo outcome test** — pick a metric the new onboarding flow *shouldn't* affect, like "did the user's first session happen within 1 hour of signup" (a behavior that occurs before onboarding ends):

```python
# Placebo test: run the same analysis on a pre-onboarding metric
# (replace 'placebo_outcome' with an actual pre-treatment metric)

if 'placebo_outcome' in df.columns:
    p_placebo_t = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 1, 'placebo_outcome'].mean()
    p_placebo_c = df.loc[df['treatment'] == 0, 'placebo_outcome'].mean()
    count_p = np.array([df.loc[df['treatment']==1,'placebo_outcome'].sum(),
                        df.loc[df['treatment']==0,'placebo_outcome'].sum()])
    _, pval_placebo = proportions_ztest(count_p, nobs)
    print(f"\n── Placebo Test ──")
    print(f"  Δ = {p_placebo_t - p_placebo_c:+.4f}, p = {pval_placebo:.4f}")
    print("  ✅ OK" if pval_placebo > 0.05 else "  ⚠️  Significant placebo effect — investigate randomization.")
```

### experiments_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8333333333333334}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

You have a **two-sided non-compliance** problem:

| Group | N | Non-compliance |
|---|---|---|
| Assigned drug | 1000 | 15% refused → never took it |
| Assigned placebo | 1000 | 5% obtained drug elsewhere |

This means your *assignment* ≠ *receipt*. Two estimands are on the table:

- **ITT** — effect of being *assigned* to drug (always valid, always report this)
- **CACE/LATE** — effect of *actually receiving* the drug, estimated for *compliers* via IV

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through what matters given your setup.

### ✅ Random Assignment
You randomized 1000 per arm — good. But non-compliance happened *after* randomization, so we still need to verify baseline balance.

### ⚠️ Compliance (the main issue here)
Two-sided non-compliance: some treated didn't comply (15%), some control crossed over (5%). The simple comparison of `received_treatment` groups would be **biased** — compliers may differ systematically from non-compliers (e.g., healthier patients may be more motivated to take the drug). This is why we need IV.

**IV logic**: Use `assigned_treatment` as an instrument for `received_treatment`. Assignment is random (relevant + independent), and it affects the outcome *only through* whether you actually took the drug (exclusion restriction). This is the standard CACE design.

> **Why exclusion restriction matters here**: If patients who *know* they were assigned to drug change behavior in other ways — exercise more, diet changes — then assignment affects outcomes through channels beyond the drug itself. Is that plausible in your trial?

### ✅ SUTVA
Patients likely don't infect each other with the treatment effect. Plausible in a clinical drug trial.

### ✅ Monotonicity
The IV CACE interpretation requires that no one is a "defier" — i.e., no one takes the drug *because* they were assigned to placebo and refuses it *because* they were assigned to drug. This is almost always safe to assume in drug trials.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```r
library(fixest)
library(cobalt)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 0. Load data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# df <- read.csv("your_data.csv")

# ── 1. Compliance summary ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cat("=== Compliance Table ===\n")
print(table(assigned = df$assigned_treatment,
            received = df$received_treatment))

comply_treat  <- mean(df$received_treatment[df$assigned_treatment == 1])
contam_ctrl   <- mean(df$received_treatment[df$assigned_treatment == 0])
compliance_rate <- comply_treat - contam_ctrl   # "first stage" compliance

cat(sprintf("\nCompliance rate (drug arm):     %.1f%%\n", comply_treat  * 100))
cat(sprintf("Contamination rate (placebo arm): %.1f%%\n", contam_ctrl   * 100))
cat(sprintf("Net compliance (complier share):  %.1f%%\n", compliance_rate * 100))

# ── 2. Balance check ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Add baseline covariates here if you have them
# bal.tab(assigned_treatment ~ age + sex + baseline_score, data = df,
#         binary = "std", continuous = "std", thresholds = c(m = 0.1))

# ── 3. ITT — effect of *assignment* ──────────────────────────────────────────
# Always valid regardless of compliance. Report this.
model_itt <- feols(outcome ~ assigned_treatment, data = df)

# ── 4. CACE/LATE — effect of *receiving* drug (for compliers) via IV ──────────
# assigned_treatment instruments for received_treatment
model_cace <- feols(outcome ~ 1 | received_treatment ~ assigned_treatment,
                    data = df)

# First-stage F: must be >> 10 for IV to be reliable
cat("\n=== First-Stage Diagnostics ===\n")
print(fitstat(model_cace, "ivf"))

# ── 5. Results table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("ITT (assigned)" = model_itt,
       "CACE/LATE (received, IV)" = model_cace),
  stars    = TRUE,
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  coef_rename = c(assigned_treatment  = "Assigned Drug",
                  fit_received_treatment = "Received Drug (IV)")
)

# ── 6. Sanity check: naive (biased) comparison for reference ──────────────────
model_naive <- feols(outcome ~ received_treatment, data = df)
cat("\n=== Naive OLS (biased — do NOT use as main estimate) ===\n")
print(coef(model_naive)["received_treatment"])

# ── 7. Quick interpretation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
itt_est  <- coef(model_itt)["assigned_treatment"]
cace_est <- coef(model_cace)["fit_received_treatment"]

cat(sprintf(
  "\nITT:  %.3f  (effect of being *assigned* to drug)\n
CACE: %.3f  (effect of *taking* drug, for compliers)\n
Ratio check: ITT / compliance_rate = %.3f (should ≈ CACE)\n",
  itt_est, cace_est, itt_est / compliance_rate
))
```

---

## What to expect

The CACE will be **larger in magnitude than the ITT** — because the ITT is diluted by the 15% who were assigned drug but didn't take it, and contaminated by the 5% in placebo who did. The relationship is:

$$\text{CACE} = \frac{\text{ITT}}{\text{compliance rate}} = \frac{\text{ITT}}{0.85 - 0.05} = \frac{\text{ITT}}{0.80}$$

So your CACE will be roughly **25% larger** than the ITT. That's the cost of imperfect compliance.

**Check the first-stage F-statistic.** With 80% net compliance and n=2000, it will almost certainly be enormous (>>100). If it were near 10, IV estimates become unreliable.

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two quick checks worth running:

1. **Placebo outcome**: If you have a baseline measurement of `outcome`, run the IV analysis on the baseline — you should find zero effect (assignment can't affect the past).
2. **Naive vs. IV comparison**: If the naive OLS estimate (`received_treatment` regressed on outcome without IV) differs substantially from CACE, that confirms the selection bias you'd have gotten without IV. Report both with an explanation.

---

## Stage 5: Interpretation template

> *"We estimate that assignment to the drug [reduced/increased] the outcome by **[ITT estimate]** units on average (ITT, 95% CI: [lb, ub]). Among patients who complied with their assignment, actually receiving the drug [reduced/increased] the outcome by **[CACE estimate]** units (CACE/LATE via IV, 95% CI: [lb, ub]). The first-stage F-statistic of [F] confirms the instrument is strong. Note that the CACE applies specifically to compliers — the ~80% of patients who would take the drug if assigned to it and not take it otherwise."*

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## One quick flag before we start

I'll proceed based on your **stated problem** (tutoring → test scores, instrument = tutoring center proximity). If you share the actual columns, I can adapt the code precisely.

---

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Here's what I'm working with:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Endogenous treatment** | Private tutoring (binary: received / didn't) |
| **Instrument** | Tutoring center opened within 1km in past year (binary) |
| **Outcome** | Test scores |
| **N** | 3,000 students |
| **Language** | R |

A few quick questions before I generate code:

1. **Is tutoring binary** (yes/no) or continuous (hours per week)? This affects the first-stage interpretation.
2. **Any covariates** to control for — student income, prior grades, school fixed effects?
3. **"Okay but not amazing"** — do you have a rough F-statistic in mind? Below 10 is a crisis; 10–30 is worrying; above 100 is comfortable. This will shape how we handle the weak instrument problem.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Let's Work Through Them

I want to flag one concern immediately before we even get to code.

---

### ⚡ 1. Relevance (First Stage Strength) — **YOUR KEY RISK**

> "Does the instrument actually predict whether students receive tutoring?"

You said the first stage is "okay but not amazing." This matters enormously because:

- **F < 10**: Fatal. IV estimates become biased *toward* OLS, defeating the entire purpose. Confidence intervals explode.
- **F between 10–100**: Worrying. Modern standards (Lee et al. 2022) recommend F >> 100. In this range, you can report results but must be transparent about weak-instrument concerns.
- **F > 100**: Comfortable.

The intuition: if the instrument only nudges treatment weakly, any outcome variation it captures is mostly noise. The IV estimator amplifies that noise badly.

✅ **This IS testable.** Code is below.

---

### 🔍 2. Exclusion Restriction — **NEEDS YOUR ARGUMENT**

> "Does a tutoring center opening nearby affect test scores *only* through whether the student actually uses tutoring?"

This is the hard one, and it's **not statistically testable**. Ask yourself:

- Could proximity to a tutoring center affect scores *directly* — e.g., by signaling neighborhood investment in education, which shifts parental behavior or student motivation?
- Could the tutoring center correlate with other neighborhood changes (new library, better transport) that independently affect scores?

If you can think of plausible direct paths from the instrument to test scores — the instrument is compromised, and no statistical fix exists.

❌ **Not directly testable. Must be argued.**

---

### 🎲 3. Independence (As-If Random) — **PARTIALLY TESTABLE**

> "Did the tutoring center open near some students essentially by chance — not because of unobserved student characteristics?"

This is plausible if tutoring center placement is driven by business/zoning decisions unrelated to individual student ability. It's *not* plausible if centers systematically locate near high-demand (high-ability) neighborhoods.

✅ **Partially testable**: we can check whether center proximity is balanced across observable student characteristics (income, prior grades, school type).

---

### ↕️ 4. Monotonicity — **ARGUE IT**

> "Does proximity only ever increase (never decrease) the probability of using tutoring? No 'defiers'?"

This is almost certainly plausible here. It's hard to imagine a student who *would* use tutoring without a center nearby but *avoids* it once one opens. One-sided non-compliance.

✅ **Plausible — low concern.**

---

### Summary so far

| Assumption | Status | Concern Level |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | Testable | 🔴 **High** — "not amazing" first stage |
| Exclusion restriction | Untestable | 🟡 **Medium** — neighborhood effects possible |
| Independence | Partially testable | 🟡 **Medium** — depends on center placement |
| Monotonicity | Argue it | 🟢 **Low** — defiers implausible |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Adapt `tutoring` / `test_score` / `center_opened` / your covariates to your actual column names.

```r
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 0. Peek at your data ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
glimpse(df)

# ── 1. First stage: does instrument predict treatment? ────────────────────────
first_stage <- feols(tutoring ~ center_opened + income + prior_grade,
                     data = df, vcov = "hetero")

summary(first_stage)

# Critical: extract the F-statistic
fs_f <- fitstat(first_stage, "f")$f$stat
cat("First-stage F-statistic:", round(fs_f, 2), "\n")
cat("Rule of thumb: >10 (Stock-Yogo), ideally >100 (Lee et al. 2022)\n")

# ── 2. Naive OLS (for comparison — likely biased upward if tutoring is
#       chosen by motivated students) ──────────────────────────────────────────
ols_model <- feols(test_score ~ tutoring + income + prior_grade,
                   data = df, vcov = "hetero")

# ── 3. 2SLS / IV estimation ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
iv_model <- feols(test_score ~ income + prior_grade | tutoring ~ center_opened,
                  data = df, vcov = "hetero")

summary(iv_model)

# IV-specific diagnostics (F-statistic in IV context)
fitstat(iv_model, "ivf")

# ── 4. Compare OLS vs IV side by side ─────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  list("OLS (naive)" = ols_model, "IV / 2SLS" = iv_model),
  stars    = TRUE,
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared")
)
```

---

### ⚠️ If the F-statistic is low (< 30): add this

```r
# LIML is less biased than 2SLS under weak instruments
iv_liml <- feols(test_score ~ income + prior_grade | tutoring ~ center_opened,
                 data = df, vcov = "hetero", method = "liml")

# Anderson-Rubin confidence sets — valid even with weak instruments
# (requires the AER package)
library(AER)

iv_aer <- ivreg(test_score ~ tutoring + income + prior_grade |
                  center_opened + income + prior_grade,
                data = df)

summary(iv_aer, diagnostics = TRUE)
# Look at: Wu-Hausman (is tutoring actually endogenous?),
#          Weak instruments (same as F-statistic check)
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification Checks

```r
# ── Check A: Reduced form ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Regress outcome directly on instrument.
# If the instrument is valid and relevant, this should be significant
# and in the same direction as IV estimate × first-stage coefficient.

reduced_form <- feols(test_score ~ center_opened + income + prior_grade,
                      data = df, vcov = "hetero")
summary(reduced_form)

# Sanity check: rf_coef ≈ iv_coef × first_stage_coef
rf_coef <- coef(reduced_form)["center_opened"]
fs_coef <- coef(first_stage)["center_opened"]
iv_coef <- coef(iv_model)["tutoring"]
cat("Reduced form coef:", round(rf_coef, 4), "\n")
cat("IV × First stage:", round(iv_coef * fs_coef, 4), "\n")

# ── Check B: Balance test (supports independence assumption) ──────────────────
# The instrument should NOT predict pre-existing student characteristics
balance_income     <- feols(income      ~ center_opened, data = df)
balance_prior      <- feols(prior_grade ~ center_opened, data = df)

modelsummary(
  list("Income ~ instrument"      = balance_income,
       "Prior grade ~ instrument" = balance_prior),
  stars = TRUE,
  note  = "Significant results would undermine instrument independence"
)
```

---

## What to watch for when you run this

| Result | What it means |
|---|---|
| F < 10 | Stop — IV is not viable as-is. Find a stronger instrument or use reduced form. |
| F 10–30 | Report LIML alongside 2SLS; note weak instrument caveat prominently |
| F 30–100 | Acceptable; note caveat; Anderson-Rubin CI recommended |
| IV >> OLS | Suggests OLS was biased downward (tutoring endogeneity in opposite direction than expected — possible if lower-achieving students self-select into tutoring) |
| IV << OLS | More typical: motivated students self-select → OLS inflated |
| Balance test significant | Independence assumption shaky; consider adding controls or finding a cleaner instrument |

---

Share your F-statistic when you have it and I'll help interpret the results and write up the findings. Also let me know the actual column names so I can make the code plug-and-play.

</details>

### matching_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 1.0}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup

Good data structure. Let me confirm what I'm working with before diving in:

| Item | Value |
|---|---|
| N | 2,000 (1,000 treated / 1,000 control — balanced) |
| Covariates | age, BMI, blood pressure, disease severity, comorbidity count |
| Outcome | Recovery time (continuous) |
| Language | R |

**Two quick clarifications before I generate code:**

1. **Estimand — ATT or ATE?** In a clinical setting, ATT (effect on patients who actually received the drug) is usually the right question. ATE (effect on the whole population, including those for whom the drug might not have been indicated) can be harder to defend. I'll **default to ATT** — correct me if you want ATE.

2. **Column names:** Your sample shows `x1` and `x2`, but you described 5 covariates. Are the actual column names `age`, `bmi`, `bp`, `disease_severity`, `comorbidity_count` (or similar)? I'll use those names in the code — just confirm or give me the exact names.

**One flag from the setup itself:** You mentioned disease severity score as a covariate. This is the variable most likely to drive selection into treatment — doctors probably prescribed the new drug more (or less) to severe cases. That directly shapes how hard the conditional independence assumption is to defend. We'll return to this.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each assumption for your specific context. This is not a checklist — understanding *why* each matters will determine how much you can trust the final estimate.

---

### 1. Conditional Independence (Unconfoundedness) ⚠️ — *Untestable, high stakes*

**What it means here:** After conditioning on age, BMI, blood pressure, disease severity, and comorbidity count, treatment assignment (new drug vs. standard care) is independent of potential recovery times. In plain English: the only reason some patients got the new drug — beyond what's captured in your five covariates — is noise.

**Why it matters:** This is the entire foundation of the analysis. If a physician's judgment, patient preference, or any other unobserved factor drove treatment assignment *and* also affects recovery time, your estimate is biased. There's no statistical test that can detect this — it lives in the unobserved world.

**Is it plausible?** Ask yourself:
- Were there formulary restrictions, insurance rules, or site-level protocols that determined who got the drug? → If yes, those factors may not be in your data.
- Did physicians give the drug to patients they expected to do *better* (or worse)? → Classic selection on prognosis.
- Is disease severity *fully* captured by your score, or is there residual clinical judgment beyond the score?

**My honest read:** With disease severity in the model, you're covering the most likely confounder. But physician judgment layered on top of a severity score is a classic source of residual confounding. Flag this in your write-up.

---

### 2. Overlap / Positivity ✅ — *Testable, check immediately*

**What it means here:** Every patient — regardless of their age, BMI, severity, etc. — had some nonzero probability of receiving either treatment. If the drug was only prescribed to patients above a certain severity threshold, units below that threshold have zero probability of treatment and cannot contribute to estimation.

**Why it matters:** With 50/50 assignment, overlap is *likely* decent. But 50/50 split at the margin doesn't prevent overlap problems within covariate strata. We need to look at the propensity score distribution.

Run this first — before any matching:

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(cobalt)
library(ggplot2)

# Pre-flight overlap check — run this BEFORE matching
ps_check <- matchit(
  treatment ~ age + bmi + bp + disease_severity + comorbidity_count,
  data = df,
  method = NULL,   # No matching yet — just estimate PS
  estimand = "ATT"
)

# Extract propensity scores
df$ps <- ps_check$distance

# Overlap histogram
ggplot(df, aes(x = ps, fill = factor(treatment))) +
  geom_histogram(alpha = 0.5, position = "identity", bins = 50) +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("steelblue", "tomato"),
                    labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(
    title = "Pre-matching propensity score overlap",
    x = "Propensity Score", y = "Count", fill = "Group"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()

# Numerical summary
cat("=== Propensity Score Summary ===\n")
cat("Treated:\n"); print(summary(df$ps[df$treatment == 1]))
cat("Control:\n"); print(summary(df$ps[df$treatment == 0]))
cat("\nFraction with PS < 0.05:", mean(df$ps < 0.05), "\n")
cat("Fraction with PS > 0.95:", mean(df$ps > 0.95), "\n")
```

**What to look for:** The two histograms should substantially overlap. If the treated group's scores are all clustered near 1.0 and controls near 0.0, you have a separation problem — matching cannot fix that.

---

### 3. SUTVA (No Interference) ✅ — *Plausible, verify contextually*

**What it means here:** One patient receiving the new drug doesn't affect another patient's recovery time.

**Is it plausible?** For an individual drug with no contagion mechanism — almost certainly yes. The one scenario where this could fail is if the drug is in short supply and one patient receiving it means another doesn't. Worth a quick check on your study context, but I'd call this low concern.

---

### 4. Correct Propensity Score Specification ✅ — *Testable via balance*

**What it means here:** The logistic regression model for propensity scores adequately captures how patients were selected into treatment.

**Why it matters:** A misspecified propensity score model produces incorrect weights/matches, which means confounding isn't fully removed. The good news: we can *observe* whether it worked by checking covariate balance after matching. Poor post-matching balance = model isn't working.

**The doubly-robust safety net:** If you combine propensity score matching with an outcome model (DR estimation), the estimate is consistent if *either* model is correct. This is worth doing here given the severity-score confounding concern.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Testable? | Concern Level | Action |
|---|---|---|---|
| Conditional independence | ❌ No | ⚠️ **High** | Sensitivity analysis (E-value, Rosenbaum bounds) |
| Overlap / positivity | ✅ Yes | 🟡 Medium | Run overlap check above first |
| SUTVA | ❌ No | 🟢 Low | Verify no supply constraints |
| PS specification | ✅ Indirect | 🟡 Medium | Check post-matching balance (SMD < 0.1) |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Full pipeline — PS matching with balance diagnostics, then doubly-robust estimation as the primary estimator (more robust to the specification concerns above):

```r
library(MatchIt)
library(cobalt)
library(marginaleffects)
library(ggplot2)

# ── 0. Define covariates ──────────────────────────────────────────────
covs <- c("age", "bmi", "bp", "disease_severity", "comorbidity_count")
ps_formula <- as.formula(
  paste("treatment ~", paste(covs, collapse = " + "))
)

# ── 1. Pre-flight overlap check ───────────────────────────────────────
m_null <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df, method = NULL, estimand = "ATT")
df$ps  <- m_null$distance

ggplot(df, aes(x = ps, fill = factor(treatment))) +
  geom_histogram(alpha = 0.5, position = "identity", bins = 50) +
  scale_fill_manual(values = c("steelblue", "tomato"),
                    labels = c("Control", "Treated")) +
  labs(title = "Pre-matching PS overlap", x = "Propensity Score",
       y = "Count", fill = "Group") +
  theme_minimal()

# Flag extreme scores — if this is large, trim before proceeding
cat("Fraction with PS > 0.95 or < 0.05:",
    mean(df$ps > 0.95 | df$ps < 0.05), "\n")

# ── 2. Nearest-neighbor matching (ATT, 1:1, with caliper) ────────────
# Caliper of 0.1 SD of the logit PS is standard practice
m_out <- matchit(
  ps_formula,
  data      = df,
  method    = "nearest",
  distance  = "glm",         # logistic PS
  estimand  = "ATT",
  ratio     = 1,
  replace   = FALSE,
  caliper   = 0.1,           # 0.1 SD of logit PS
  std.caliper = TRUE
)

cat("\n=== Matching Summary ===\n")
print(summary(m_out, un = FALSE))

# ── 3. Balance diagnostics ────────────────────────────────────────────
# Primary diagnostic: SMD < 0.1 for all covariates after matching
bal <- bal.tab(m_out, thresholds = c(m = 0.1, v = 2))
print(bal)

# Love plot — the standard way to report balance
love.plot(
  m_out,
  thresholds  = c(m = 0.1),
  var.order   = "unadjusted",
  abs         = TRUE,
  colors      = c("tomato", "steelblue"),
  shapes      = c("circle", "square"),
  title       = "Covariate Balance: Before vs. After Matching"
)

# If SMDs are still > 0.1 after matching, try full matching:
# m_out <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df,
#                  method = "full", estimand = "ATT")

# ── 4. Extract matched data and estimate ATT ──────────────────────────
m_data <- match.data(m_out)

# Outcome model on matched data (regression-adjusted within matched sample)
# Using cluster-robust SEs on subclass (matched pair) — important!
out_model <- lm(
  outcome ~ treatment + age + bmi + bp + disease_severity + comorbidity_count,
  data    = m_data,
  weights = weights
)

att_est <- avg_comparisons(
  out_model,
  variables = "treatment",
  vcov      = ~subclass,   # cluster on matched pairs
  newdata   = m_data,
  wts       = "weights"
)

cat("\n=== ATT Estimate ===\n")
print(att_est)

# ── 5. Doubly-robust check ────────────────────────────────────────────
# IPW weights on full data (for comparison / DR robustness check)
df$ipw <- ifelse(
  df$treatment == 1,
  1,
  df$ps / (1 - df$ps)
)

# Trim extreme weights (stabilizes variance)
w_99 <- quantile(df$ipw, 0.99)
df$ipw_trim <- pmin(df$ipw, w_99)

dr_model <- lm(
  outcome ~ treatment + age + bmi + bp + disease_severity + comorbidity_count,
  data    = df,
  weights = ipw_trim
)

dr_est <- avg_comparisons(
  dr_model,
  variables = "treatment",
  vcov      = "HC3"
)

cat("\n=== Doubly-Robust (IPW + outcome model) Estimate ===\n")
print(dr_est)
```

## Stage 4: Falsification

Three checks I'd run on this data, in order of priority:

**1. Sensitivity analysis — how fragile is the estimate?**

```r
library(sensemakr)

# Fit a simple OLS on matched data first (sensemakr needs lm object)
sens_model <- lm(
  outcome ~ treatment + age + bmi + bp + disease_severity + comorbidity_count,
  data = m_data, weights = weights
)

sens <- sensemakr(
  model     = sens_model,
  treatment = "treatment",
  benchmark_covariates = "disease_severity",  # use severity as benchmark
  kd        = 1:3
)

summary(sens)
plot(sens)
# Key output: robustness value (RV). How strong would an unobserved
# confounder need to be — relative to disease_severity — to fully
# explain away the estimated effect?
```

**2. Placebo outcome — does matching "find" spurious effects?**

If you have a pre-treatment outcome measure (e.g., recovery time from a prior illness episode), run the full pipeline on that. A significant "effect" signals residual confounding.

**3. Specification sensitivity**

```r
# Compare: nearest neighbor vs. full matching vs. CEM
m_full <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df, method = "full", estimand = "ATT")
m_cem  <- matchit(ps_formula, data = df, method = "cem",  estimand = "ATT")

# If the ATT estimates diverge substantially across specs,
# results are not stable — interpret with extra caution.
```

### matching_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.7222222222222222}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Check

Before diving into code, let me flag two serious issues you've already surfaced — because they affect whether matching can work here at all.

---

## 🚨 Two Red Flags Before We Begin

### 1. Poor Overlap — Already a Known Problem

You said the propensity score distributions barely overlap. This is **not a nuisance to work around — it's a fundamental identification failure.** When treated and control units don't overlap in covariate space, matching is forced to extrapolate. IPW weights explode. Estimates become dominated by a handful of outliers.

Let's verify and visualize this first before writing any estimation code.

### 2. Key Confounders Are Unmeasured

You told me enrollees tend to be **younger, healthier, and higher income** — but your dataset only has `age`, `tenure`, and `department`. There is no health status variable. There is no income variable. These are variables that almost certainly affect **both** who enrolls **and** productivity outcomes.

This means **Conditional Independence (CIA) is likely violated** even after matching on your available covariates. Matching cannot remove confounding from variables you didn't measure.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your context:

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| **CIA / Unconfoundedness** | 🔴 Likely violated | Health and income are unmeasured. Voluntary enrollment is classic self-selection. |
| **Overlap / Positivity** | 🔴 Likely violated | You've already flagged this. We'll confirm below. |
| **SUTVA** | 🟡 Plausible | Employees are unlikely to interfere with each other's productivity, but team/department dynamics could matter. |
| **Model Specification** | 🟡 Unknown | Depends on how well logistic regression captures enrollment probability with only 3 covariates. |

---

### Why CIA matters here

CIA says: *after conditioning on age, tenure, and department, treatment assignment is as good as random.* But you've told me healthier, higher-income people enrolled. Neither health nor income is in your dataset. So two employees with identical age, tenure, and department could still have very different enrollment probabilities — driven by unmeasured health and income. Matching cannot fix that.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation — Overlap Diagnostic First

Run this **before** any estimation. It will tell us whether matching is even feasible:

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler

# ── 1. Estimate propensity scores ────────────────────────────────────────────
covariates = ['age', 'tenure', 'department']
X = df[covariates].values
D = df['treated'].values

# Standardize for better logistic regression convergence
scaler = StandardScaler()
X_scaled = scaler.fit_transform(X)

ps_model = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
ps_model.fit(X_scaled, D)
df['ps'] = ps_model.predict_proba(X_scaled)[:, 1]

# ── 2. Overlap diagnostic ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
treated_ps = df.loc[df['treated'] == 1, 'ps']
control_ps = df.loc[df['treated'] == 0, 'ps']

fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(14, 5))

# Histogram
axes[0].hist(treated_ps, bins=50, alpha=0.6, label='Enrolled (treated)',
             color='steelblue', density=True)
axes[0].hist(control_ps, bins=50, alpha=0.6, label='Not enrolled (control)',
             color='salmon', density=True)
axes[0].set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
axes[0].set_ylabel('Density')
axes[0].set_title('Propensity Score Overlap')
axes[0].legend()

# Empirical CDF — makes separation crystal clear
from statsmodels.distributions.empirical_distribution import ECDF
ecdf_t = ECDF(treated_ps)
ecdf_c = ECDF(control_ps)
x_grid = np.linspace(0, 1, 300)
axes[1].plot(x_grid, ecdf_t(x_grid), label='Enrolled', color='steelblue')
axes[1].plot(x_grid, ecdf_c(x_grid), label='Not enrolled', color='salmon')
axes[1].set_xlabel('Propensity Score')
axes[1].set_ylabel('Cumulative Probability')
axes[1].set_title('CDF — Separation Between Groups')
axes[1].legend()

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig('overlap_diagnostic.png', dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 3. Numerical overlap summary ─────────────────────────────────────────────
print("=== Propensity Score Summary ===")
print(f"Treated  — min: {treated_ps.min():.3f}, median: {treated_ps.median():.3f}, "
      f"max: {treated_ps.max():.3f}")
print(f"Control  — min: {control_ps.min():.3f}, median: {control_ps.median():.3f}, "
      f"max: {control_ps.max():.3f}")

# Common support region
cs_low  = max(treated_ps.min(), control_ps.min())
cs_high = min(treated_ps.max(), control_ps.max())
in_support = df['ps'].between(cs_low, cs_high)

print(f"\nCommon support region: [{cs_low:.3f}, {cs_high:.3f}]")
print(f"Treated units inside common support: "
      f"{in_support[df['treated']==1].sum()} / {(df['treated']==1).sum()} "
      f"({100*in_support[df['treated']==1].mean():.1f}%)")
print(f"Control units inside common support: "
      f"{in_support[df['treated']==0].sum()} / {(df['treated']==0).sum()} "
      f"({100*in_support[df['treated']==0].mean():.1f}%)")

# Fraction with extreme scores
print(f"\nFraction with PS < 0.05: {(df['ps'] < 0.05).mean():.3f}")
print(f"Fraction with PS > 0.95: {(df['ps'] > 0.95).mean():.3f}")
```

---

### What to look for

| Signal | Interpretation |
|---|---|
| Distributions visually separated | Poor overlap — matching extrapolates |
| Common support < 80% of treated | Serious problem — many treated have no comparable controls |
| Large fraction with PS > 0.95 | Those employees always enroll — no valid counterfactual |
| Distributions overlap well | Matching may be feasible — proceed to Stage 3 full estimation |

---

## What To Do Based on Results

**If overlap is poor (which I expect given what you told me):**

The honest answer is that matching cannot reliably estimate the treatment effect here. Your options are:

1. **Restrict the estimand** — only estimate effects for the subset with common support. This is a valid ATT on the *matchable* subgroup, but you must be explicit that it doesn't generalize to all enrollees.
2. **Trim on propensity score** — drop units with PS outside [0.10, 0.90]. Reduces bias from extrapolation, but you're now answering a narrower question.
3. **Reconsider the design** — Is there any variation in *access* to the program (e.g., some departments launched it later)? That could enable DiD or IV. Is there an enrollment threshold based on a score? That could enable RDD. **Either would be far more credible than matching here.**
4. **Add the missing covariates** — Can you get income or health data from HR? Even a proxy (e.g., prior sick days, salary band) would help close the CIA gap.

---

Shall I generate the full estimation code (with trimming and doubly-robust estimation) once you've run the overlap diagnostic? And — is there any chance a stronger design is available, like a phased rollout or an eligibility threshold?

</details>
