### planner_pedagogy_ambiguous

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.3333333333333333, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.611111111111111}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

This is a rich scenario with **two overlapping identification strategies** — and the right choice depends on what "effect on driver earnings" means to you. Let me flag that immediately, then refine.

---

## Preliminary Recommendation

**Primary: Fuzzy RDD** at the driver score threshold of 80, for the driver-level question.
**Secondary: Synthetic Control** at the city level, if you want the aggregate city-wide effect.

Here's why there are two:

| Question | Unit | Best Method |
|---|---|---|
| Does the *new algorithm* increase driver earnings? | Driver | **Fuzzy RDD** (score 80 cutoff) |
| Did the *city-wide rollout* affect average driver earnings? | City | **Synthetic Control** (1 treated city, 49 donors, 12 pre-periods) |

---

## Why Fuzzy RDD is Strong Here

The score-80 threshold is a near-ideal instrument for the driver-level question:

- **Running variable**: driver score (continuous, presumably hard to perfectly manipulate)
- **Cutoff**: 80 (eligibility for early opt-in)
- **Fuzziness**: threshold raises *probability* of treatment (from 0% to ~30%), but doesn't force it — that's exactly fuzzy RDD territory
- **Estimand**: the LATE for drivers near the threshold (compliers who opted in because they crossed 80)

The threshold acts like a naturally occurring encouragement design. Drivers just above 80 vs. just below 80 are plausibly comparable in everything except eligibility.

**Python package**: `rdrobust`

---

## Why Synthetic Control is Right at the City Level

- 1 treated city, 49 donor cities → too few treated units for DiD; SCM is the canonical fix
- 12 pre-treatment months is solid for donor weighting
- You can match on pre-period ride volume, prices, earnings, and driver supply

**Python package**: `scpi`

---

## One Concern to Flag Now

> *30% of eligible drivers opted in*

If you try to estimate the driver-level effect using a simple comparison of opt-in vs. non-opt-in drivers, you'll get **selection bias** — the 30% who opted in likely differ systematically from those who didn't (more entrepreneurial, higher risk tolerance, etc.). That's exactly why the RDD threshold is valuable: it gives you a cleaner comparison that doesn't rely on who chose to opt in.

---

## Before I Build the Full Plan — Two Quick Questions

1. **Which question is primary?** Driver-level earnings (RDD), city-level effect (SCM), or do you want both?

2. **What's the driver score?** Is it a continuous metric drivers can observe and act on, or is it more of an internal rating they don't directly control? This matters for the RDD manipulation test — if drivers can game the score to cross 80, the running variable may not be clean.

</details>

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — specifically a TWFE (two-way fixed effects) panel design with an event study to visualize pre-trends and post-treatment dynamics.

You have a strong setup: 190 control stores, 78+ pre-treatment weeks, and a clear treatment date. That's exactly what DiD needs.

The one thing that could change or complicate this recommendation is **how those 10 stores were selected**. That's the key question I need to ask first:

---

**How were the 10 stores chosen to get the loyalty program first?**

For example:
- Were they picked based on past performance (e.g., struggling stores, or top performers)?
- Were they picked by region, store size, or demographics?
- Was it essentially arbitrary — whoever had the infrastructure ready, or a pilot group with no strong selection logic?

The reason this matters: if the 10 stores were selected *because* of characteristics that also affect their sales trajectory (e.g., already-growing stores, or stores in expanding markets), then the parallel trends assumption — the backbone of DiD — may be under pressure, and we'd need to think carefully about which control stores are valid comparisons.

### rdd_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 1.0, "overall": 0.8888888888888888}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

Great setup — you have everything you need for a clean RDD. Let me walk you through all five stages.

---

## Stage 1: Setup — Confirming Your Design

Here's what I'm reading from your description:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Design** | Sharp RDD (≥ 70 → admitted; deterministic) |
| **Running variable** | `test_score` |
| **Cutoff** | 70 |
| **Treatment** | `scholarship` (honors program admission) |
| **Outcome** | `gpa` |
| **N** | 5,000 students |
| **Language** | R |

One quick note: your column is named `scholarship` but you're studying the **honors program**. Make sure that column captures honors admission, not a separate scholarship award — if those are different things, the treatment variable needs to match your question.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions — Walking Through Each One

Since you're new to RDD, I'll explain *why* each assumption matters before asking if it holds.

---

### Assumption 1 — No manipulation of the running variable ⚠️ *Testable*

**What it means in your context:** Could students influence their own test score to land just above 70? If students who score 68–69 can retake the exam, appeal their grade, or if instructors round up borderline scores, then students just below 70 are *not* comparable to students just above 70 — the ones who made it over are the ones with the motivation or social capital to push themselves over the line.

**Why it matters:** The whole logic of RDD is that students right above and right below the cutoff are essentially identical — they differ by chance, not by ability. Manipulation breaks this.

> **For you:** Can students retake the entrance exam? Do teachers or administrators have any discretion over scores near 70? Is there any rounding of scores?

We'll run a formal test for this (McCrary density test) in the code. If there's a suspicious spike of students at 70–71 in the histogram, that's a red flag.

---

### Assumption 2 — Continuity of potential outcomes *Partially testable*

**What it means in your context:** If the honors program didn't exist, GPA would change *smoothly* as test scores increase through 70 — no jump at the cutoff. The only reason GPA would jump at 70 is the program itself.

**Why it matters:** If something else also changes at 70 (say, students above 70 also get a scholarship, or a different teacher), the jump in GPA isn't cleanly attributable to the honors program.

We test this indirectly by checking whether **pre-determined covariates** (age, prior grades, demographics) are smooth through the cutoff. If those jump, something is off.

> **One concern I have:** Your dataset sample shows only `student_id`, `test_score`, `scholarship`, and `gpa`. Do you have any other student characteristics — demographics, prior GPA, school attended? Without those, we can't run the covariate smoothness test, which is a meaningful gap. If you have them, include them.

---

### Assumption 3 — No compound treatments ⚠️ *Critical to check*

**What it means in your context:** The honors program is the **only** thing that changes at the 70-point threshold. If students above 70 *also* receive a financial scholarship, priority housing, a different instructor pool, or access to tutoring — and those things don't exist below 70 — you'd be estimating the combined effect of all of those, not the honors program alone.

> **For you:** Does admission to the honors program come bundled with other benefits (financial aid, housing, special instructors)? If yes, your estimate captures the full "bundle" — be explicit about that in your write-up.

---

### Assumption 4 — Local identification (not a violation — an interpretation caveat)

**What it means:** Your RDD estimate applies only to students who scored **near 70**. A student who scored 45 or 95 may respond to the honors program very differently. The estimate is the effect at the margin, not the average effect across all students.

**Why it matters for your question:** If the goal is "does the honors program work in general?", RDD gives you a narrow answer: "does it work for students right at the admission threshold?" That's still a policy-relevant question — it tells you what happens to the *marginal admit* — but it's not the full picture.

---

### Assumption summary

| Assumption | Status | Action needed |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | ❓ Check with McCrary test | Ask about retakes/rounding |
| Continuity (potential outcomes) | ❓ Partially testable | Need pre-determined covariates |
| No compound treatments | ❓ Needs subject knowledge | Confirm what the program includes |
| Local identification | ✅ Always holds | Note in interpretation |

No red flags so far — let's move to the code.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Install packages if you haven't already:

```r
install.packages(c("rdrobust", "rddensity", "tidyverse"))
```

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(rdrobust)
library(rddensity)

# ── 0. Setup ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

cutoff <- 70

df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    running_centered = test_score - cutoff,
    treated          = as.integer(test_score >= cutoff)
  )

# Quick summary: how many students on each side, and average GPA?
df %>%
  summarise(
    n_below       = sum(test_score < cutoff),
    n_above       = sum(test_score >= cutoff),
    avg_gpa_below = mean(gpa[test_score < cutoff],  na.rm = TRUE),
    avg_gpa_above = mean(gpa[test_score >= cutoff], na.rm = TRUE)
  )


# ── 1. RD Plot ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Visual inspection: do you actually see a jump in GPA at 70?
# If you can't see it in the plot, it probably isn't there.

rdplot(
  y       = df$gpa,
  x       = df$test_score,
  c       = cutoff,
  title   = "RD Plot: Honors Program and Final GPA",
  x.label = "Entrance Exam Score",
  y.label = "Final GPA"
)


# ── 2. McCrary Density Test ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Tests whether students can manipulate their score around the cutoff.
# H0: density of test_score is continuous at 70 (no sorting).
# A significant result (p < 0.05) warns of manipulation.

density_test <- rddensity(X = df$test_score, c = cutoff)
summary(density_test)

# Visual: does the histogram spike right above 70?
rdplotdensity(density_test, df$test_score,
              title  = "McCrary Density Test — Entrance Exam Score",
              xlabel = "Entrance Exam Score",
              ylabel = "Density")


# ── 3. Main RD Estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# rdrobust uses a local linear regression on each side of the cutoff.
# It automatically selects the MSE-optimal bandwidth (how wide a window
# around 70 to use). The "Robust" row is what you should report — it
# corrects for bias introduced by the bandwidth selection.

rd_est <- rdrobust(
  y = df$gpa,
  x = df$test_score,
  c = cutoff
)

summary(rd_est)

# Extract the key numbers for reporting
cat("RD Estimate (robust):       ", round(rd_est$coef["Robust", ], 3), "\n")
cat("95% CI (robust):            [",
    round(rd_est$ci["Robust", 1], 3), ",",
    round(rd_est$ci["Robust", 2], 3), "]\n")
cat("Optimal bandwidth (h):      ", round(rd_est$bws["h", 1], 2), "\n")
cat("Effective N (left / right): ", rd_est$N_h[1], "/", rd_est$N_h[2], "\n")


# ── 4. Summary Table ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

rd_table <- tibble(
  Term             = "Honors Program",
  Conventional     = rd_est$coef["Conventional", 1],
  `Bias-Corrected` = rd_est$coef["Bias-Corrected", 1],
  Robust           = rd_est$coef["Robust", 1],
  `SE (Robust)`    = rd_est$se["Robust", 1],
  `p-value`        = rd_est$pv["Robust", 1],
  `CI Lower`       = rd_est$ci["Robust", 1],
  `CI Upper`       = rd_est$ci["Robust", 2],
  `BW (h)`         = rd_est$bws["h", 1],
  `N Left`         = rd_est$N_h[1],
  `N Right`        = rd_est$N_h[2]
)

knitr::kable(rd_table, digits = 3,
             caption = "RD Estimate: Effect of Honors Program on Final GPA")
```

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Two checks I'd prioritize for your study:

### Check A — Bandwidth sensitivity
The estimate should not flip sign or lose significance just because you used a slightly wider or narrower window around 70.

```r
# Try 50%, 75%, 100% (optimal), 125%, and 150% of the optimal bandwidth
opt_bw <- rd_est$bws["h", 1]
bw_grid <- opt_bw * c(0.50, 0.75, 1.00, 1.25, 1.50)

bw_results <- map_dfr(bw_grid, function(h) {
  fit <- rdrobust(y = df$gpa, x = df$test_score, c = cutoff, h = h)
  tibble(
    bandwidth = round(h, 2),
    estimate  = fit$coef["Conventional", 1],
    ci_lower  = fit$ci["Robust", 1],
    ci_upper  = fit$ci["Robust", 2]
  )
})

print(bw_results)

ggplot(bw_results, aes(x = bandwidth, y = estimate)) +
  geom_point(size = 3) +
  geom_errorbar(aes(ymin = ci_lower, ymax = ci_upper), width = 0.3) +
  geom_hline(yintercept = 0, linetype = "dashed", color = "grey50") +
  labs(
    title = "Bandwidth Sensitivity: Effect of Honors Program on GPA",
    x     = "Bandwidth around cutoff (exam score points)",
    y     = "RD Estimate"
  ) +
  theme_minimal()
```

### Check B — Placebo cutoffs
Run the exact same RD at cutoffs where there is *no* treatment change (e.g., 50 and 85). You should find no "effect" there. If you do, it suggests the model is picking up something other than the honors program.

```r
placebo_cutoffs <- c(50, 60, 80, 85)

placebo_results <- map_dfr(placebo_cutoffs, function(pc) {
  # Only use students on one side of the true cutoff to avoid contamination
  df_sub <- df %>% filter(test_score < cutoff)  # below-cutoff students only
  fit <- rdrobust(y = df_sub$gpa, x = df_sub$test_score, c = pc)
  tibble(
    placebo_cutoff = pc,
    estimate       = fit$coef["Conventional", 1],
    ci_lower       = fit$ci["Robust", 1],
    ci_upper       = fit$ci["Robust", 2],
    pvalue         = fit$pv["Robust", 1]
  )
})

print(placebo_results)
# Good result: p-values > 0.05 and CIs crossing zero at all placebo cutoffs.
```

---

## Stage 5: How to Interpret Your Results

Once you run the code, here's how to read `summary(rd_est)`:

- **Report the "Robust" row** — it's bias-corrected and has better coverage properties than "Conventional." This is the current best practice (Calonico, Cattaneo & Titiunik 2014).
- The estimate is in **GPA points**. If it's 0.15, the honors program raises final GPA by 0.15 points for students right at the admission threshold.
- The **bandwidth** tells you which students are actually used — e.g., `h = 8` means only students with scores 62–78 enter the analysis. That's your effective sample, not all 5,000.

**Plain-language template** (fill in your numbers):

> *Among students who scored near the 70-point admissions threshold, admission to the honors program increased final GPA by [estimate] points (95% CI: [lower, upper]). This estimate is local to students near the cutoff and may not generalize to students who scored much higher or lower.*

4. Once you have results, `/causal-auditor` can stress-test the design for threats I might have missed.

## Stage 1: Setup ✓

Before diving in — **your data columns don't match your problem description**. The sample shows `student_id`, `test_score`, `scholarship`, `gpa`, but your problem is about **firms, employee count, and revenue**. I'll build everything around your described problem. Please re-share the correct data when ready.

---

Here's what I've extracted from your description:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| Running variable | Number of employees |
| Cutoff | 50 employees |
| Treatment | Subsidy (firms < 50 qualify) |
| Outcome | Firm revenue |
| N | ~10,000 firms |
| Variant | **Sharp RDD** (rule is deterministic) |
| Language | Python |

**Variant confirmed — Sharp RDD.** Treatment is fully determined by the cutoff rule. The manipulation concern doesn't change the design type; it's an assumption threat we'll test directly.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific context.

---

### 1. 🔴 No Manipulation of the Running Variable

**Why it matters here**: Firms control their own employee count reporting. A firm with 52 employees has a direct financial incentive to report 49. If firms are successfully gaming the threshold, the firms just below 50 are *not* comparable to firms just above 50 — those below selected themselves in by manipulating. Your estimate would reflect a mix of "subsidy effect" and "effect of being a firm willing/able to manipulate."

**You already flagged this as a concern — this is the most critical assumption for your study.**

We'll test it with the McCrary/`rddensity` density test. A visible spike in firm counts just below 50 would be a red flag.

---

### 2. ⚠️ Continuity of Potential Outcomes

**Why it matters here**: In the absence of the subsidy, firm revenue should trend smoothly through the 50-employee threshold. If there are other reasons small firms (< 50) differ from larger ones — say, different tax treatment, labor regulations, or loan eligibility that *also* kicks in at 50 — we can't isolate the subsidy's effect.

**Ask yourself**: Is there *any other policy, regulation, or program* that uses a 50-employee cutoff? In many countries, labor law thresholds (mandatory health benefits, works councils, reporting requirements) coincide with round numbers like 50. This would be a **compound treatment** problem.

---

### 3. 🔴 No Other Discontinuities at the Cutoff

**Why it matters here**: The 50-employee threshold is a common regulatory cutoff globally (EU labor law, for instance, uses exactly this threshold for works councils). If another rule *also* changes at 50, you're estimating the combined effect of all those changes, not just the subsidy.

**Action needed**: List any other rules that use a 50-employee cutoff in your jurisdiction.

---

### 4. 🟡 Local Identification

**Why it matters here**: Even if everything works, your estimate applies only to firms *near* 50 employees — say, firms with 35–65 employees. It doesn't tell you what the subsidy would do for a 5-person startup or a 200-person firm. That's fine as long as you frame it correctly.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Testable? |
|---|---|---|
| No manipulation | 🔴 High concern (you flagged it) | ✅ Density test |
| Continuity at cutoff | ⚠️ Needs investigation | ✅ Covariate smoothness |
| No compound treatments | 🔴 Plausible concern (50 is common cutoff) | Partially (check known policies) |
| Local identification | 🟡 Inherent limitation | N/A — interpretation caveat |

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
from rdrobust import rdrobust, rdplot
from rddensity import rddensity, rdplotdensity

# ── 0. Setup ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Adjust these to match your actual column names
df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")

Y = df["revenue"].values           # outcome: firm revenue
X = df["n_employees"].values       # running variable: employee count
cutoff = 50                        # threshold: <50 qualifies for subsidy

# Center the running variable at the cutoff (optional but conventional)
# X_centered = X - cutoff
# Then use c=0. Here we use the raw value and set c=50.

# ── 1. RD Plot — visual inspection ───────────────────────────────────────────
# This is your first sanity check: do you see a visible jump in revenue at 50?
fig = rdplot(Y, X, c=cutoff,
             title="Effect of Subsidy on Firm Revenue",
             x_label="Number of Employees",
             y_label="Firm Revenue")
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("rd_plot.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 2. McCrary Density Test — check for manipulation ─────────────────────────
# NULL hypothesis: density is continuous at the cutoff.
# A significant p-value (< 0.05) + visual spike below 50 = strong manipulation signal.
density_test = rddensity(X, c=cutoff)
print("\n── McCrary Density Test ──")
print(density_test)

# Visual density plot: look for a spike just below 50
fig_density = rdplotdensity(density_test, X,
                             title="Density of Firm Size at Cutoff",
                             xlabel="Number of Employees",
                             ylabel="Density")
plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("density_test.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()

# ── 3. Main RD Estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# rdrobust uses a local polynomial (default: linear) with data-driven
# bandwidth selection (MSE-optimal) and robust bias-corrected inference.
rd_main = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff)
print("\n── Main RD Estimate ──")
print(rd_main)

# ── 4. RD Estimate with Covariates (for precision) ───────────────────────────
# Adding pre-determined firm characteristics reduces residual variance
# and tightens confidence intervals — does NOT change identification.
# Replace with your actual covariate columns.
covs = df[["firm_age", "industry_code", "region"]].values

rd_cov = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, covs=covs)
print("\n── RD Estimate with Covariates ──")
print(rd_cov)

# ── 5. Covariate Smoothness Checks ───────────────────────────────────────────
# Run the RDD with pre-determined firm characteristics as the "outcome."
# These should NOT show a discontinuity at 50.
# A jump in, say, firm_age at the cutoff would suggest sorting.
pre_determined = {
    "firm_age":      df["firm_age"].values,
    "capital_stock": df["capital_stock"].values,   # adjust to your columns
    "industry_code": df["industry_code"].values,
}

print("\n── Covariate Smoothness Checks ──")
for name, covariate in pre_determined.items():
    rd_check = rdrobust(covariate, X, c=cutoff)
    coef  = rd_check.coef.values[0][0]
    pval  = rd_check.pv.values[2][0]   # robust p-value
    flag  = "⚠️  JUMP DETECTED" if pval < 0.05 else "✅ Smooth"
    print(f"  {name}: coef={coef:.4f}, p={pval:.3f}  {flag}")
```

> **Installation**: `pip install rdrobust rddensity` — these are the Python ports of the canonical R packages and use identical estimation routines.

---

## Stage 4: Robustness Checks

Given your manipulation concern, I'd prioritize these two:

```python
# ── Robustness 1: Bandwidth Sensitivity ───────────────────────────────────────
# The optimal bandwidth is data-driven, but we should verify the estimate
# is stable when we use 50%, 75%, 125%, and 150% of the optimal bandwidth.
opt_bw = rd_main.bws.values[0][0]
print(f"\nOptimal bandwidth: {opt_bw:.2f} employees")
print("\n── Bandwidth Sensitivity ──")

for mult in [0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.25, 1.5]:
    bw = opt_bw * mult
    rd_bw = rdrobust(Y, X, c=cutoff, h=bw)
    coef = rd_bw.coef.values[0][0]
    ci_l = rd_bw.ci.values[2][0]   # robust CI lower
    ci_u = rd_bw.ci.values[2][1]   # robust CI upper
    print(f"  h={bw:5.1f} (×{mult:.2f}): coef={coef:.1f}  "
          f"95% CI [{ci_l:.1f}, {ci_u:.1f}]")

# ── Robustness 2: Donut Hole Test ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Exclude firms RIGHT at the cutoff (most likely to be manipulators).
# If the effect survives when we drop firms within ±2 employees of 50,
# it's not driven purely by the manipulators.
print("\n── Donut Hole Test ──")

for donut in [1, 2, 3]:
    mask = np.abs(X - cutoff) > donut
    rd_donut = rdrobust(Y[mask], X[mask], c=cutoff)
    coef  = rd_donut.coef.values[0][0]
    pval  = rd_donut.pv.values[2][0]
    n_dropped = (~mask).sum()
    print(f"  Donut ±{donut}: coef={coef:.1f}, p={pval:.3f}  "
          f"(dropped {n_dropped} firms near cutoff)")

# ── Robustness 3: Placebo Cutoffs ─────────────────────────────────────────────
# Run the RDD at fake cutoffs (30, 40, 60, 70) where no policy changes.
# Finding an "effect" at a placebo cutoff suggests model misspecification.
print("\n── Placebo Cutoffs ──")

for placebo in [30, 40, 60, 70]:
    # Use only firms on one side of the real cutoff to avoid contamination
    if placebo < cutoff:
        mask = X < cutoff
    else:
        mask = X >= cutoff
    try:
        rd_placebo = rdrobust(Y[mask], X[mask], c=placebo)
        coef = rd_placebo.coef.values[0][0]
        pval = rd_placebo.pv.values[2][0]
        flag = "⚠️  SIGNIFICANT" if pval < 0.05 else "✅ Not significant"
        print(f"  Cutoff={placebo}: coef={coef:.1f}, p={pval:.3f}  {flag}")
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"  Cutoff={placebo}: insufficient data — {e}")
```

```
The RDD estimate suggests that receiving the subsidy is associated with
a [COEFFICIENT] change in firm revenue (robust 95% CI: [LOWER, UPPER]).

This is estimated using firms within [BANDWIDTH] employees of the
50-employee cutoff — so it applies to firms of roughly [50 - h] to
[50 + h] employees, not to all firm sizes.

McCrary density test:
  → p = [VALUE]
  → [If p < 0.05]: ⚠️ Evidence of bunching below the cutoff. Some firms
    likely manipulate employee counts to qualify. The donut hole results
    help assess robustness, but interpret with caution.
  → [If p ≥ 0.05]: ✅ No strong evidence of manipulation.
```

### sc_pedagogy_messy

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 1.0, "safety": 1.0, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.8333333333333334}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

You're describing a classic synthetic control setup with one important red flag already on the table. Let me map it out before walking through assumptions:

| Element | Value | Note |
|---|---|---|
| Treated units | 1 country | ✓ SC appropriate |
| Donor pool | 15 countries | ✓ Good pool size |
| Pre-treatment periods | 10 | ⚠️ Borderline minimum |
| Post-treatment periods | 10 | ✓ |
| Outcome | Annual GDP growth | |
| Language | Python (`scpi`) | |
| Key concern | Treated unit is volatile outlier | 🚨 Convex hull risk |

**Variant flag**: The "unusually volatile, different from all donors" detail is the most important thing you've told me. It almost certainly means the treated country sits **outside the convex hull** of your donor pool — meaning no non-negative weighted combination of the 15 donors can reproduce its pre-treatment trajectory. Standard SCM will struggle here. I'll address this concretely in the assumptions below.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

Let me walk through each one for your specific setup.

---

### 1. 🚨 Convex Hull (Critical)

**What it means**: For standard SCM to work, your treated country's pre-treatment GDP growth profile must lie *within the range* spanned by your 15 donors. If it's genuinely more volatile than all of them, no convex (non-negative, sum-to-one) combination of donors can match it — the optimizer will do its best but produce a poor fit regardless.

**Why it matters**: A synthetic control built on extrapolation doesn't give you a valid counterfactual. The post-treatment "gap" will partly reflect the model's inability to match the pre-period, not the carbon tax effect.

**Testable**: Yes — partially. After fitting, check:
1. Is the pre-treatment RMSPE large relative to the outcome's standard deviation?
2. Are donor weights concentrated on 1–2 units (the model "reaching" for the closest approximation)?
3. Do the treated country's pre-period values fall outside the min/max range of donors on key predictors?

**Mitigation for Python**: Unlike R (where `augsynth` handles this via ridge-regularized bias correction), in Python with `scpi` you can relax the weight constraint from `"simplex"` to `"ridge"` or `"L1-L2"`. This allows the optimizer more flexibility at the cost of some interpretability — similar in spirit to augmented SCM. I'll generate both versions so you can compare.

> **Quick check**: Do the 15 donor countries have *any* that were similarly volatile pre-treatment? If so, they're the natural anchors. If not, the convex hull violation is real and we should use relaxed weights.

---

### 2. ⚠️ Adequate Pre-Treatment Fit (Closely Related)

**What it means**: Even setting the convex hull aside, 10 pre-treatment periods is the practical minimum for SCM. You need enough history for the optimizer to find stable weights — and for the permutation-based inference to be reliable (15 donors × poor pre-fit = noisy placebo distribution).

**Why it matters**: Pre-treatment RMSPE is both your fit diagnostic *and* the denominator in the placebo p-value. If it's large, your test has low power.

**Testable**: Yes — this is the first plot we'll produce.

---

### 3. No Interference Between Units

**What it means**: The 15 donor countries weren't themselves affected by the treated country's carbon tax. Given this is a single country implementing a national policy, spillovers to other countries' GDP growth are likely minor — but worth considering if the treated country is a major trading partner of your donors.

**Testable**: Not formally. Argue substantively: are these 15 countries economically isolated enough from the treated country?

> **Question for you**: Is the treated country a large economy (e.g., Germany, China) where a carbon tax might affect global energy prices or trade? Or a smaller economy where spillovers are negligible?

---

### 4. No Anticipation

**What it means**: The treated country's GDP growth didn't start changing *before* the tax was implemented (e.g., firms adjusting investment in anticipation of the policy). Carbon taxes are often debated publicly for years before passage.

**Testable**: Yes — the pre-treatment fit plot will show this. If the gap starts opening 1–2 periods *before* your treatment date, you have anticipation effects and need to adjust your effective treatment date.

---

### 5. No Structural Breaks

**What it means**: The relationship between the treated country and donors should be stable throughout the pre-treatment window. If the treated country's volatility was a *temporary* anomaly (e.g., one severe recession year), that's different from persistent structural differences.

**Why this matters for your case**: If the treated country's volatility is driven by a one-time shock in, say, period 7, the optimizer will up-weight that period in fitting — potentially distorting the weights.

**Testable**: In-time placebo (fake treatment at period 5) — if a gap appears in the pre-period, the model relationship is unstable.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Severity |
|---|---|---|
| No interference | 🟡 Plausible but verify trading relationships | Medium |
| Convex hull | 🔴 Likely violated given volatility description | **Critical** |
| Adequate pre-fit | 🔴 At risk — 10 periods is minimum, volatility compounds this | **Critical** |
| No anticipation | 🟡 Carbon taxes often telegraphed — check pre-period trend | Medium |
| No structural breaks | 🟡 Volatility may indicate breaks — need visual inspection | Medium |

---

## Recommended Approach

Given the two critical flags, I recommend a **two-track implementation**:

1. **Standard SCM** (`scpi` with `simplex` constraints) — to establish the baseline and diagnose the fit problem concretely
2. **Relaxed SCM** (`scpi` with `ridge` or `L1-L2` constraints) — to allow extrapolation and compare estimates

If the two estimates diverge substantially in the post-period, it confirms the standard SCM was biased by the convex hull violation, and the relaxed version is more credible.

---

Before I generate the full implementation, two quick questions:

1. **What period does treatment begin?** (Is period 11 the first post-treatment year, or is there a specific value in the data?)
2. **Do any donor countries share the treated country's volatility profile?** (Even one "similar" donor helps the optimizer significantly.)

Once you confirm, I'll generate the complete Stage 3 code with both SCM variants, pre-fit diagnostics, and the placebo inference setup.

</details>

### timeseries_pedagogy_clean

**Run 1**: {"pedagogy": 0.6666666666666666, "safety": 0.5, "actionable": 0.5, "overall": 0.5555555555555555}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Stage 1: Setup Confirmed

Here's what I'm working with:

| Parameter | Value |
|---|---|
| Pre-treatment observations | 42 months ✓ |
| Post-treatment observations | 6 months |
| Seasonal cycles in pre-period | ~3.5 annual cycles ✓ |
| Control series | None |
| Language | Python |

**Method: Segmented Regression (ITS)** — the right call with no control series. You get a level shift estimate (did revenue jump at launch?) and a slope change estimate (did the growth *rate* change?). I'll also include an ARIMA-based counterfactual as a robustness check.

> ⚠️ **No control series is the main limitation here.** It means we can't partial out concurrent events — anything else that happened 6 months ago (a competitor exiting, a macro shift, a seasonal anomaly) would be confounded with the campaign. Keep that front of mind throughout.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. Pre-period structural break (pre-flight — run this first)

Before modeling anything, check whether your pre-period is itself stable. A level shift or trend change in the pre-period means the model gets fit on two different regimes and the counterfactual projection will be unreliable.

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.patches as mpatches
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import breaks_cusumolsresid
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf

# --- Visual inspection ---
intervention_time = 43  # month where post=1 begins

pre = df[df['post'] == 0].copy()

fig, axes = plt.subplots(2, 1, figsize=(12, 7), sharex=False)

# Full series
axes[0].plot(df['time'], df['outcome'], color='steelblue', linewidth=1.5)
axes[0].axvline(x=intervention_time - 0.5, color='red', linestyle='--',
                label='Campaign launch')
axes[0].set_title('Revenue over time — look for trend breaks or level shifts '
                  'in the PRE period', fontsize=12)
axes[0].set_ylabel('Revenue')
axes[0].legend()

# Pre-period only with trend line
axes[1].scatter(pre['time'], pre['outcome'], s=20, color='steelblue', alpha=0.7)
z = np.polyfit(pre['time'], pre['outcome'], 1)
p = np.poly1d(z)
axes[1].plot(pre['time'], p(pre['time']), color='orange',
             linestyle='--', label='Linear trend')
axes[1].set_title('Pre-period only — does the trend look stable throughout?',
                  fontsize=12)
axes[1].set_ylabel('Revenue')
axes[1].legend()

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# --- CUSUM test for structural break ---
# Fit simple trend to pre-period, then check residuals for instability
model_pre = smf.ols('outcome ~ time', data=pre).fit()
cusum_result = breaks_cusumolsresid(model_pre.resid)

print("=== CUSUM Test for Structural Break ===")
print(f"Test statistic: {cusum_result[0]:.4f}")
print(f"P-value:        {cusum_result[1]:.4f}")
print()
if cusum_result[1] < 0.05:
    print("⚠️  STRUCTURAL BREAK DETECTED in the pre-period (p < 0.05).")
    print("   The pre-period is not stable. Consider truncating to after the break")
    print("   before proceeding — discuss with causal-timeseries advisor.")
else:
    print("✓  No structural break detected (p ≥ 0.05). Safe to proceed.")
```

**Why it matters:** If there's a break in the pre-period, the model learns the wrong baseline and the counterfactual you project into the post-period will be off. This is the single most common silent failure in ITS.

---

### 2. No concurrent confounding events

❌ **Not statistically testable — must be argued substantively.**

Ask yourself: *What else changed 6 months ago that could affect monthly revenue?*
- New product launches? Pricing changes? Macroeconomic shifts?
- Did a competitor enter or exit?
- Seasonal patterns that happen to coincide with the launch date?

Run a placebo test (Stage 4) as supporting evidence, but ultimately this is a knowledge claim, not a statistical one.

---

### 3. Stationarity

```python
from statsmodels.tsa.stattools import adfuller, kpss

pre_series = df.loc[df['post'] == 0, 'outcome'].values

# ADF: H0 = unit root (non-stationary)
adf = adfuller(pre_series, autolag='AIC')
print(f"ADF statistic: {adf[0]:.4f}  |  p-value: {adf[1]:.4f}")
print("  → Stationary" if adf[1] < 0.05 else "  → ⚠️ Possible unit root")

# KPSS: H0 = stationary
kpss_stat, kpss_p, _, _ = kpss(pre_series, regression='c', nlags='auto')
print(f"KPSS statistic: {kpss_stat:.4f}  |  p-value: {kpss_p:.4f}")
print("  → Stationary" if kpss_p > 0.05 else "  → ⚠️ Non-stationary")

# Visual: ACF
from statsmodels.graphics.tsaplots import plot_acf
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))
axes[0].plot(pre_series)
axes[0].set_title('Pre-period Revenue')
plot_acf(pre_series, ax=axes[1], lags=20, title='ACF — should decay quickly')
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
```

**Why it matters:** A trending or random-walk series can produce a spurious "effect" at any intervention date — the post-period is just higher because the series was going up anyway. If you find a unit root, we'll difference the series before modeling.

---

### 4. Pre-period model fit

Tested in Stage 3 after fitting the model. MAPE < 5% is ideal; < 10% is acceptable. If fit is poor, the counterfactual is unreliable regardless of what the p-values say.

---

### 5. Adequate pre-period length

✅ **42 months (~3.5 annual cycles) — adequate.** Just enough to capture seasonal patterns. Worth flagging if the series shows strong seasonality that isn't fully captured.

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from statsmodels.stats.diagnostic import acorr_ljungbox
from scipy.stats import shapiro

# ── 0. Setup ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Assumes: df with columns [time, outcome, post]
# time: 1..48, post: 0 for t<=42, 1 for t>=43
intervention_time = 43

# Slope-change variable: 0 in pre-period, counts up in post-period
# Captures whether the GROWTH RATE changed after the campaign
df['time_since'] = np.maximum(0, df['time'] - (intervention_time - 1))

# ── 1. Fit segmented regression ───────────────────────────────────────────────
# time      → pre-period trend (slope)
# post      → level shift at campaign launch
# time_since→ slope change after campaign launch
model = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post + time_since', data=df).fit(
    cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4}  # corrects for autocorrelation
)
print(model.summary())

# ── 2. Extract key estimates ──────────────────────────────────────────────────
level_shift  = model.params['post']
slope_change = model.params['time_since']
ci_level     = model.conf_int().loc['post']
ci_slope     = model.conf_int().loc['time_since']

print("\n=== Key Estimates ===")
print(f"Level shift at launch:  {level_shift:+.2f}  "
      f"(95% CI: {ci_level[0]:.2f}, {ci_level[1]:.2f})")
print(f"Slope change per month: {slope_change:+.2f}  "
      f"(95% CI: {ci_slope[0]:.2f}, {ci_slope[1]:.2f})")

# ── 3. Build counterfactual ───────────────────────────────────────────────────
# What would revenue have looked like without the campaign?
df_counter = df.copy()
df_counter['post']       = 0
df_counter['time_since'] = 0

df['predicted']      = model.predict(df)
df['counterfactual'] = model.predict(df_counter)
df['point_effect']   = df['predicted'] - df['counterfactual']

# ── 4. Plot ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
fig, axes = plt.subplots(2, 1, figsize=(13, 9))

# Panel 1: Observed vs counterfactual
axes[0].plot(df['time'], df['outcome'],
             color='steelblue', linewidth=1.5, label='Observed revenue')
axes[0].plot(df['time'], df['predicted'],
             color='steelblue', linewidth=1.5, linestyle='--', alpha=0.5,
             label='Model fit')
post_mask = df['post'] == 1
axes[0].plot(df.loc[post_mask, 'time'], df.loc[post_mask, 'counterfactual'],
             color='gray', linewidth=2, linestyle='--',
             label='Counterfactual (no campaign)')
axes[0].fill_between(df.loc[post_mask, 'time'],
                     df.loc[post_mask, 'counterfactual'],
                     df.loc[post_mask, 'outcome'],
                     alpha=0.2, color='green', label='Estimated effect')
axes[0].axvline(x=intervention_time - 0.5, color='red', linestyle='--',
                alpha=0.7, label='Campaign launch')
axes[0].set_title('Observed Revenue vs. Counterfactual', fontsize=13)
axes[0].set_ylabel('Revenue')
axes[0].legend(loc='upper left')

# Panel 2: Point effect over time
axes[1].bar(df.loc[post_mask, 'time'], df.loc[post_mask, 'point_effect'],
            color='green', alpha=0.6, label='Monthly effect')
axes[1].axhline(0, color='black', linewidth=0.8)
axes[1].set_title('Estimated Monthly Campaign Effect', fontsize=13)
axes[1].set_ylabel('Revenue lift')
axes[1].set_xlabel('Month')

plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()

# ── 5. Cumulative effect ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
cumulative = df.loc[post_mask, 'point_effect'].sum()
print(f"\nCumulative effect over 6 post-period months: {cumulative:+.2f}")

# ── 6. Pre-period model fit diagnostics ──────────────────────────────────────
pre_mask = df['post'] == 0
residuals_pre = model.resid[pre_mask]

obs_pre   = df.loc[pre_mask, 'outcome']
pred_pre  = model.predict(df[pre_mask])
mape_pre  = np.mean(np.abs((obs_pre - pred_pre) / obs_pre)) * 100

lb = acorr_ljungbox(residuals_pre, lags=10, return_df=True)
sw_stat, sw_p = shapiro(residuals_pre)

print("\n=== Pre-Period Model Fit ===")
print(f"MAPE: {mape_pre:.2f}%  "
      f"({'✓ Good' if mape_pre < 5 else '~ Acceptable' if mape_pre < 10 else '⚠️ Poor'})")
print(f"Ljung-Box p-value (lag 10): {lb['lb_pvalue'].iloc[-1]:.4f}  "
      f"({'✓ No autocorrelation' if lb['lb_pvalue'].iloc[-1] > 0.05 else '⚠️ Autocorrelation in residuals'})")
print(f"Shapiro-Wilk p-value: {sw_p:.4f}  "
      f"({'✓ Residuals approx normal' if sw_p > 0.05 else '~ Non-normal residuals'})")

# Residual plot
fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 2, figsize=(12, 4))
axes[0].plot(df.loc[pre_mask, 'time'], residuals_pre,
             marker='o', markersize=3, linewidth=0.8, color='steelblue')
axes[0].axhline(0, color='red', linestyle='--')
axes[0].set_title('Pre-Period Residuals')
axes[0].set_xlabel('Month')
axes[0].set_ylabel('Residual')

from statsmodels.graphics.tsaplots import plot_acf
plot_acf(residuals_pre, ax=axes[1], lags=15,
         title='ACF of Pre-Period Residuals')
plt.tight_layout()
plt.show()
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification

Two checks — run both.

**Check 1: Placebo intervention date** — Did the model find an "effect" somewhere in the pre-period where we know there was none? If yes, the model is unstable or something else is driving the result.

```python
# Place a fake intervention at month 21 (midpoint of pre-period)
placebo_time = 21
df['post_placebo']       = (df['time'] >= placebo_time).astype(int)
df['time_since_placebo'] = np.maximum(0, df['time'] - (placebo_time - 1))

# Fit only on data BEFORE the real intervention
pre_only = df[df['time'] < intervention_time].copy()

model_placebo = smf.ols(
    'outcome ~ time + post_placebo + time_since_placebo', data=pre_only
).fit(cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})

p_level_placebo = model_placebo.pvalues['post_placebo']
p_slope_placebo = model_placebo.pvalues['time_since_placebo']

print("=== Placebo Test (fake launch at month 21) ===")
print(f"Level shift p-value:  {p_level_placebo:.4f}  "
      f"({'⚠️ Significant — concern' if p_level_placebo < 0.05 else '✓ Not significant — good'})")
print(f"Slope change p-value: {p_slope_placebo:.4f}  "
      f"({'⚠️ Significant — concern' if p_slope_placebo < 0.05 else '✓ Not significant — good'})")
```

**Check 2: Different pre-period lengths** — Do the estimates change substantially if you shorten or extend the pre-period? Stable results build confidence; large swings suggest the model is sensitive to arbitrary choices.

```python
results = []
for cutoff in [24, 30, 36, 42]:   # varying pre-period lengths
    df_sub = df[df['time'] <= cutoff + 6].copy()  # keep 6 post-period months
    df_sub['post_sub']       = (df_sub['time'] > cutoff).astype(int)
    df_sub['time_since_sub'] = np.maximum(0, df_sub['time'] - cutoff)
    m = smf.ols('outcome ~ time + post_sub + time_since_sub', data=df_sub).fit(
        cov_type='HAC', cov_kwds={'maxlags': 4})
    results.append({
        'pre_months': cutoff,
        'level_shift': m.params['post_sub'],
        'p_level': m.pvalues['post_sub'],
        'slope_change': m.params['time_since_sub'],
    })

print("\n=== Sensitivity to Pre-Period Length ===")
print(pd.DataFrame(results).to_string(index=False))
print("\nLook for: stable level_shift estimates across rows.")
```

The monthly growth rate changed by **[slope_change]** per month after launch (95% CI: **[lower, upper]**). The cumulative estimated revenue lift over 6 post-launch months is **[cumulative]** units.*

<details><summary>Response</summary>

## Pre-Flight Flag: Structural Break in Pre-Period

Before writing a single line of estimation code, this needs to be resolved. A structural break in the pre-period is one of the most common ways an ITS analysis quietly produces wrong answers — the model gets trained on two different data regimes and projects a nonsense counterfactual.

You already suspect months 15–20. Let's verify it formally, then decide how to proceed.

---

### Step 1: Diagnose the Break

```r
library(tidyverse)
library(strucchange)

# --- Load your data (adjust path as needed) ---
df <- read_csv("your_data.csv")

# Restrict to pre-period only — the break we care about is there
pre <- df %>% filter(post == 0)  # months 1–36

# 1. Visual inspection — always first
ggplot(pre, aes(x = time, y = outcome)) +
  geom_line() +
  geom_vline(xintercept = c(15, 20), linetype = "dashed", color = "red", alpha = 0.6) +
  labs(title = "Pre-Period Outcome (months 1–36)",
       subtitle = "Red dashed lines mark suspected break region",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()

# 2. CUSUM test — detects structural instability in OLS fit
cusum_model <- efp(outcome ~ time, data = pre, type = "OLS-CUSUM")
plot(cusum_model, main = "CUSUM Test — Pre-Period")
# A line that exits the boundary bands = significant instability

# 3. Chow test at the suspected break point
# Try a few candidates given your uncertainty about exact month
library(gap)   # or do it manually:

chow_test <- function(df, break_point) {
  df$post_break <- as.integer(df$time >= break_point)
  df$time_x_post <- df$time * df$post_break
  m <- lm(outcome ~ time + post_break + time_x_post, data = df)
  summary(m)
}

# Test each candidate
for (bp in 15:20) {
  cat("\n--- Break at month", bp, "---\n")
  print(chow_test(pre, bp)$coefficients["post_break", ])
}

# 4. Data-driven breakpoint detection (Bai-Perron)
bp_test <- breakpoints(outcome ~ time, data = pre, breaks = 1)
summary(bp_test)
cat("Estimated break at month:", bp_test$breakpoints, "\n")
```

Run this first. The Bai-Perron `breakpoints()` call will give you the data-estimated break location — compare it against what you saw visually and what you know about month 15–20.

---

### Step 2: Choose a Path (Do Not Skip This Decision)

Once you confirm the break, you have two options. Here's the honest math:

| Option | Pre-period obs | Risk |
|--------|---------------|------|
| **Truncate** — use months 21–36 only | **16 observations** | Below the 30-minimum threshold. Very wide CIs, poor power. |
| **Model the break explicitly** — include a methodology-change dummy | 36 observations | Break must be correctly specified; adds parameters |

Given that truncating leaves you with only 16 pre-period observations, **modeling the break explicitly is almost certainly the right call** — unless the data before the break is so corrupted that it contains no useful signal about the true trend.

The key question to answer before choosing: *Was the methodology change a one-time level shift, or did it also change the slope of the trend?* If you shifted from, say, a different sampling frame, it's probably a level shift. If you changed what you're measuring, the slope may have changed too.

---

### Step 3: Implementation (Assuming Explicit Break Modeling)

Once you confirm the break month (let's say it was month `b`):

```r
# --- ITS with methodology-change break modeled explicitly ---
# Using month 37 as intervention, month b as methodology break

b <- 17  # <-- replace with your confirmed break month

df <- df %>%
  mutate(
    # Methodology change dummy (level shift at month b)
    method_change   = as.integer(time >= b),
    # Slope change after methodology break (optional — add if slope also changed)
    time_since_method = pmax(0, time - b + 1),

    # Treatment variables (the regulation — what you want to estimate)
    time_since_tx   = pmax(0, time - 37 + 1)
  )

# Model A: level shift only at methodology break
model_a <- lm(
  outcome ~ time + method_change + post + time_since_tx,
  data = df
)

# Model B: level + slope shift at methodology break (if warranted)
model_b <- lm(
  outcome ~ time + method_change + time_since_method + post + time_since_tx,
  data = df
)

# Use HAC standard errors — serial correlation is expected in monthly data
library(sandwich)
library(lmtest)

coeftest(model_a, vcov = vcovHAC(model_a))
coeftest(model_b, vcov = vcovHAC(model_b))

# Compare models
AIC(model_a, model_b)
BIC(model_a, model_b)

# Visualize: observed vs fitted
df$fitted_a <- fitted(model_a)

ggplot(df, aes(x = time)) +
  geom_line(aes(y = outcome), color = "black") +
  geom_line(aes(y = fitted_a), color = "steelblue", linetype = "dashed") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = b,  color = "orange", linetype = "dashed") +
  geom_vline(xintercept = 37, color = "red",    linetype = "dashed") +
  annotate("text", x = b + 0.5,  y = max(df$outcome), label = "Method change", hjust = 0, color = "orange") +
  annotate("text", x = 37 + 0.5, y = max(df$outcome), label = "Regulation",    hjust = 0, color = "red") +
  labs(title = "ITS with Methodology Break Controlled",
       x = "Month", y = "Outcome") +
  theme_minimal()
```

The coefficients you'll interpret:
- **`post`** → level shift at month 37 (immediate regulation effect)
- **`time_since_tx`** → slope change after month 37 (does the trend change?)
- **`method_change`** → the methodology artifact (nuisance parameter — controls for it, don't interpret causally)

---

### Before Moving Forward

Please run the diagnostics in Step 1 and come back with:
1. The confirmed break month (Bai-Perron output)
2. Whether you think it was a level-only or level+slope shift
3. Whether the pre-break data (months 1–14) is meaningful or essentially from a different measurement instrument

That determines whether Model A or B is the right spec — and whether we need to discuss truncation as a fallback anyway.

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Great setup — this is a **classic single-cohort DiD** (all treated stores switch at the same time), so we avoid the staggered TWFE pitfalls entirely. Here's the full 5-stage walkthrough.

---

## Stage 1: Study Design ✅

| Element | Your Study |
|---|---|
| **Units** | 100 stores (50 treated, 50 control) |
| **Time** | 24 months |
| **Treatment** | New checkout system, starts month 12 |
| **Outcome** | Sales |
| **Variant** | Classic TWFE DiD (single treatment date) |

Since all treated stores switch at the **same** time, standard TWFE is unbiased — no need for Callaway-Sant'Anna here.

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

I'll walk through each one for your specific setup.

---

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Most Important*

**What it means here:** Without the new checkout system, your 50 treated stores and 50 control stores would have followed similar sales *trends* month-over-month. They can have different sales *levels* (a fancy store vs. a small store is fine), but the trajectory must be parallel.

**Why it matters:** If treated stores were already on an upward trajectory before month 12 (e.g., they were higher-performing stores that were chosen first), DiD will incorrectly attribute that pre-existing growth to the checkout system.

**How to check:** Event study plot — the pre-treatment coefficients (months 1–11) should hover near zero with no visible trend.

**Plausibility check for you:** Were stores selected for early rollout based on their sales performance? If yes, this is a concern.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ⚠️ *Check Carefully*

**What it means here:** Stores didn't change their sales behavior *before* month 12 in anticipation of the new system. For example, if managers knew the system was coming and ran promotions or changed staffing in months 10–11, those changes would bleed into your "pre-treatment" period.

**Why it matters:** If anticipation exists, your pre-trend test will fail, and the baseline is contaminated.

**Plausibility check for you:** Was there a pre-installation period where staff were trained or systems were tested? If stores knew months in advance, flag this.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Likely Fine*

**What it means here:** Stores shouldn't enter or exit your dataset because of the treatment. If the new system caused some stores to close (or be excluded from reporting), you'd have survivorship bias.

**Why it matters:** If only "surviving" treated stores remain in month 24, the effect estimate is too optimistic.

**Plausibility check:** Verify you have all 100 stores across all 24 months (a balanced panel).

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ⚠️ *Worth Thinking About*

**What it means here:** Customers of treated stores shouldn't shift to or from nearby control stores because of the system. If a faster checkout at a treated store pulls customers away from an adjacent control store, the control group's sales are *depressed by the treatment* — making the effect look bigger than it is.

**Why it matters:** Retail is particularly vulnerable to this if treated and control stores are geographically proximate.

**Plausibility check:** Are treated and control stores interleaved geographically, or are they in different regions?

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Testable | Run event study (code below) |
| No anticipation | ⚠️ Context-dependent | Check if stores knew in advance |
| Stable composition | ✅ Likely fine | Verify balanced panel |
| No spillovers | ⚠️ Retail risk | Check geographic proximity |
| Functional form | ✅ Not an issue | Single cohort — TWFE is fine |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

Here's the complete analysis. Adapt the variable names to match your data.

```r
# ── 0. Packages ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
library(tidyverse)
library(fixest)
library(modelsummary)

# ── 1. Data Prep ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Expected columns: store_id, month, sales, treated
# `treated` = 1 for stores that received the new checkout system, 0 for control
# It should be time-INVARIANT (1 for treated stores in ALL months)

df <- df |>
  mutate(
    post          = as.integer(month >= 12),         # Post-treatment indicator
    treated_post  = treated * post,                   # DiD interaction term
    time_to_treat = month - 12                        # Relative time (0 = treatment month)
  )

# Sanity check: balanced panel?
df |>
  count(store_id) |>
  summarise(
    n_stores       = n(),
    min_obs        = min(n),
    max_obs        = max(n),
    is_balanced    = min(n) == max(n)
  )

# ── 2. Main DiD Estimate ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# IMPORTANT: Do NOT include `treated` as a regressor — store fixed effects
# already absorb all time-invariant store characteristics.
# The `treated_post` interaction is fine because it varies over time.

model_did <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post | store_id + month,  # Store FE + Month FE
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id                        # Cluster SEs at store level
)

summary(model_did)

# ── 3. Results Table ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
modelsummary(
  model_did,
  stars    = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  gof_map  = c("nobs", "r.squared"),
  title    = "DiD Estimate: Effect of New Checkout System on Sales"
)

# ── 4. Effect Size with 95% CI ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
coef_est  <- coef(model_did)["treated_post"]
ci        <- confint(model_did, level = 0.95)["treated_post", ]
baseline  <- df |> filter(treated == 1, post == 0) |> pull(sales) |> mean()

cat(glue::glue("
  ── DiD Results ──────────────────────────────────
  ATT estimate : {round(coef_est, 2)}
  95% CI       : [{round(ci[1], 2)}, {round(ci[2], 2)}]
  Baseline avg (treated, pre): {round(baseline, 2)}
  Effect size  : {round(coef_est / baseline * 100, 1)}% of pre-treatment sales
  ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
"))

# ── 5. Event Study (Pre-Trends + Dynamic Effects) ──────────────────────────────
# Reference period = month 11 (one period before treatment)
model_es <- feols(
  sales ~ i(time_to_treat, ref = -1) | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

iplot(
  model_es,
  main  = "Event Study: Effect of New Checkout System on Sales",
  xlab  = "Months Relative to Treatment (0 = Treatment Month)",
  ylab  = "Estimated Sales Effect (vs. Month 11)",
  col   = "steelblue"
)
abline(v = -0.5, col = "red", lty = 2)   # Treatment onset line
abline(h = 0,    col = "gray", lty = 2)  # Zero line
```

**What to look for in the event study plot:**
- **Pre-treatment (left of red line):** Coefficients should hover near zero with overlapping confidence intervals — this supports parallel trends.
- **Post-treatment (right of red line):** A jump indicates the treatment effect. A gradual ramp-up suggests the checkout system took time to boost sales.

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness

Run at least one of these checks:

```r
# ── Robustness 1: Placebo Test (Fake Treatment Date) ───────────────────────────
# Restrict to PRE-TREATMENT data only and pretend treatment happened at month 6.
# If DiD finds an "effect," your pre-trends aren't actually flat.

df_pre <- df |>
  filter(month < 12) |>
  mutate(
    fake_post        = as.integer(month >= 6),
    fake_treated_post = treated * fake_post
  )

model_placebo <- feols(
  sales ~ fake_treated_post | store_id + month,
  data    = df_pre,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

summary(model_placebo)
# Desired result: coefficient near zero and NOT significant

# ── Robustness 2: Pre-Trends Joint Test ────────────────────────────────────────
# Formally test H0: all pre-treatment event study coefficients = 0
pre_periods <- grep("time_to_treat::-", names(coef(model_es)), value = TRUE)
wald(model_es, keep = pre_periods)
# Desired result: p-value > 0.05 (fail to reject that pre-trends = 0)

# ── Robustness 3: Specification Check ─────────────────────────────────────────
# Compare: with and without additional controls (e.g., store size, region)
# If the effect is stable across specs, that's reassuring.
# model_controls <- feols(sales ~ treated_post + store_size | store_id + month,
#                         data = df, cluster = ~store_id)
# modelsummary(list("No controls" = model_did, "With controls" = model_controls))
```

## Stage 5: Interpretation Template

Once you have results, here's how to write them up:

> The DiD estimate suggests the new checkout system **increased monthly sales by `[coef]` units** (95% CI: `[lower, upper]`), representing approximately **`[X]%`** of pre-treatment baseline sales among treated stores.
>
> The event study shows no evidence of pre-existing trends between treated and control stores in the 11 months before rollout, supporting the parallel trends assumption. The effect appears **[immediately / gradually]** after treatment onset and **[stabilizes / continues growing]** through month 24.
>
> **Caveats:** *(1)* If treated stores were selected based on sales performance, parallel trends may not hold despite the visual test. *(2)* If treated and control stores share geographic markets, spillover effects could inflate the estimate.

--- Step 2 ---
No existing plan file — I'm auditing the analysis output directly. Here's my full assessment.

---

# Audit Report: Checkout System DiD

**Date:** 2026-04-01
**Method audited:** Difference-in-Differences (Classic single-cohort TWFE)
**Overall assessment:** 🟡 **Yellow** — the core design is sound but five substantive threats were unaddressed. None are automatically fatal, but two could seriously bias results if the underlying conditions hold.

---

## Summary

The method skill correctly diagnosed the staggered-TWFE non-issue and provided a solid event study implementation. However, it missed a potentially serious seasonal confound, provided no pre-treatment balance check, left the treatment-assignment clustering level ambiguous, and gave no guidance on functional form or statistical power. These are fixable — but the analysis shouldn't be reported until at least the seasonality and balance issues are resolved.

---

## Findings

---

### 🔴 Serious: Seasonality confound — "month" is doing double duty

**Category:** Assumption Violations / Data Threats

**Explanation:** The model uses `| store_id + month` fixed effects. Month FEs absorb *common* seasonal shocks — but only if treated and control stores face the same seasonal patterns. In retail, this often isn't true: a ski-town store and a beach-town store have opposite seasonality. More importantly, the analysis never clarifies whether `month` is a **sequential index** (1–24) or a **calendar month** (Jan–Dec repeated twice).

If it's a calendar month, there's a subtler problem: treatment turns on at observation-period month 12, but the post-treatment window always covers the *same* calendar months across both years. This is fine if the world was stable, but it means you can't separate the treatment effect from any macro event that hit in that calendar period (a competitor's closure, a supply chain disruption, COVID-era patterns). Common time FEs won't save you here because the treated and control groups experienced the *same* calendar months — the confound isn't differential, it's that the treatment cohort is collinear with calendar time.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
# Step 1: clarify your time variable
# Is `month` sequential (1, 2, ..., 24) or calendar (1–12 repeated)?
df |> distinct(month) |> arrange(month) |> print()

# Step 2: if calendar month, add a year variable and use year x month FEs
df <- df |>
  mutate(
    year_month = paste0(year, "_", sprintf("%02d", month))
  )
# Then your FE spec becomes: | store_id + year_month

# Step 3: if stores have different seasonality by region/format,
# test whether treated vs. control stores show different pre-period seasonality
df |>
  filter(post == 0) |>
  group_by(treated, month) |>
  summarise(avg_sales = mean(sales), .groups = "drop") |>
  ggplot(aes(x = month, y = avg_sales, color = factor(treated))) +
  geom_line() +
  labs(title = "Pre-Period Seasonality: Treated vs. Control",
       color = "Treated") +
  theme_minimal()
# Concern: if the two lines have different shapes (not just different levels),
# common month FEs won't fully control for seasonality.
```

**Suggested fix:** Clarify whether `month` is calendar or sequential. If calendar and there's evidence of differential seasonality, include store-type × calendar-month fixed effects, or restrict the comparison to stores with similar seasonal profiles (e.g., same retail format or geographic region).

---

### 🔴 Serious: No pre-treatment balance check

**Category:** Identification Threats

**Explanation:** The event study tests whether pre-treatment *trends* were parallel, but it does not test whether treated and control stores were comparable in the pre-period on observable dimensions. Even with parallel trends visually, a large difference in pre-treatment sales *levels*, growth trajectories, or store characteristics should raise flags — it increases the plausibility that unobservables also differ.

The analysis asks "were stores selected based on sales performance?" but provides no code to actually check this. If the 50 treated stores were the chain's highest-performing locations (e.g., the rollout was resource-intensive and deployed to flagships first), the parallel trends assumption is under real pressure even if the event study looks okay.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
# Pre-treatment covariate balance
pre_summary <- df |>
  filter(post == 0) |>
  group_by(store_id, treated) |>
  summarise(
    mean_sales   = mean(sales),
    trend_sales  = coef(lm(sales ~ month))[2],   # Pre-period slope
    .groups = "drop"
  )

pre_summary |>
  group_by(treated) |>
  summarise(
    n             = n(),
    avg_sales     = mean(mean_sales),
    sd_sales      = sd(mean_sales),
    avg_trend     = mean(trend_sales),
    sd_trend      = sd(trend_sales)
  )

# Standardised mean difference for sales level
smd <- function(x, g) {
  (mean(x[g == 1]) - mean(x[g == 0])) /
    sqrt((var(x[g == 1]) + var(x[g == 0])) / 2)
}

smd(pre_summary$mean_sales, pre_summary$treated)
# Rule of thumb: |SMD| < 0.1 suggests balance; > 0.25 is a concern
```

**Suggested fix:** Run the balance check above *before* interpreting the event study. If SMD for baseline sales is large, consider matching or reweighting to find a more comparable control group, or add the pre-treatment level as a covariate.

---

### 🔴 Serious: Clustering level may be wrong

**Category:** Statistical Threats

**Explanation:** The analysis clusters standard errors at `store_id`. This is correct *if* treatment was independently assigned to each store. But in most retail rollouts, a regional manager, district director, or implementation team decides which batch of stores gets the system first — meaning treatment was effectively assigned at the **district or region level**. If that's the case, clustering at the store level understates your standard errors (stores within the same region share common shocks and selection), and your p-values will be too small.

With 100 stores but potentially only 5–10 regions, this can dramatically change inference.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
# If you have a region or district variable:
library(fixest)

# Cluster at the higher level
model_region_cluster <- feols(
  sales ~ treated_post | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~region_id    # or district_id
)

# Compare SEs
modelsummary(
  list("Cluster: Store" = model_did,
       "Cluster: Region" = model_region_cluster),
  stars  = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  title  = "SE Sensitivity: Clustering Level"
)
# If the SE roughly doubles, your inference was overstated.

# If clusters are truly few (<20), use wild cluster bootstrap instead
library(fwildclusterboot)
boot_res <- boottest(model_did,
                     clustid = "region_id",
                     param   = "treated_post",
                     B       = 9999)
summary(boot_res)
```

**Suggested fix:** Find out *how* the 50 treated stores were selected — was it store-by-store or batch/region? If batch, re-cluster at that level. If you have fewer than ~20 clusters at the higher level, use wild cluster bootstrap rather than asymptotic cluster SEs.

---

### 🟠 Serious: Sales in levels — functional form of the outcome unaddressed

**Category:** Statistical Threats

**Explanation:** The analysis regresses `sales` directly in levels. Retail sales data is almost always right-skewed (a few flagship stores with dramatically higher revenue), and absolute effects in levels are harder to interpret than percentage effects. Two specific problems:

1. **Outliers dominate:** A few high-sales treated stores can drive the entire estimate. The DiD coefficient could be $50K/month because of two flagship stores, while the median treated store saw $200/month — a very different story.

2. **Interpretability:** "The checkout system increased sales by 3,200 units" is harder to interpret than "by 4.2%." Log specification gives you the percentage effect directly.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
# Check skewness of sales
df |>
  filter(post == 0) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = sales)) +
  geom_histogram(bins = 40) +
  facet_wrap(~treated, labeller = label_both) +
  labs(title = "Pre-Period Sales Distribution by Group") +
  theme_minimal()

# Run log-sales specification alongside levels
model_log <- feols(
  log(sales) ~ treated_post | store_id + month,
  data    = df,
  cluster = ~store_id
)

modelsummary(
  list("Levels" = model_did, "Log" = model_log),
  stars  = c("*" = 0.1, "**" = 0.05, "***" = 0.01),
  coef_map = c("treated_post" = "Treatment × Post")
)
# Log coefficient ≈ percentage effect (× 100)
# If level and log estimates tell very different stories, outliers matter.
```

**Suggested fix:** Always run both levels and log specifications. If the distribution is skewed or if sales include zeros, prefer log(sales + 1) or an inverse hyperbolic sine transformation (`asinh(sales)`). Report whichever is more economically interpretable for your context.

---

### 🟠 Serious: No power analysis — null result is uninterpretable

**Category:** Statistical Threats

**Explanation:** The analysis provides no power calculation. With 100 stores and 11 pre-treatment periods, what's the smallest effect this study can reliably detect? If the checkout system produced a 1% lift in sales but the study is only powered to detect a 10% lift, a non-significant result means nothing — it doesn't tell you the system failed, just that the study was too small to see it.

This is especially important for a business decision: the company presumably has a break-even threshold (e.g., "the system must increase sales by at least X to justify the cost"). That threshold should be specified before analysis, and the study's power to detect it should be confirmed.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
library(pwr)

# Back-of-envelope power for DiD (approximately a two-sample t-test)
# after absorbing fixed effects
# Assume: 50 treated stores, 12 post-periods per store
# Estimate within-store SD from pre-period data
within_sd <- df |>
  filter(post == 0) |>
  group_by(store_id) |>
  mutate(demeaned = sales - mean(sales)) |>
  pull(demeaned) |>
  sd()

baseline_mean <- df |>
  filter(treated == 1, post == 0) |>
  pull(sales) |>
  mean()

cat(glue::glue("
  Within-store SD (pre-period): {round(within_sd, 1)}
  Baseline mean (treated, pre): {round(baseline_mean, 1)}
"))

# MDE at 80% power, alpha = 0.05, 50 treated vs 50 control stores
# (simplified — actual DiD MDE depends on ICC and T)
mde <- power.t.test(n = 50, sd = within_sd / sqrt(12),
                    sig.level = 0.05, power = 0.80)$delta
cat(glue::glue("
  Approximate MDE: {round(mde, 1)} ({round(mde/baseline_mean*100, 1)}% of baseline)
"))
```

**Suggested fix:** Calculate the MDE before interpreting results. Compare it to the minimum economically meaningful effect. If the MDE is larger than the business threshold, the study is underpowered and a null result should be reported as "inconclusive," not "no effect."

---

### 🔵 Minor: Anticipation test is conflated with pre-trends test

**Category:** Assumption Violations

**Explanation:** The method skill uses a placebo test at month 6 (halfway through the pre-period) to check for anticipation. This actually tests for *pre-trends at month 6*, not for anticipation. Anticipation would show up specifically in the **2–3 months just before treatment** (t = −2, −3 in the event study) as stores ramp up activity ahead of the system launch — not back at month 6.

If managers knew the system was coming 3 months in advance and ran promotions or hired extra staff in months 9–11, the placebo at month 6 would look clean while anticipation is real.

**Diagnostic:**

```r
# Look at event study coefficients specifically at t = -2 and t = -3
# (already produced by model_es — this is about where to look, not new code)

# Extract and inspect the pre-treatment coefficients near treatment
coef_table <- broom::tidy(model_es, conf.int = TRUE) |>
  filter(str_detect(term, "time_to_treat")) |>
  mutate(period = as.integer(str_extract(term, "-?\\d+"))) |>
  arrange(period)

# Flag coefficients at t = -2, -3 specifically
coef_table |>
  filter(period %in% -3:-2) |>
  select(period, estimate, conf.low, conf.high, p.value)
# Concern: if these are significant AND in the same direction as post-period
# coefficients, anticipation is likely — not just pre-trends.
```

**Suggested fix:** In addition to the joint pre-trends test, explicitly inspect and report the t = −2 and t = −3 coefficients in the context of anticipation. Ask whether stores received official notice 2–3 months before go-live.

---

### 🔵 Minor: Estimand not stated — ATT vs. ATE matters for interpretation

**Category:** External Validity Threats

**Explanation:** The analysis never explicitly states that the estimand is the **ATT** (Average Treatment Effect on the Treated) — the effect for *these specific 50 stores*. If the company wants to know what would happen if the system rolled out to *all* stores (ATE), and if the 50 treated stores were selected because they were most likely to benefit (e.g., high-traffic locations), the ATT will overstate the ATE.

This isn't a bug in the analysis — ATT is the right estimand for an ROI calculation on the treated stores — but it needs to be stated explicitly so the results aren't over-generalized.

**Suggested fix:** Add one sentence to the write-up: "This estimate is the ATT — the average effect among the stores that received the system. If treated stores were selected based on expected benefit, this estimate likely exceeds what a full rollout to all stores would achieve."

---

## Recommendations (Prioritized)

| Priority | Action |
|---|---|
| **1 — Do first** | Clarify whether `month` is sequential or calendar. Plot pre-period seasonality by group. |
| **2 — Do first** | Run pre-treatment balance check (SMD for sales level and pre-trend). |
| **3 — Before reporting** | Find out how the 50 stores were selected. Re-cluster at the assignment level if batch/regional. |
| **4 — Before reporting** | Run log-sales alongside levels. Report both; explain the difference if they diverge. |
| **5 — Before reporting** | Calculate MDE. If it exceeds your business threshold, label null results "inconclusive." |
| **6 — Nice to have** | Inspect event study at t = −2, −3 explicitly for anticipation (not just joint pre-trends test). |
| **7 — In write-up** | State explicitly that the estimand is the ATT. |

---

## What the Method Skill Got Right

Worth noting: the single-cohort TWFE choice is correct (no staggered pitfalls), the event study implementation is clean, the `iplot` reference period at t = −1 is appropriate, and the spillover concern was flagged qualitatively. The audit is building on a solid foundation — these are refinements, not a teardown.

> ### DiD: Seasonality confound not flagged for single-cohort designs
> **Layer**: Auditor
> **Trigger**: Single-cohort DiD with monthly panel data, retail context
> **Mistake**: Skill included month FEs but did not flag that common month FEs
>   only absorb shared seasonality — differential seasonality across store types
>   or geographic regions is uncontrolled. Also did not prompt clarification of
>   whether `month` is sequential vs. calendar.
> **Rule**: Always ask whether `month` is calendar or sequential. Plot pre-period
>   seasonality by group. Warn if treated/control stores differ by format or region.
> **Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
