--- Step 1 ---
Here's your exercise:

---

## 📋 Scenario: RetailCo Store Layout Test

You're a data analyst at **RetailCo**, a mid-size retail chain with 100 stores across the country. In **month 13**, the operations team rolled out a redesigned store layout in 50 stores — wider aisles, better product placement, improved signage. The other 50 stores kept their original layout.

Your manager wants to know: **Did the new layout increase monthly revenue?**

You have 24 months of store-level revenue data (months 1–24), with the layout change beginning in month 13 for the treated stores. All stores were chosen for the pilot based on geography (not performance), so the assignment is effectively as-good-as-random conditional on store fixed effects.

---

## 🐍 Data Generation Code

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

np.random.seed(303)
n_stores = 100
n_months = 24
treat_month = 13

store_id = np.repeat(np.arange(1, n_stores + 1), n_months)
month    = np.tile(np.arange(1, n_months + 1), n_stores)

# First 50 stores received the new layout
treated = (store_id <= 50).astype(int)
post    = (month >= treat_month).astype(int)

# Store-level fixed effects (some stores are just higher-revenue)
store_fe = np.repeat(np.random.normal(0, 3, n_stores), n_months)

# Shared upward time trend (industry-wide growth)
time_trend = 0.2 * month

# Revenue in thousands of dollars
revenue = (
    50
    + store_fe
    + time_trend
    + 5.0 * treated * post          # <-- treatment effect
    + np.random.normal(0, 2, n_stores * n_months)
)

df = pd.DataFrame({
    "store_id": store_id,
    "month":    month,
    "treated":  treated,
    "post":     post,
    "revenue":  np.round(revenue, 2),
})

df.to_csv("exercise_data.csv", index=False)
print(df.head(10))
print(f"\nShape: {df.shape}")
print(f"Treated stores: {df[df['treated']==1]['store_id'].nunique()}")
print(f"Control stores: {df[df['treated']==0]['store_id'].nunique()}")
```

Run this to generate **`exercise_data.csv`** — 2,400 rows (100 stores × 24 months).

---

## ✅ True Treatment Effect

The new layout caused a **+$5,000/month increase in revenue** per treated store (ATT = 5.0 in the thousands-of-dollars units in the data).

---

## 🎯 Your Task

Using `exercise_data.csv`, estimate the ATT using **difference-in-differences**. A few things to try:

1. **Manual 2×2 DiD** — compute the four group means (treated/control × pre/post) and apply the DiD formula by hand. Does it come close to 5.0?
2. **Regression DiD** — run a regression with `treated × post` as your interaction term. Does the coefficient match?
3. **TWFE regression** — add store and month fixed effects. Does precision improve? Why?
4. **Pre-trends check** — plot average revenue over time for treated vs. control stores. Do the pre-treatment trends look parallel? This is the key identifying assumption.

> 💡 **Why parallel trends?** DiD attributes the post-treatment divergence between groups entirely to the treatment. If treated and control stores were already diverging *before* month 13, your estimate picks up that pre-existing difference too — so the answer would be wrong even with perfect data. Checking pre-trends is how you stress-test the assumption.

---

When you've run your analysis, share your estimate and I'll compare it to the ground truth and walk through what worked and what didn't.

--- Step 2 ---
## RetailCo DiD Analysis

Good setup to work from — single treatment date, balanced panel, clear treatment/control split. Let me walk through this properly.

---

## Stage 1: Design Confirmation

| Element | Details |
|---|---|
| **Treatment** | New store layout (month 13 onward) |
| **Units** | 100 stores (50 treated, 50 control) |
| **Outcome** | Monthly revenue (thousands $) |
| **Structure** | Panel: 100 stores × 24 months = 2,400 rows |
| **Variant** | Single treatment date → TWFE with store + month fixed effects |
| **Assignment** | Geography-based → effectively as-good-as-random |

---

## Stage 2: Assumptions

### 1. Parallel Trends ⚠️ *Partially testable — critical*

**What it means here**: Without the layout change, treated stores (1–50) and control stores (51–100) would have followed the same revenue growth trajectory. Their pre-month-13 trends should move together.

**Why it matters**: DiD attributes *all* post-treatment divergence between groups to the layout change. If treated stores were already growing faster before month 13, your estimate inflates the true effect — even with clean data.

**Testable**: Yes, on the pre-treatment period (months 1–12). The event study plot (Task 4) is your primary diagnostic. Pre-treatment coefficients should hover near zero.

**Plausibility here**: Strong. Geography-based assignment means stores weren't cherry-picked based on performance. No reason to expect treated stores were trending differently before rollout.

---

### 2. No Anticipation ✅ *Likely satisfied*

**What it means here**: Store managers and customers didn't change behavior *before* month 13 in anticipation of the layout change. No early renovations, no pre-announced promotions tied to the rollout.

**Why it matters**: If treated stores started improving in month 11–12 because staff were prepping, the "pre-treatment" period is contaminated — your DiD comparison is off.

**Plausibility here**: Strong. Physical layout changes require construction — hard to anticipate in revenue. And your event study pre-coefficients (months 1–12) should show no upward ramp before month 13.

---

### 3. Stable Composition ✅ *Satisfied by design*

**What it means here**: No stores opened or closed because of the layout change. The same 100 stores are observed all 24 months.

**Why it matters**: If struggling stores closed after the renovation (survivorship bias), you'd only observe the "surviving" treated stores — inflating the estimated effect.

**Plausibility here**: Very strong. You have a balanced panel (2,400 = 100 × 24). No attrition issue.

---

### 4. No Spillovers (SUTVA) ⚠️ *Worth flagging*

**What it means here**: The layout change in treated stores doesn't affect revenue at nearby control stores. If a treated store improves and draws customers away from a geographically close control store, the control group is contaminated.

**Why it matters**: If spillovers exist, the control group no longer represents the untreated counterfactual — DiD underestimates the true effect (control stores look worse than they would in a world with no treated stores).

**Plausibility here**: Moderate concern. Retail has real substitution effects. If treated/control stores are in the same metro areas, foot traffic could shift. Geography-based assignment makes this possible. Worth noting as a caveat, not fatal.

---

### 5. Correct Functional Form ✅ *Not a concern here*

**What it means here**: You have a single treatment date — all treated stores switched in month 13. There's no staggered rollout (different cohorts treated at different times).

**Why it matters**: Staggered adoption breaks naive TWFE because it uses already-treated units as controls for later-treated units. That's not your situation.

**Plausibility here**: Clean. Single date → standard TWFE is unbiased.

---

### Assumption Summary

| Assumption | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Parallel trends | ⚠️ Check pre-trends plot | Geography assignment is favorable |
| No anticipation | ✅ Likely satisfied | Physical construction limits anticipation |
| Stable composition | ✅ Satisfied | Balanced panel confirmed |
| No spillovers | ⚠️ Monitor | Geographic proximity could cause substitution |
| Correct functional form | ✅ Not a concern | Single treatment date |

---

## Stage 3: Implementation

```python
import numpy as np
import pandas as pd
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import matplotlib.ticker as mticker
from linearmodels.panel import PanelOLS
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
from scipy import stats

# ── Load data ────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
df = pd.read_csv("exercise_data.csv")

TREAT_MONTH = 13
TRUE_ATT    = 5.0

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# TASK 1: Manual 2×2 DiD
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
means = (df.groupby(["treated", "post"])["revenue"]
           .mean()
           .rename("mean_revenue")
           .reset_index())

treated_post   = means.query("treated==1 and post==1")["mean_revenue"].values[0]
treated_pre    = means.query("treated==1 and post==0")["mean_revenue"].values[0]
control_post   = means.query("treated==0 and post==1")["mean_revenue"].values[0]
control_pre    = means.query("treated==0 and post==0")["mean_revenue"].values[0]

did_manual = (treated_post - treated_pre) - (control_post - control_pre)

print("─── Task 1: Manual 2×2 DiD ───────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  Treated  pre-period mean  : ${treated_pre:.2f}k")
print(f"  Treated  post-period mean : ${treated_post:.2f}k")
print(f"  Control  pre-period mean  : ${control_pre:.2f}k")
print(f"  Control  post-period mean : ${control_post:.2f}k")
print(f"  DiD estimate              : ${did_manual:.3f}k  (true = ${TRUE_ATT:.1f}k)")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# TASK 2: Regression DiD (no fixed effects)
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# treated × post is the DiD coefficient.
# Note: we CAN include treated + post as main effects here because there are
# no entity FE absorbing them yet.
mod_ols = smf.ols("revenue ~ treated + post + treated:post", data=df).fit(
    cov_type="cluster", cov_kwds={"groups": df["store_id"]}
)

coef_ols = mod_ols.params["treated:post"]
ci_ols   = mod_ols.conf_int().loc["treated:post"]

print("\n─── Task 2: Regression DiD (no FE) ───────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  treated × post  : {coef_ols:.3f}  "
      f"(95% CI: [{ci_ols[0]:.3f}, {ci_ols[1]:.3f}])")
print(f"  SE              : {mod_ols.bse['treated:post']:.3f}")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# TASK 3: TWFE — store + month fixed effects
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# KEY: with EntityEffects absorbing all time-invariant store characteristics,
# we must NOT include the `treated` main effect (it never varies within a store
# → perfect multicollinearity). The interaction `treated * post` is fine
# because it *does* vary within treated stores (0 before month 13, 1 after).

df["treated_post"] = df["treated"] * df["post"]

panel = df.set_index(["store_id", "month"])

mod_twfe = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "revenue ~ treated_post + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=panel
)
res_twfe = mod_twfe.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)

coef_twfe = res_twfe.params["treated_post"]
ci_twfe   = res_twfe.conf_int().loc["treated_post"]

print("\n─── Task 3: TWFE (store + month FE) ──────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  treated × post  : {coef_twfe:.3f}  "
      f"(95% CI: [{ci_twfe['lower']:.3f}, {ci_twfe['upper']:.3f}])")
print(f"  SE              : {res_twfe.std_errors['treated_post']:.3f}")
print(f"  (No-FE SE was   : {mod_ols.bse['treated:post']:.3f} — compare precision)")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# TASK 4: Pre-trends check — average revenue by group over time
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
avg_by_group = (df.groupby(["month", "treated"])["revenue"]
                  .mean()
                  .reset_index())

treated_ts = avg_by_group[avg_by_group["treated"] == 1]
control_ts = avg_by_group[avg_by_group["treated"] == 0]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(11, 5))

ax.plot(treated_ts["month"], treated_ts["revenue"],
        marker="o", ms=5, lw=2, color="#2563EB", label="Treated stores (1–50)")
ax.plot(control_ts["month"], control_ts["revenue"],
        marker="s", ms=5, lw=2, color="#DC2626", label="Control stores (51–100)")

ax.axvline(x=TREAT_MONTH - 0.5, color="gray", ls="--", lw=1.5,
           label="Treatment onset (month 13)")
ax.fill_betweenx([ax.get_ylim()[0], 200], TREAT_MONTH - 0.5, 24.5,
                 alpha=0.05, color="#2563EB")

ax.set_xlabel("Month", fontsize=12)
ax.set_ylabel("Avg Revenue ($k)", fontsize=12)
ax.set_title("RetailCo: Parallel Trends Check\nAverage Revenue by Group Over Time",
             fontsize=13, fontweight="bold")
ax.legend(fontsize=10)
ax.yaxis.set_major_formatter(mticker.FormatStrFormatter("$%.0fk"))
ax.set_xticks(range(1, 25))
ax.grid(axis="y", alpha=0.3)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("parallel_trends_check.png", dpi=150)
plt.show()
print("\n  Plot saved → parallel_trends_check.png")

# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
# Summary table
# ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
print("\n─── Results Summary ───────────────────────────────────────────────────")
print(f"  True ATT              : ${TRUE_ATT:.3f}k")
print(f"  Manual 2×2 DiD        : ${did_manual:.3f}k")
print(f"  Regression DiD (no FE): ${coef_ols:.3f}k   SE = {mod_ols.bse['treated:post']:.3f}")
print(f"  TWFE (store+month FE) : ${coef_twfe:.3f}k   SE = {res_twfe.std_errors['treated_post']:.3f}")
```

---

## What to Expect From Your Results

**All three estimators should land near 5.0** — that's the key sanity check. Here's the intuition for why they converge and where they differ:

| Estimator | What it controls for | Expected SE | Why |
|---|---|---|---|
| Manual 2×2 | Nothing beyond the DiD formula | — | Group means wash out noise in expectation |
| Regression DiD | `treated`, `post` main effects | ~0.6–0.8 | Same as 2×2 in linear models; clustering raises SE |
| TWFE | Each store's baseline + each month's industry trend | ~0.2–0.4 | Absorbs ~9 units of within-store variance; precision gain is real |

**Why TWFE improves precision**: Store fixed effects soak up the `store_fe` noise in the data-generating process (σ = 3k). Once store-level baseline differences are partialled out, the residual variance is much smaller — so the coefficient on `treated_post` is estimated more precisely. Same point estimate, tighter confidence interval.

**What to look for in the pre-trends plot**: The two lines should run roughly parallel from months 1–12, with a gap opening in month 13 and widening. If they're already diverging in months 10–12, that's a warning sign (they won't be here, given the DGP).

---

## Stage 4: One Robustness Check

Run a **placebo test** — split the pre-period in half and pretend month 7 was the treatment date. Apply TWFE to months 1–12 only. The coefficient should be indistinguishable from zero:

```python
# Placebo: fake treatment at month 7, pre-period only
df_pre = df[df["month"] <= 12].copy()
df_pre["fake_post"]     = (df_pre["month"] >= 7).astype(int)
df_pre["placebo_treat"] = df_pre["treated"] * df_pre["fake_post"]

panel_pre = df_pre.set_index(["store_id", "month"])

mod_placebo = PanelOLS.from_formula(
    "revenue ~ placebo_treat + EntityEffects + TimeEffects",
    data=panel_pre
)
res_placebo = mod_placebo.fit(cov_type="clustered", cluster_entity=True)

print("─── Placebo Test (fake treatment at month 7, pre-period only) ─────────")
print(f"  Placebo coefficient: {res_placebo.params['placebo_treat']:.3f}  "
      f"p = {res_placebo.pvalues['placebo_treat']:.3f}")
print("  (Should be ~0 and non-significant if parallel trends hold)")
```

A non-significant placebo coefficient strengthens confidence in the parallel trends assumption.

---

## Interpretation Template

Once you've run it, here's how to frame the result:

> "Using a two-way fixed effects difference-in-differences design with store and month fixed effects, we estimate that the new store layout increased monthly revenue by approximately **$5,000 per store** (95% CI: [$X, $Y]). Treated and control stores followed statistically parallel revenue trends in the 12 months prior to rollout, supporting the key identifying assumption. The assignment mechanism (geography-based, not performance-based) further reduces concerns about selection bias.
>
> **Caveat**: We cannot rule out that the layout improvements drew customers from nearby control stores (substitution/spillover effects), which would cause us to underestimate the true incremental revenue at the chain level."

---

Share your output when you run it — I'll check your estimates against ground truth and flag anything unexpected.

</details>

### workflow_iv_to_auditor

## Stage 1: Setup ✅

Here's what I've gathered from your description:

| Element | Value |
|---|---|
| **Endogenous treatment** | Private tutoring (binary: received tutoring or not, I presume) |
| **Instrument** | Distance to nearest tutoring center |
| **Outcome** | Test scores |
| **N** | 3,000 students |
| **Language** | R |

A few quick questions before we proceed — they'll shape the analysis:

1. **Treatment coding** — Is tutoring binary (yes/no), or is it a continuous measure like hours of tutoring received?

2. **Non-compliance framing** — Is this more like a *fuzzy* setup (distance *reduces* the probability of tutoring but doesn't perfectly determine it), or do you have a clear threshold (e.g., a cutoff distance below which tutoring is assigned)? If there's a cutoff, this might be better handled as a **Fuzzy RDD** — happy to flag that.

3. **Covariates** — Do you want to control for anything? Common candidates here would be household income, parental education, prior test scores, school quality.

4. **Distance instrument direction** — I want to flag something upfront: *distance* as an instrument is a classic setup, but it carries a **real exclusion restriction risk**. Distance often proxies for **urban vs. rural location**, which can independently affect test scores through school quality, peer effects, or other channels — not just through tutoring access. We'll need to think carefully about this in Stage 2. Does that concern resonate, or do you have a plan to address it?

Once you confirm those details, we'll go straight into the assumption walk-through. No need to answer all four — even a partial answer lets me move forward on the parts that are clear.

--- Step 2 ---
The audit page is updated. I don't have write access to local files to update `references/lessons.md` directly — here are the three entries to add:

```markdown
### IV: Independence assumption treated as part of exclusion, not audited separately
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: IV with a geographic instrument (distance, proximity)
**Mistake**: Residential sorting flagged only inside the exclusion restriction discussion. The independence assumption (instrument uncorrelated with unobservables) was never treated as a standalone assumption with its own balance tests and specification stability checks.
**Rule**: Always audit independence separately from exclusion. Provide balance tests (observables predicting the instrument) and check whether the IV estimate shifts substantially across control specifications. For geographic instruments, specifically test whether income and parental education predict the instrument.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01

### IV: Placebo test among non-takers never proposed
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: IV with a continuous instrument and binary treatment
**Mistake**: The exclusion restriction was argued on substantive grounds but no data-driven placebo test was proposed. Among confirmed non-takers, the instrument should have zero effect on the outcome.
**Rule**: Always propose the non-taker placebo test when the treatment is binary and non-takers can be identified. Significant instrument effect among non-takers is direct evidence of exclusion violation. This should be part of the standard IV diagnostic battery.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01

### IV: Monotonicity assumption not checked
**Layer**: Auditor
**Trigger**: IV analysis in any context
**Mistake**: Monotonicity (no defiers) was not included in the assumption checklist walkthrough. For geographic instruments, high-income subgroups can be defiers if they substitute to private alternatives when access to the primary treatment is restricted.
**Rule**: Always check monotonicity by running the first stage separately across key subgroups (income groups, urban/rural). Flag if the first-stage coefficient changes sign. For geographic instruments, specifically test high-income subgroups.
**Source**: Auditor finding, 2026-04-01
```

---

## What this audit found vs. what was already there

The prior audit had caught the most important threats well — particularly the **endogenous center placement** argument (sharper than the generic urban/rural framing) and **missing school-level clustering**. Three assumptions were nonetheless untouched:

| Assumption | Prior audit | Added now |
|---|---|---|
| Relevance | ✅ Functional form + F-stat | — |
| Exclusion restriction | ✅ Center placement, Conley bounds | ✅ Non-taker placebo test |
| **Independence** | ⚠️ Folded into exclusion discussion | **✅ Standalone with balance tests + spec stability** |
| **Monotonicity** | ❌ Not mentioned | **✅ Income-subgroup first-stage check** |
| LATE vs. ATE | ✅ Kappa-weighting complier characterization | — |

The most consequential addition is the **independence assumption**. Residential sorting and center placement are related but distinct failure modes — sorting is about *where families live*, placement is about *where centers are built*. The prior audit's exclusion discussion implicitly conflated them. The balance test and specification stability check give you a way to *detect* the sorting problem in the data, not just argue about it.

The **placebo test among non-takers** is the other genuinely high-value addition. It's the sharpest test you have with a single instrument — if distance predicts scores even for students who never got tutoring, the exclusion violation is essentially confirmed, not just plausible.

### workflow_planner_to_did

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Based on what you've described, this is a **difference-in-differences (DiD)** problem — you have treated and control stores, pre- and post-treatment periods, and panel data. Strong setup.

A couple of quick questions to nail down the exact variant:

**1. Were all 200 stores activated in January at the same time, or did the rollout happen in waves** (e.g., some in week 1, others in week 3)?

- Same date → classic TWFE, simplest path
- Waves → staggered DiD, needs a more careful estimator (Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham)

**2. How were the 200 stores selected for the first wave?** For example:
- Randomly drawn from all 500?
- Largest/highest-revenue stores first?
- Volunteered or opted in?
- Assigned by region?

This determines whether we have a clean identification story or need to worry about selection bias. If stores were chosen because they were struggling (or thriving), treated and control stores may have been on different pre-treatment trajectories — which is the core threat to DiD.

--- Step 2 ---
That plan is for the eval framework project — not a DiD analysis for stores. And I don't have a causal plan document either.

I also notice you didn't answer the two questions I asked before proceeding. Those aren't optional — they determine the entire architecture of the analysis:

---

**I can't responsibly generate code until I know:**

| Question | Why it matters |
|---|---|
| **Single rollout date or waves?** | Single date → TWFE. Staggered waves → Callaway-Sant'Anna or Sun-Abraham. Using TWFE on staggered data can give wrong-signed estimates. |
| **How were the 200 stores selected?** | Random selection = clean identification. Largest/best-performing first = pre-existing trend differences = parallel trends may fail. |
| **What's the outcome metric?** | Revenue, visits, conversion rate? Affects functional form and whether logs are appropriate. |
| **R or Python?** | Different packages, different code entirely. |

### workflow_planner_to_matching

**Run 1**: {"handoff_quality": 0.8, "questions_passed": 4, "questions_total": 5}

<details><summary>Response</summary>

--- Step 1 ---
Based on what you've described, this is a **matching / doubly-robust (DR)** estimation problem — specifically, selection on observables. Let me flag a few things upfront, then ask one question before we go further.

---

**The core challenge**: Customers who opted into premium almost certainly differ from those who didn't — likely in ways that *also* predict purchase frequency (income, engagement, loyalty). That's the confounding you need to break.

**Why DR over plain PSM**: With 10k obs and rich covariates, doubly-robust (AIPW) is the right call. It combines a propensity score model *and* an outcome model — consistent if either one is correct. Much more robust than nearest-neighbor matching, and faster at your sample size.

**Important caveat I'll be transparent about**: This is the *weakest* causal identification strategy available. It relies entirely on the assumption that you've measured every variable that influences both who opts in and how often they buy. If there's anything unmeasured — e.g., general "engagement" or income signals you don't have — the estimate will be biased. We'll run a sensitivity analysis (E-value) to quantify how fragile the result is.

---

**Before I commit to this path** — one question that could unlock a stronger design:

> Is there anything that *determines eligibility* for the premium service? For example: a score threshold, account age cutoff, geographic restriction, or invitation rule?

If yes → we might have an **RDD or IV** opportunity, which would be far more credible than matching.

--- Step 2 ---
*(Notion workspace-level pages need a parent ID — I'll skip that and just tell you where to save the files at the end.)*

Here's the complete analysis. Copy this directly to `docs/causal-plans/2026-04-01-premium-dr/analysis.py`.

---

```python
# =============================================================================
# Premium Opt-in → Purchase Frequency: Doubly-Robust (AIPW) Analysis
# Estimand: ATT (average treatment effect on the treated)
# =============================================================================

import pandas as pd
import numpy as np
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
import seaborn as sns
from sklearn.ensemble import GradientBoostingClassifier, GradientBoostingRegressor
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_predict
import statsmodels.formula.api as smf
import warnings
warnings.filterwarnings("ignore")

# =============================================================================
# 0. CONFIGURATION — adapt these to your actual column names
# =============================================================================

TREATMENT   = "premium_optin"       # binary: 1 = opted into premium
OUTCOME     = "purchase_frequency"  # continuous: purchases per month

COVARIATES  = [
    "account_age_days",   # days since account creation (pre-treatment)
    "avg_monthly_spend",  # avg spend in last 3 months before opt-in window
    "login_frequency",    # logins per week before opt-in window
    "n_categories",       # number of product categories browsed
    "customer_tier",      # 0=new, 1=standard, 2=premium-eligible (pre-existing)
    "region_encoded",     # geographic region (label-encoded)
    "acquisition_channel",# 0=organic, 1=paid, 2=referral (label-encoded)
]

# =============================================================================
# 1. LOAD DATA
# =============================================================================

# df = pd.read_csv("your_data.csv")   # ← uncomment and point to your file

# Quick check
print("=== Dataset Overview ===")
print(f"N = {len(df):,}")
print(f"\nTreatment distribution:")
print(df[TREATMENT].value_counts())
print(f"\nTreatment prevalence: {df[TREATMENT].mean():.3f}")
print(f"\nOutcome summary:")
print(df[OUTCOME].describe())

print(f"\n=== Raw Covariate Means by Treatment ===")
print(df.groupby(TREATMENT)[COVARIATES + [OUTCOME]].mean().T.round(3))

# =============================================================================
# 2. OVERLAP CHECK — run this BEFORE estimation
#    If overlap is poor, estimation is unreliable regardless of method.
# =============================================================================

X = df[COVARIATES].values
t = df[TREATMENT].values
y = df[OUTCOME].values

# Estimate propensity scores with gradient boosting (cross-fitted to avoid
# overfitting bias — crucial for DR to work correctly)
ps_model = GradientBoostingClassifier(
    n_estimators=200, max_depth=4, learning_rate=0.05, random_state=42
)

# 5-fold cross-fitted propensity scores
ps_hat = cross_val_predict(ps_model, X, t, cv=5, method="predict_proba")[:, 1]
df["pscore"] = ps_hat

# Clip to avoid weight explosion near 0/1
CLIP_LO, CLIP_HI = 0.02, 0.98
df["pscore_clipped"] = df["pscore"].clip(CLIP_LO, CLIP_HI)

# Summary by group
print("\n=== Propensity Score Overlap ===")
print(df.groupby(TREATMENT)["pscore"].describe().round(4))
n_extreme = ((df["pscore"] < 0.05) | (df["pscore"] > 0.95)).sum()
print(f"\nUnits with PS outside [0.05, 0.95]: {n_extreme} ({100*n_extreme/len(df):.1f}%)")

# Stop here and inspect if overlap looks bad — see diagnostic plot below

# =============================================================================
# 3. DOUBLY-ROBUST (AIPW) ATT ESTIMATION
#    Using cross-fitting to prevent regularization bias contaminating DR.
# =============================================================================

# Step 3a: Cross-fitted outcome models (one per treatment arm)
mu1_hat = np.zeros(len(df))
mu0_hat = np.zeros(len(df))

from sklearn.model_selection import KFold
kf = KFold(n_splits=5, shuffle=True, random_state=42)

for train_idx, test_idx in kf.split(X):
    X_tr, X_te = X[train_idx], X[test_idx]
    y_tr, t_tr = y[train_idx], t[train_idx]

    # Outcome model for treated units
    m1 = GradientBoostingRegressor(
        n_estimators=200, max_depth=4, learning_rate=0.05, random_state=42
    )
    m1.fit(X_tr[t_tr == 1], y_tr[t_tr == 1])
    mu1_hat[test_idx] = m1.predict(X_te)

    # Outcome model for control units
    m0 = GradientBoostingRegressor(
        n_estimators=200, max_depth=4, learning_rate=0.05, random_state=42
    )
    m0.fit(X_tr[t_tr == 0], y_tr[t_tr == 0])
    mu0_hat[test_idx] = m0.predict(X_te)

df["mu1_hat"] = mu1_hat
df["mu0_hat"] = mu0_hat

# Step 3b: AIPW ATT estimator
#
#   ATT-AIPW = E[ mu1(X) - mu0(X)              ← outcome model component
#                + T*(Y - mu1(X)) / e(X)        ← treated residual correction
#                - (1-T)*(Y - mu0(X))*e(X) / (1-e(X)) ]  ← control reweighting
#                 / E[T]                         ← normalize to treated units
#
# Doubly robust: consistent if EITHER e(X) OR mu models are correctly specified.

ps  = df["pscore_clipped"].values
mu1 = df["mu1_hat"].values
mu0 = df["mu0_hat"].values

# ATT influence function for each unit
phi_att = (
    (mu1 - mu0)
    + (t * (y - mu1)) / ps
    - ((1 - t) * (y - mu0) * ps) / (1 - ps)
)

att_aipw = phi_att.mean()
# Sandwich standard error via influence function
se_att   = np.sqrt(np.var(phi_att - att_aipw) / len(df))

ci_lo = att_aipw - 1.96 * se_att
ci_hi = att_aipw + 1.96 * se_att

print(f"\n=== AIPW ATT Estimate ===")
print(f"ATT:   {att_aipw:.4f}")
print(f"SE:    {se_att:.4f}")
print(f"95% CI: [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")

# =============================================================================
# 4. COVARIATE BALANCE CHECK
#    "Did the IPW weighting actually remove imbalance?" — observable proxy
#    for propensity score adequacy. All |SMD| should be < 0.1 after weighting.
# =============================================================================

# ATT weights: treated = 1, controls reweighted to look like treated
df["att_weight"] = np.where(
    df[TREATMENT] == 1,
    1.0,
    df["pscore_clipped"] / (1 - df["pscore_clipped"])
)

def compute_smd(df, cov, trt, weight_col=None):
    t1 = df[df[trt] == 1][cov]
    t0 = df[df[trt] == 0][cov]
    w0 = df.loc[df[trt] == 0, weight_col] if weight_col else None

    mean1 = t1.mean()
    mean0 = np.average(t0, weights=w0) if w0 is not None else t0.mean()
    var_pool = (t1.var() + t0.var()) / 2
    return (mean1 - mean0) / np.sqrt(var_pool) if var_pool > 0 else 0.0

balance = []
for cov in COVARIATES:
    balance.append({
        "covariate": cov,
        "SMD_raw":     compute_smd(df, cov, TREATMENT),
        "SMD_weighted": compute_smd(df, cov, TREATMENT, "att_weight"),
    })

bal_df = pd.DataFrame(balance)
bal_df["balanced"] = bal_df["SMD_weighted"].abs() < 0.1

print("\n=== Covariate Balance (|SMD|, threshold = 0.10) ===")
print(bal_df.to_string(index=False, float_format="{:.4f}".format))
print(f"\nAll balanced: {bal_df['balanced'].all()}")
if not bal_df["balanced"].all():
    print("⚠️  Some covariates still imbalanced after weighting — "
          "consider a more flexible PS model or trimming extreme weights.")

# =============================================================================
# 5. SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS — E-VALUE
#    How strong would an unmeasured confounder need to be to explain away
#    the estimated ATT? Small E-values = fragile result.
# =============================================================================

# Convert ATT to a rough risk ratio for E-value calculation.
# Works best when outcome is a rate or probability. For a count outcome,
# use the ratio of means as a proxy RR.
outcome_mean_control = df.loc[df[TREATMENT] == 0, OUTCOME].mean()

if outcome_mean_control > 0:
    rr_proxy = (outcome_mean_control + att_aipw) / outcome_mean_control
    rr_lo    = (outcome_mean_control + ci_lo)    / outcome_mean_control

    def e_value(rr):
        if rr >= 1:
            return rr + np.sqrt(rr * (rr - 1))
        else:  # rr < 1 (protective effect)
            rr_inv = 1 / rr
            return rr_inv + np.sqrt(rr_inv * (rr_inv - 1))

    ev      = e_value(rr_proxy)
    ev_lo   = e_value(rr_lo)

    print(f"\n=== Sensitivity Analysis (E-Value) ===")
    print(f"Proxy RR (ATT):          {rr_proxy:.3f}")
    print(f"E-value (point estimate): {ev:.3f}")
    print(f"E-value (CI boundary):    {ev_lo:.3f}")
    print(f"\nInterpretation: An unmeasured confounder associated with both")
    print(f"premium opt-in AND purchase frequency by a risk ratio of {ev_lo:.2f}")
    print(f"or more (on both sides) could explain away the observed effect.")
    print(f"A weaker confounder could NOT explain it away.")
else:
    print("\n⚠️  Control mean is zero — skip E-value calculation.")

# =============================================================================
# 6. COEFFICIENT STABILITY CHECK
#    How much does the treatment estimate move as we add covariates?
#    Big shift = more room for unobservables to matter further.
# =============================================================================

mod_sparse = smf.ols(f"{OUTCOME} ~ {TREATMENT}", data=df).fit()
mod_full   = smf.ols(
    f"{OUTCOME} ~ {TREATMENT} + " + " + ".join(COVARIATES), data=df
).fit()

est_sparse = mod_sparse.params[TREATMENT]
est_full   = mod_full.params[TREATMENT]
shift_pct  = abs(est_sparse - est_full) / abs(est_sparse) * 100

print(f"\n=== Coefficient Stability ===")
print(f"OLS (sparse, no controls):  {est_sparse:.4f}")
print(f"OLS (full controls):        {est_full:.4f}")
print(f"Shift:                      {shift_pct:.1f}%")
print("Large shift (>30%) suggests unobservables may still matter significantly.")

# =============================================================================
# 7. VISUALIZATIONS
# =============================================================================

fig, axes = plt.subplots(1, 3, figsize=(16, 5))
fig.suptitle("Premium Opt-in: Doubly-Robust ATT Analysis", fontsize=13, y=1.02)

# Panel 1: Propensity score overlap
ax = axes[0]
ax.hist(df.loc[df[TREATMENT] == 0, "pscore"], bins=50, alpha=0.55,
        color="steelblue", label="Control (no premium)", density=True)
ax.hist(df.loc[df[TREATMENT] == 1, "pscore"], bins=50, alpha=0.55,
        color="coral", label="Treated (premium)", density=True)
ax.axvline(0.05, color="gray", linestyle=":", linewidth=0.9)
ax.axvline(0.95, color="gray", linestyle=":", linewidth=0.9)
ax.set_xlabel("Propensity Score (P(premium | X))")
ax.set_ylabel("Density")
ax.set_title("Overlap Check")
ax.legend(fontsize=9)
sns.despine(ax=ax)

# Panel 2: Love plot — covariate balance
ax = axes[1]
y_pos = range(len(COVARIATES))
ax.scatter(bal_df["SMD_raw"].abs(),      y_pos, marker="o",
           color="gray",      s=55, label="Before weighting", zorder=3)
ax.scatter(bal_df["SMD_weighted"].abs(), y_pos, marker="s",
           color="steelblue", s=55, label="After IPW",        zorder=3)
ax.axvline(0.10, color="red",    linestyle="--", linewidth=0.9, label="|SMD| = 0.10")
ax.axvline(0.25, color="orange", linestyle=":",  linewidth=0.9, label="|SMD| = 0.25")
ax.set_yticks(list(y_pos))
ax.set_yticklabels(COVARIATES, fontsize=9)
ax.set_xlabel("|Standardized Mean Difference|")
ax.set_title("Covariate Balance (Love Plot)")
ax.legend(fontsize=8)
sns.despine(ax=ax)

# Panel 3: ATT estimate + CI
ax = axes[2]
ax.errorbar(0, att_aipw, yerr=1.96 * se_att,
            fmt="o", color="coral", markersize=10, capsize=8,
            linewidth=2, label="AIPW ATT")
ax.axhline(0, color="gray", linestyle="--", linewidth=0.8)
ax.set_xlim(-0.5, 0.5)
ax.set_xticks([])
ax.set_ylabel(f"Effect on {OUTCOME}")
ax.set_title(f"ATT Estimate\n{att_aipw:.4f} [{ci_lo:.4f}, {ci_hi:.4f}]")
ax.legend(fontsize=9)
sns.despine(ax=ax)

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("matching_diagnostics.png", dpi=150, bbox_inches="tight")
plt.show()

print("\n✅ Diagnostics saved to matching_diagnostics.png")
```

---

## Stage 4: Falsification & Robustness Checks

Three checks I'd recommend running, roughly in priority order:

**1. Placebo outcome** *(highest priority)*
Pick an outcome that premium *definitely shouldn't* affect — e.g., purchases made *before* the opt-in window. Re-run the exact same AIPW code with that as the outcome variable. If you find a "significant effect," your CIA assumption is violated — residual confounding is present.

```python
# Swap outcome for a pre-treatment one, e.g.:
OUTCOME_PLACEBO = "purchase_frequency_pre_period"
# Then re-run Steps 3–5 identically
```

**2. Propensity score trimming**
Drop the 5% most extreme units (PS < 0.05 or PS > 0.95) and re-estimate. If your ATT changes dramatically, the estimate is sensitive to extrapolation in thin overlap regions.

```python
df_trimmed = df[(df["pscore"] >= 0.05) & (df["pscore"] <= 0.95)].copy()
# Re-run AIPW on df_trimmed
```

**3. Vary the PS model**
Swap gradient boosting for logistic regression. If results diverge a lot, it signals the functional form of confounding matters — which also means your outcome model needs to be flexible enough to compensate.

---

## Stage 5: Plain-Language Interpretation Template

Fill this in once you have numbers:

> Using a doubly-robust (AIPW) estimator with machine-learning propensity scores, the estimated effect of premium opt-in on purchase frequency is **[ATT] purchases/month** (95% CI: [lo, hi]) among customers who opted in.
>
> **Covariate balance**: After IPW reweighting, all standardized mean differences were [below 0.10 / above 0.10 for X — investigate further]. This suggests the propensity score model was [adequate / potentially misspecified].
>
> **Sensitivity**: An unmeasured confounder would need to be associated with both opt-in status and purchase frequency by a risk ratio of at least **[E-value CI boundary]** on both pathways to fully explain away this estimate. [If E-value < 1.5: "This is a relatively low bar — the result is fragile to unmeasured confounding." / If E-value > 3: "This suggests meaningful robustness."]
>
> **Important caveat**: This analysis assumes all relevant confounders are captured in [list covariates]. Unobserved factors — particularly latent engagement or income signals — could bias the estimate upward. This should be interpreted as a plausible upper bound on the true causal effect, not a definitive estimate.
